THE RED PAGE.
STAR-TRYST.
When the sun is down and the stars ashine,
(Chough you in the city may drink or danoe,
I think it better than music or wine
To bow to the stars and dream romance.
You may play or sing or go where you please,
Or light love’s lamp in a girl’s blue eyes ;
But I am in love with a life of ease—
A pipe in the dark and stars in the skies.
Your wine is a serpent with glittering eye ;
It strikes—ah, how vacant the world and forlorn !
Your kisses are roses that wound by-and-by—
The reddest, perchance, with the bitterest thorn.
But I in the arms of Enchantment repose,
My cares put aside, and in lyrical vein
Enamel the world with the sheen of a rose
Till it blooms as in Arcady, laughing again.
Thus, late in the night, and ere Slumber has kissed
My eyes with drugged lips, I go out all alone
To keep in the silence a beautiful tryst
With eyes by the legion that startle my own.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
George Bernard Barton.
EORGE BERNARD BARTON (a
8 w" brother of the Federal
who died at Goulburn last week,
may be fairly regarded as the first purely
literary man whom New South Wales produced.
He meant to be a barrister, passed his terms
at the Inner Temple, was called to the Eng-
lish Bar, and admitted in Sydney. But on his re-
turn to Australia, in the early sixties, an aural
infirmity from which he had long suffered became
so pronounced that Barton had to abandon all
thought of the Bar as a profession. Even at this
time he seemed to be more interested in literature
than in law, and the writer can quite remember,
as a boy, how Barton’s best and most vivacious
talk was about books and book folk. He had re-
turned from Europe a fair German, and more than
fair French scholar. Subsequently his knowledge
of French literature became wide and various.
Presently George Barton was himself making
guerrilla-like forays in the field of literature, or
at least journalism—skirmishing in the columns of
the Sydney Morning Herald with satirical skits in
verse on current topics of popular interest. He
may have done a little in prose before this, but
these “ verses of occasion ” are his first published
work I can recall. They appeared over the pen-
name “Snowy River,” ;md attracted a good deal
of attention. The little pasquinades, indeed, dis-
covered-plenty of birr and fun, and were always
technically tradesmanlike in respect of rhythm and
rhyme. One of them is still remembered by
George Barton’s contemporaries ; a particularly
spirited jingle, dealing with the discomfiture of
Sir Frederick Pottinger and his aides by highway-
man Frank Gardiner and his rascals.
1 By a flash of the pan we missed the man,
So we ’ll take the boy instead.
in the sixties Sydney Punch was started
with George Barton as fits first editor, and poor
Greig as its chief artist. * A man of signal capacity
as a caricaturist, Greig met his death by a
disastrous harbour accident, and Punch was never
able wholly to replace him. Barton did some of
his brightest humorous work, both in prose and
verse, for Punch. He, of course, followed closely
the English models, as did all our local
press humorists of that day. Not that Barton’s
contributions were imitative of any of the old-
world jesters in particular ; but that his point of
view, his approach of his subject, and his literary
methods generally were those of the English
masters. His theme only was local, the handling
was European. This almost of necessity. In a
virgin land new themes may be galore, but it
takes a little time for the coming of the local
literary note —the environing atmosphere, argot.
In Australia it has taken pretty well up to the
last decade.
That George Barton was a young man of
capacity and promise had become fairly widely
known; and when it seemed quite certain that
his hopes of a legal career had vanished, those who
had the power proffered him the solatium of the
Commissionership of Stamps. This was a com-
fortable enough providing for a still quite young
man.
But Barton did not enjoy his new office for
long. Though he had of course withdrawn from
the actual editorship of Punch, he still continued
to write for the journal. There was no harm in
that; but the particular shape which one of his
contributions took was not a little unfortunate.
Barton wrote a classical burlesque entitled
“Jupiter and Ganymede,” in which he made fun
of the reigning Ministry, and particularly of its
leader, Mr. Charles Cowper, with such telling
effect that the Government took the matter up.
They demanded the name of the writer of the
satire from the proprietors of Punch; it was given ;
and George Barton was retired from the Commis-
sionership of Stamps.
That Barton was unhappily inspired in lam-
pooning the men who had conferred a snug post
upon him need hardly be argued about; that his
punishment was out of all proportion to his crime
is equally patent. Anyone now reading “Jupiter
and Ganymede,” in the light of the trenchant
fashion in which the satire of public men and their
acts is now done, will admit at once that Barton
laughed at the politicians in a very good-humoured
and straightforward way, and that there is no
trace of personal maliciousness in the lampoon.
In a word, were any Government officer to-day
found out in burlesquing the heads of depart-
ments—on the, I hope, improbable supposition
that any present-day Newspaper would be so
cowardly as to betray its contributor—and the
deed were visited by the immediate and condign
punishment meted out to Barton, such action on
the part of any Government would not be allowed
to pass without the severest criticism from both
press and public. All that happened in Barton’s
case was that a very able letter which he wrote
showing cause why he should not be dismissed
was, at the instance of Mr. William Forster, laid
on the table of the House.
It is probable that this untoward episode in
Barton’s early career projected its shadow across
his whole life ; andfihat he never quite forgot the
taste of bitterness.''
In those days we had the Empire with us, an
ably-conducted journal in many ways, but one that
was always strenuously breasting the waves of
financial difficulty, and never quite mounted
them. George Barton had written a good deal
for the Empire, and it was his connection with the
paper which prompted him to suggest to Samuel
Bennett the experiment of a Sydney evening
paper. The idea struck Bennett as a feasible one,
and the result was the establishment of the
Evening News. The new venture caught on from
the jump,"and in a short space did for the
proprietors what the morning paper had never ,
been able to effect— secured a solid footing in
Australian journalism. To the Evening News
Lreorge Barton contributed a column of very
bright and pithy comment on current topics, over
r ® a °k ri quet Ehe Elaneur in Sydney. Some
ol Barton s most brilliant journalistic work was
done at the sign of the Flaneur. The column was
subsequently continued, many will remember, by
Trank Hutchinson, who proved a very capable
successor to Barton.
Between the years tflfw and 1868 George Barton
was Header in English Literature, as the office
was then styled, at the Sydney University. Eor
this lectureship he was remarkably well fitted, his
of English -literature being wide and
catholic ; while he was at the same time an easy
and polished speaker. He also lectured occa-
sionally in public on literary subjects.
The next important phase of George Barton’s
literary life was his acceptance of the editorship of
the Otago Daily Times , which was even at that
time a thriving and influential journal. He
wrought vigorously fbr the Times, and his editor-
ship was not without storm and stress. At that
time, 'Julius Vogel was a paramount force in
Maoriland political life. Barton opposed his
policy at many points, and the conflict between
the two was ultimately d outrance.
During Barton’s conduct of the Otago Daily
Times an incident occurred which is probably un-
precedented in Australian political history, or,
indeed, in that of any country under British rule.
A letter appeared in the paper reflecting on a
member of the existing M.L, Ministry. The
Government instituted an action for libel. Pend-
ing its hearing, the Governor of the colony went
on tour. The action came on. A number of the
employees of the journal declined to give evidence,
on the ground that their answers might incriminate
them, and it was generally thought that the pro-
ceedings would fail. The Government was equal
to the occasion, for a number of pardons
were produced and handed to the unwilling wit-
nesses. It came out that the Governor, before
starting on his tour, had actually left behind him
a number of signed blank pardons for his Ministers
to fill in. There was a very considerable public
excitement over this virtual demission, this practi-
cal surrender, to a party, of the purely personal
prerogative of the Crown. The case is, I imagine,
a record one, and of so unprecedentedly scandalous
a character that it is perhaps worth recalling and
chronicling here. As far as the Otago Daily
Times was concerned, the. action, if I remember
rightly, ended in a fine.
After a successful and notable editorship of the
Times for a year or two, Barton severed his con-
nection with the journal. The friction between
Vogel and himself had become uncomfortably
acute, and the proprietors of the paper were,
probably, hardly prepared to wholly endorse the
editor’s uncompromising oppposition to the local
dictator. Witn the intellectual facility charac-
teristic of the man, George Barton now reverted
to his first calling of law, and began practice as a
barrister and solicitor, the two professions being
then (as now) in Maoriland amalgamated.
Despite his physical disability he prospered, his
practice being largely a consulting one. In a few
years, however, journalism again tempted him,
and a promising opening presenting itself in
Melbourne, he sought that city and joined the
staff of the Age.
The early eighties found George Barton once
more back in Sydney, and again writing for the
Evening News. In 1886 he began to do leader and
oilier work for the Herald, and a little later was
commissioned to write the history of' New South
Wales. No writer in the province was better
equipped for this task than George Barton, as the
single volume (which was all that he completed)
abundantly proved. Since then Barton has been
occupied in miscellaneous journalism. He wrote
for the Evening News a series of notes on the
Federal Convention held in Sydney, which
were so well thought of that the Govern-
ment gave instructions that they should be
printed. For the same journal he compiled a
“ full and particular ” account of Margaret
Catchpole, which certainly deserves mention.
A body of romantic myth and legend had grown
up round and about the life of this notable
woman. Barton set himself to winnow the grain
of fact from the chaff of glamour, putting himself
to great pains to ascertain the actual truths of the
story. The result proved to be a very interesting
and authoritative narrative.
George Barton’s first and chief claim in the
literary history of New South Wales is probably
the two volumes, “ Literature in New South
Wales,” and “The Poets and Prose Writers of
New South Wales,” written at the suggestion of
the Government for the Paris Exhibition of 1887.
All future historians of Australian literature must
necessarily be more or less indebted to these two
works. The first was the outcome of much intel-
ligent labour and research, while the second is an
exceedingly interesting work, and even at this
day makes vastly good reading. In it George
Barton is about at his best as a critic. We have
had few, if any, literary critics in Australia more
sympathetic and, at the same time, more judicial
than Barton ; and none, with the exception, per-
haps, of It. H. Horne, who have brought a wider
knowledge to bear upon their subject. Deniehy
as a critic had more enthusiasm, mqre imagina-
tion, and a more brilliant literary “brio,” but he
was less discriminating, had less penetration, and
less, certitude of touch—was, in brief, less truly a
critic.
The attitude which Barton adopts in “The Poets
and Prose Writers of New South Wales” is
exactly the just and fair one in the circumstances.
He w“as dealing with a literature in its abso-
lute infancy, with the first tentative lispings of a
national literary speech ; and, therefore, he was
entitled to write in a tone of encouragement, and
to praise wherever he got the shadow of an open-
ing for praise. This he does, but at th&same time
he never surrenders his critical office, and wherever
praise is bestowed he easily succeeds in convincing
us that praise is there due.
As a writer, in the regard of style, George
Barton has been among the very best we have
had. He possesses that first-rate sort of style that
never makes one pause and remark how good and
clever it is. Its “distinction” is the distinction
of just being good. You feel that his writing is
good as you read along ; it never occurs to you to
ask why; and if the writer took any particular
pains to make his writing as it is he has success-
fully concealed them. Clean-cut, nervous, flexible
simple English English—that is what Barton
writes. A scholarly style withal, yet one as free
from academic rigidity as it is free from deca-
dent preciosity and every other end-of-the-cen-
tury affectation. And this sort of style is, for
nine out of ten given literary uses, about as good
as you can desire.
Though George Barton’s intellectual temper was
that of the man of letters before aught else, he
never showed signs of oapacity for being
anything beyond a first-rate journalist and maga-
zine writer. He reyealed no imaginative
gift. His faculty is essentially the critical one.
He has been fairly various, but always within the
limitations of magazine and newspaper work.
He once wrote a burlesque entitled “ Oberon,” in
which, old Sydney playgoers may remember, the
vivacious and engaging Lady Don played the
title-rdle. It was a very smart extravaganza in
the manner of Planche, Byron and Burnand when
those playrights were at their best (circa the era
of Ixion and the “Field of the Cloth of Gold”),
and, perhaps, had he had the opportunity, he might
have written light comedy. I never heard that
he ever attempted novel-writing or even short
stories.
George Barton’s social gifts were always emin-
ent and agreeable. He possessed a manner of
singular suavity and attraction. A half hour’s
tete-a-tete with him was at once a social and intel-
lectual pleasure of a rare and delightful kind.
While still a young man he was an admirable
talker, and he developed into an unrivalled table
comrade. His talk was easy, various, full, polished
and witty. You could not take refuge from the
rain under a gable-end with Edmund Burke with-
out being impressed by his mental gifts. I was
never placed in these circumstances with George
Barton, but I may be permitted to say that you
could not meet him by chance on a trip to Manly
without hearing something from him worth the
hearing—some interesting fact in literary history
that you did not know before, some happy and
apposite scrap of literary criticism and the like.
A facile and copious talker, George Barton was
also a good listener, and though fond of argument
and the breathing cut-and-thrust of conversational
sword-play, was almost always open-minded and
courteous to hjs opponent’s contention. He had
a particularly soft and pleasant voice, and in the
most animated junctures of talk conversed with a
gentleness and polish that were apt to disarm the
unwary. For this satin smoothness of touch not
seldom hides a mailed hand, and at the proper
moment George could flash out the swift and con-
clusive retort that left him, for the moment at any
rate, master of the argumentative position.
But as a literary critic Barton usually trended
to leniency. This he has shown again and again
in his judgments on our native-born literature. I
remember his telling me, shortly after the publi-
cation of “ Poets and Prose Writers,” how Kendall
came to him to express his surprised pleasure at
the generous and sympathetic treatment he had
received in that volume. Sydney Punch, during
Barton’s editorship, had on several occasions
laughed at and burlesqued Kendall’s muse, and
the poet had looked for nothing but that he should
be severely handled by Barton in his office of
authoritatively-constituted critic-in-chief of Aus-
tralian literature. It may be added that Barton,
on this occasion, was squally pleased that the poet
should be so unmfi„akeably gratified.
It cannot be said that George Barton’s worldly
success was commensurate with his talents.
Whether it has been in proportion to his industry,
or his moral endowments, or his theological views,
or any other ethical or mental part of the man, I
do not propose to inquire. No very illuminating
solutions have ever been arrived at to puzzles of
that sort—often as the task has been attempted.
Barton certainly had as many chances in life as
most men, and it looks (to the casual eye, at any
rate) as if he had hardly made the most of them.
The tide was at its flood with him more than once,
but each time he seemed to let it ebb again with-
out profit. More than once the gods pressed
a brimming cup of prosperity to his lips, but he
had scarce touched its rim when the flashing
chalice slipped somehow through his fingers.
Which was most at fault, the uncertain hand or
the slippery, fragile vessel, or the careless gods
themselves, who dower us with the right iiand
and rob us with the left—none, I say, may even
approximately determine.
George Barton was never much of a Bohemian.
He “ranged himself” early in life, married a
pretty and charming woman, and was always a by-
no-means sparing cultivator of the hearthside
deities.
George - Bernard Barton has done good and
valuable work for Australian literature, as a
historian, and as a critic ; work that can never be
ignored, and that should not be forgotten. His
place in the early literary annals of New South
Wales is a secure and honourable one. His two
published volumes alone, not to speak of his
numerous miscellaneous writings in the same
direction, are of historic value, and such as all
Australian literary students cannot choose but be
grateful for.
ROBERT RICHARDSON.
Writes “J.R.”
You say (10/8/’01) “ Cuss right, J.R. wrong.” How do
you know ? I gave you a clear and full statement of my
reasons for the view I took. It is a question of opinion
as to the meaning of certain words which are open to two
opposite constructions. lam no more infallible than you
are, and you no more than I. You say : “ J.R. spoke so
authoritatively that we trusted him—and fell in. . . .
Verify your contributor’s quotations. ” That means that I
misled you by a wilful misquotation, and am a liar.
As a matter of fact, my quotation was correct, and if I
was wrong at all it was in misunderstanding the context.
Then you give Ancient Pistol’s advice to his wife. You
should have finished your quotation—
“ Let us to France ; like horse-leeches, my boys,
To suck, to suck, the very blood to suck.”
This of Hastings applies—
“ More pity that the eagle should be mew’d,
While kites and buzzards prey at liberty.”
And Gloster—
“ Wrens make prey where eagles dare not perch.”
That’s enough.—J.R.
J.B, writes—
Unless the Royal Academy and National Gallery, the
Salon and Luxembourg, have altered considerably within
the last few years, very few picture-lovers (in contradis-
tinction to artists) will agree with Mr. Fischer when he
says, “I thought English work a long way ahead of
French at present.”
To the technically untrained (but not necessarily un-
appreciative or uncritical) eye the distinctions of French
Art are its Audacity and Character ; its charms are vivid
(? “ tawdry ”) colouring and emotion portrayed by a
dramatic instinct essentially French. No theme is too
sacred—no realisation too horrible—no creation of the
brain too impossible—for the French artist. Neither is
he wanting in pathos of the homely kind, humour of the
subtler sort. Before these things, the robed aristocracy,
and sweet-faced, well-conducted maidens, and angelic
children, and everlasting landscapes—the half-tones and
pale pinks and blues—the ordinary ideas in pleasant garb
of the Royal Academy, pale and are tame.
Enter a room of the Salon : your mouth waters, you are
attracted almost by force to three or four different points
at once. The picture of Cain—though Doomed is written
upon every line of its canvas, and the God of Calvin sits
behind the picture-frame—is but one powerful picture
among many. For majesty and pose, go to Signol’s
“Woman Taken in Adultery”; for classical beautv, to
Moreau’s “ Orpheus” ; for portraiture, to Bonnat's “Por-
trait of M. L. Cogniet ” ; for painful and sordid realism, to
Tassaert’s “ Unhappy Family ” ; for supernatural horror,
to Azrael, in Delaunay’s “Plague in Rome”; for war
horror, to Beaumont’s “ The Captain’s Booty ” ; for physi-
cal horror, to Sylvestre’s “ Locusta Before Nero demon-
strating the Effect of the Poison Prepared for Britan-
nicus”; for horror wholesale, Bida’s “ Massacre of the
Mamelukes” ; for history, Meissonier’s “ Napoleon 111 at
Solf erino ”; for picturesque quaintness, Breton’s 1 ‘ Blessing
the Crops ” ; for atmosphere and novelty, Guillaumet’s
“ Evening Prayer in the Sahara”; for intensity, Lecomte du
Nouy’s “ Bearers of Bad Tidings”; for conflicting emotion,
Laurens’ “ Excommunication of Robert the Pious” ; for
delicacy in treatment of the nude, Collin’s “Floidal”;
for boyhood (the boyhood of the people), Mile. Bashkirt-
seff’s “Meeting”; for truth and infinity of pathos,
Tassaert’s “ Portrait of a Dead Woman ” ; and for the
“ everydayness of this workday world,” Robert-Fleury’s
“ Aged Women of the Place Navone,” and E. le Roux’s
“New-born Infant.”
The landscapes of the Academy are, foft the most part,
enjoyable. Yet, when you have looked intently at a
dozen or so, you feel as though you had been in the train
a long time and would like to get out. In the Academy
the British matron of the upper middle class is much in
evidence, with the sort of eye of faith, hope for the best,
expression she wears in church ; also papa, who has dined
well, and their girls, who whisper, “ How nice that is ! ”
In the National Gallery no person is in evidehce, and any-
one who should try to fathom the numerous landscapes
purporting to result from the efforts of an individual
named Turner is liable to overbalance his or her brain.
The Salon and the Luxembourg are always well-filled—
the workmen in their blue blouses gesticulate and argue
Boftly. I do not believe the British workman ever
darkens the door of the Academy. He prefers to leave
his shilling at thff Alhambra or the Zoo.
While Mr. Fischer feels himself (no doubt with good
reason) uplifted into a higher realm of art by “ the tone
on the plums” and the “atmosphere round the girl’s
arm ” (La Thangue, who was responsible for the tone and
atmosphere, ought to have been French, anyhow !) the
mere picture-lover will obstinately hanker after vital grip
and feeling, and—variety.
Unmounted photos., perfectly finished, of all the best
Salon and Luxembourg paintings can be bought in Paris
for 4d. each. In the town of Canterbury you pay 2s. for
unmounted photo, (same size) of the cloisters of Canter-
bury Cathedral. Truly Art
“ Is of the English life a thing apart.”
You leave the Academy tired with the stodgy tiredness
of a wet day shut up in a town house. You leave the
Salon tired with the tiredness of the occasional play-
goer, who has laughed, cried, and sat breathless, and
eaten French lollies between the acts.
France has lost much—may she keep her Art !
G. B. Barton, who died last week from initw-
enza in his middle sixties, was a man of letters
whose talent has been equalled but rarely in this
country. His volume of The History of New
South Wales is the best of the series ; and his book
on The Poets and Prose- Writers of New South Wales
was the first worthy book of Australian literary
criticism : indeed, after some score of years have
passed, it is our only book of literary criticism that
counts. The writer of the friendly sketch of
Barton’s life in another column decides that he
sacrificed what is commonly called “success in life.”
His treasured independence was well worth the
sacrifice. Barton truckled to none. He hated
shams, he despised dullards; and he resolutely
expressed himself at all hazards, in his writings
and in his life. His love of truth and justice was
almost savage in its sincerity. An Australian, he
loved his country ; an intellectual cosmopolitan,
he loathed the shameful conduct of Britain to the
Boers. Naturally he was a person distasteful to
time-servers and sycophants. But he died happily
in harness, with his self-respect intact, and he
carried to his grave the esteem of the few who
could appreciate his character—an honour out-
weighing all the applause of the mean-minded
mob.
Bush commentary—
The teacher culled by the Bathurst Free Press (Aug.
10) seems to me a very fictitious person. Fancy a real
live teacher letting the children’s hats lie in “ a heap ” on
the floor for want of two-pennyworth of 2-inch nails !
Teachers may be curious—surely none of them is posi-
tively dirty? Nature never intended the consumptive
youth with a precarious appetite for a teacher. And,.
“ however gentlemanly he may be,” what notice does he
expect the Dept, to take of the fact ? Govt, has taken a
contract to provide facilities (apparently of a very lacka-
daisical order) for plain education to children on “ a
marshy island in the Manning River,” and m other locali
ties where persons who are induced to “go on the land ”
live and move and have their being. Should the Dept,
institute a supplemental exam, in deportment,
and send those who come out on top to places
where they are likely \to receive sacrificial offer-
ings of food and bedding ? The Dept. know T s that
the “ however gentlemanly he' may be ” qualification is
often connected with the tailor and barber tribe, and
with a surface soil of manner which may or may not be
an indication of productive strata beneath. Also, the
gentlemanly.teacher is strangely inconsistent in his desire
for personal comfort. He forgets to make the children
bring in dry bark and sticks every evening, and has to
“ grope ” for his socks under a pile of old coats. An
increase of salary would do him no harm ; it would soothe
his mind, and would, at any rate, enable him to hire a
small boy to look after his socks and the dry firing.
The poor, dull, bootless girl is no worse, and in some
respects much better, than the cunning, pert, “difficult”
street? child, often taught in Sydney schools.— Johs
Carew.

The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and verses of sailor-men s
and wharf-men’s life whichJS. J. Brady has contributed
to The Bulletin and the Sunday Times, Sydney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Tack Bowlin Steered 1 Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing; and the price is ss.j
post-free.

BRIGHT’S DISEASE
For the full understanding of the curative action
of Warner’s Safe Cure, it is necessary to bear in
mind the processes of nature by which human life
is maintained. Reduced to their essential prin-
ciples, these processes are simply the wasting and
renewal of the tissues of the body.
It is absolutely necessary to life that the body
should waste away, and that its substance should
be renewed by food, water, and fresh air. Night
and day this process of waste and replaceman goes
on unceasingly. Every time a man draws breath
his body gains in weight by the oxygen, etc., taken
from the air, and every time he breathes out it
loses weight by the carbonio acid and moisture
given forth.
Insensibly, too, the body is losing weight all the
time. You can prove this by placing your hand
on a cold piece of polished glass, when you will
see that moisture given forth by the skin has con-
densed. Were the body to be entirely covered
with a coat of waterproof varnish, death would
quickly result.
Apart from the skin and lungs, the organs em-
ployed in this process of waste are the kidneys and
liver, and the maintenance of those organs in
thorough working order is the grand secret of good
health.
The Kidneys. —The function which the kidneys
perform is to filter the blood and otherwise
separate from it the surplus water and the various
impurities which it contains. These impurities
are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter, which,
with water, constitute the urine.
When the kidneys have done their work, the
purified blood is delivered to the veins, and is
returned to the heart, having in its course
traversed the body and become laden with a fresh
supply of waste matter. The heart again delivers
the blood to the kidneys, which once more filter
it and extract the impurities. This process is
continuous.
The Liver. —The part the liver plays in the
process of waste is chiefly the manufacturing from
the blood the liquid called “ Bile,” which is
delivered by the liver into the intestines, and acts
as a natural cathartic.
The liver and kidneys are very sensitive organs.
Sudden chills, fever, alcoholic intemperance, and
tropical heat are all liable to congest the liver and
reduce it to an abnormal condition. The kidneys
are exceedingly susceptible to inflammation. An
ordinary feverish cold, which closes the pores and
hinders the skin in performing its share in the
removal of the waste products of the body, throws
a burden of extra work upon the kidneys which
they may be unable to perform. Influenza, or
any kind of fever, taxes the kidneys unduly, and
may cause them to break down under the strain.
Any inflammation of the kidneys is very apt to
affect the liver, and vice versa.
Should the liver or kidneys, or both, become
unable to fulfil their purpose thoroughly, then the
blood becomes laden with urinary and biliary
poisons, which are carried to every part of the
body.
Sometimes these poisons become deposited in
the joints or tissues, and cause Rheumatism,
Lvmibago, Sciatica, or Gout. In other cases the
blood, being contaminated, is in an unfit condition
to absorb nutriment, which causes Anaemia,
Neuralgia, digestive troubles, and Debility. In
other cases the supply of bile to the intestines
being inadequate, Constipation, with its attendant
evils result; in others, Biliousness. In others
again the impurity of the blood develops boils and
skin eruptions. Sometimes the uric acid is
deposited in the kidneys and produces Stone,
Gravel, and urinary and bladder troubles.
The reason, then, that Warner’s Safe Cure cures
so many apparently diverse disorders is that they
all proceed from one cause, namely -the inability
of the kidneys or liver to do their work properly,
and so rid the blood of these poisons in the
manner provided by nature.
Warner’s Safe Cure has complete mastery over
functional and chronic liver and kidney derange-
ments, and by restoring those vital organs to
health and activity, does away with the cause of
these several complaints. When the kidneys and
liver are efficiently carrying out the wonderful
scheme of work allotted to them by nature, none
of the disorders here enumerated can exist.
A pamphlet containing fuller particulars
relating to Warner’s Safe Cure and the diseases it
cures will be sent post free to any address on
application to H. H. Warner and Co., Limited,
Australasian Branch, $2B to 532 Little Lonsdale
Street, Melbourne, Victoria.

Bards of the Bacblocks.
Broken Hill—the grimy place where they
produce silver, lead, maimed men and miners’
widows—had at least one institution that it looked
on with satisfaction: its hospital. Sick of the
begging and cadging which alone keeps most
N.S.W. country hospitals going, and sick also of
feeling that when they were taken to the hospital
they were accepting charity, the miners made a
bargain with the hospital board, the mining co’s.
and the Government. They authorised the co’s.
to keep back a halfpenny in the £ on their wages,
on condition that the co’s. subsidised that amount
equally and paid the lot to the hospital, and the
Government, as usual, paid £ for £. In return,
the men nominated a third and the co’s. a third of
the members of the board of management; and
the hospital undertook to provide accommodation
for every suitable case. The scheme was a com-
Slete success—perhaps the most successful ever
evised in Australia for maintaining a hospital;
and latterly it became possible to reduce the con-
tributions by 50 per cent. Without warning,
however, the Truck Act unintentionally knocked
out the scheme, since it prohibited deductions by
any employer from his workmen’s wages. A too-
comprehensive amending Bill, to climb over the
difficulty, was voted down by the Assembly ; but
another try is to be made—this time limiting the
operations of the amending Bill to Broken Hill.
On Lachlan SidE
Silk crests upon their clothing and silver on the
gear,
With roller-hood and quarter-cloth they load their
favorites here;
But sheltering in the timber where half his com-
rades died
The pearl of all good horses runs out on Lachlan
Side !
Six hundred for a team to drive, and eighty for a
hack !
Full-fed, and blown and weary with their ten miles
out and back !
Not one to see a muster through or stand a station
ride
With a horse that I could name you on the Lachlan
River Side !
Straw-bedded to the fetlocks they tug their clover
hay!
Their corn is in the manger set, four full feeds in
the day !
But out upon the wintry flats with bones that
stretch his hide
A better than the best of them goes loose on
Lachlan Side !
I’d give the loafing stable-full, the best of box and
stall,
For one game-headed warrior whose heart could
hold them all !
Ah ! the nights are cold in August where the
station hackneys bide,
I hope they’ll spare a rug for one that runs on
Lachlan Side!
ENG.
WILL OGILVIE.
The Boot of Fate.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’re a God-forsaken legion, and we lift our
heads with pride,
Or sit, blown with self-importance, in the saddles
we bestride,
Caring not for Fate’s grim shadow or for Des-
tiny’s bequests,
For Conceit has set a temple for self-worship in
our breasts;
Ever striving for a fancy or a yearning undefined,
While our ears are deaf to footsteps that are
creeping up behind, —
Till the rough-shod foot has touched us, and our
folly shows too late,
And we’re kicked from out our stirrups by the
ruthless Boot of Fate.
There are wreaths of fame and glory that we fancy
we can win:
“Nothing surer—very easy,” comes the whisper
from within ;
And our pride and self-importance make us eager
to compete,
But we wonder what has struck us when we’re
lifted from our seat.
When a woman’s smile has lured us—to the Fiend
with spur and whip !
Shall we think of girth and stirrup when we kiss
a ruby lip ?
And we never heed disaster, and we ride a reck-
less gait,
But it takes a practised acrobat to dodge the Boot
of Fate !
Then be gentle, stiff-backed brothers, to the men
who are unhorsed;
Some mad passion of a moment in a smooth career
has forced
Just one reckless, mad, wild gallop when the rein
has hung too slack,
And they did not think old Nemesis was riding on
their track.
Have a feeling for the footmen as you press on in
the race;
There are many who rode like you and who watch
your giddy pace,
And some trifle long-forgotten, that you never
calculate,
May start the cursed lever that controls the Boot
of Fate !
N.S.W.
JAMES F. DWYER.

none of them are worth the space they occupy if any form of moisture is
allowed to come in contact with them.
A great desideratum, therefore, is to exclude all moisture from the
insulation. To do this an absolutely moisture-proof protection must be
provided, one that will stand the great range and variety of temperature
incident to chilling and freezing rooms without deterioration. It must
also be capable of withstanding the shrinkage of the timbers to which it is
attached.
Many materials have been tried, and for a time have given question-
able satisfaction. Some papers are made temporarily waterproof by the
use of oils, which (in a short time) are absorbed by the boarding against
which they are laid, which leaves the paper totally unfit for waterproof
insulation. Other papers never were and never will be wa^P r °° f !
although so represented. P& B Insulating Paper (impregnated) and
P & B Ruberoid Roofing are, however, the only materials which have
stood the test of time and are known to furnish complete immunity from
moisture. The cost of insulation with P& B Paper or P& B Ruberoid is
approximately the same. We therefore say unhesitatingly—

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1901.
The £4,000,000 Loan and Other Loans.
A few days ago New South Wales floated a
new £4,000,000 loan in London, to the great
anger of some other States, which also
intended holding out the familiar hat of
mendicant Australia in the usual quarter,
and which found themselves forestalled.
The London market has partly recovered
from its late illness, and is able to get about
again, but, all the same, it can’t stand many
big Australian loans in rapid succession just
at present—hence the ill-feeling above-men-
tioned. All over Australasia just now there
appears to be an idea that the period of
local loans is over, either wholly or to a great
extent, for the time being, and that it is time
for this country to howl again at the door of
its old 4 and 5 per cent, benefactor, John
Bull. Not that these States ever really
ceased to make hungry appealing noises at J.
Bull’s portal, or ever really took to supply-
ing their own financial requirements ; but
for a few years they raised a considerable
proportion of their borrowings from their
own people, and only howled partly on their
distant relative’s premises for assistance.
But lately there has become manifest a feel-
ing that they should howl there more com-
pletely and unanimously.
* * * *
There is no visible necessity behind this
feeling, for money is not at present unusually
scarce in Australasia. At latest advices
(last June) there was £22,786,680 of coin
and bullion in the banks of this country,
which is less than there has been at some
recent periods, yet it is a million and a
quarter more than there was at some dates
in 1898. Also it is rather more than there
was three months ago, so at present the
stock is rather increasing than diminishing.
All the local borrowing that has been done
of late yeans—and there has been a good
deal of it—has made no permanent reduction
in this country’s accumulation of cash, as
shown by the banking returns. These are
the stocks of coin in the banks, taking no
account of the (probably) four or five
millions in private hands, for the June
quarters of the last four years :
June, 1898 £21,948,095
» 1899 21,180,965
>. 1900 25,006,727
» 1901 22,786,680
The temporary inflation of a year ago has
not been maintained, but, despite much bor-
rowing in the local market during the last
three years, there is more money in that
market than there was three years ago. The
money borrowed locally was also spent
locally ; it remained in the country, and the
fact that it had been borrowed makes no
difference whatever in the fact that it is
there available to be borrowed again. Also,
it is fully as available to be taken by the
Government in the form of taxation as to be
taken in the form of loan.
* * •* *
If Australasia was a country with a
very scanty stock of available cash,
then there might be some excuse for the
policy of floating large loans in England or
other foreign parts. If the money was
not here, then the Governments couldn’t
well find it here, either by taxation
or by floating loans. But this country is
better supplied in proportion to its popula-
tion than most others. It isn’t good to
inquire too closely where it got this supply.
The important fact is that it is here. And if
only Australasia gets no further in debt, and
so far restricts its imports of merchandise by
a Protective system that it can pay for them
and cover the interest on its foreign liabili-
ties, and, possibly, pay a little off the prin-
cipal, by its export of merchandise and by its
new output of gold and silver, the stock
isn’t likely to diminish. The following are
the supplies of gold and silver money per
head in the five countries which possess the
largest hoards, as set forth in Coghlan’s
‘‘ Wealth and Progress of N.S. W.,” page 939:
Gold per Silver per Coin per
Inhabitant. Inhabitant. Inhabitant.
France .. ..£4 6 7 £2 4 10 £6 11 5
Australasia .. 5 18 1 057 638
United States .. 2 11 1 1 15 2 463
Germany.. .. 2 12 6 0 16 9 393
Great Britain and
Ireland .. 2 6 3 0 12 7 2 17 10
Considering that Australasia has the largest
stock of gold per inhabitant in the world,
and the largest combined stock of gold and
silver per inhabitant in the world, the neces-
sity for floating £4,000,000 loans in England
or anywhere else, isn’t very apparent. With
all this available coin it should be possible to
raise what is wanted locally by loans, or still
better, by taxation. The public works ex-
penditure doesn’t leave the country, or the
portion that leaves it is an infinitesimal
fraction. If every penny of this country’s
public works outlay was raised locally it
wouldn’t diminish the country’s cash assets.
Yet there is a foolish and unaccountable
legend that this country must, of necessity,
go borrowing from States that have less
money lying around loose than Australasia
itself possesses!
* % * * *
The alleged necessity doesn’t arise because
the public works expenditure is anything
very extraordinary. It is made to look
extraordinary by classing as public works an
immense number of items that, in other
countries, are treated as mere ordinary ex-
penditure, but that is only an arithmetical
delusion. Nor is it because taxation is
already very %eavy. The revenue and ex-
penditure are made to look unusually large
by including in them the whole railway
receipts and outgoings ; and by putting into
the national accounts items that many other
countries leave Local Government bodies to
provide for ; but Australasia is not, on the
whole, a heavily-burdened country, though
by reason of the increase of its debt it is
steadily getting that way. The explanation of
the present tendency to float another mass of
large Australasian loans in London is that the
local market won’t provide the money on the
old terms. The local market has the money,
but there is an inclination to ask for higher
rates than recently. It wants per cent,
just now, and it may even want 3f or 4 per
cent, for a new loan with which to repair old
roads, and dredge old rivers that have been
dredged with previous loans and have
silted up again, and to rebuild old bridges
(not yet paid for) that have worn out,
and do other like works. And if the
local market was applied to for a loan
to pay civil service salaries, and to meet the
half-year’s interest on the public debt, and
the military charges, and the wages of tram-
way conductors and railway officials, it would
probably meet the application in exactly the
same way. But even an O’Sullivan doesn’t
ask the local market how it feels about these
matters ; he simply takes the money by
taxation. In like manner he takes the
money to pay interest on English loans
raised for public works. These things are
regarded as a matter of course. But to take
the money for the works themselves and
have no English loans is contrary to the
insane and drunken precedents of this great
country —Pkovidence only knows why.
* * * *
The new Cornstalk loan of £4,000,000 will
make the debt of N.S.W. about £75,000,000.
It will bring a lot of money into the State,
and in two years or so there will almost cer-
tainly be no more money in the State than
there is now. Also, there will be no more
property by reason of the loan ; it is floated
merely to pay for the transfer of certain
existing properties from private owners to
the State. The State will owe £4,000,000
extra, and there will be £130,000 a year
extra to pay in interest, but while the
liability will last for an absolutely unlimited
period, the benefit will be a mere temporary
ripple on the surface. In the days of the
old financial razzle-dazzle Australasia has
borrowed close on £12,000,000 in London in
one year, and the cash in the seven States
only showed an increase of £300,000. It has
also, in one recent year, borrowed as much
as £4,000,000 locally without the cash in the
country showing any diminution, and if
it had raised that £4,000,000 locally by
taxation the result would have been the
same. The only probable gain through the
See-O'Sullivan £4,000,000 British loan
will be that, for a year or two, there will
be a slight extra activity in trade—
that imports will look up for a while—
that there will be less room for local industry
through imports looking up —and when the
temporary excitement is over the exports
of this country will have to show £130,000
a year more of a margin over the imports
because there will be that much more to pay
to our foreign creditor.
m * m m
The four years from 1896 to 1900 were
years when foreign borrowing was about at
its lowest in Australasia. During that period
these States relied more on their own
resources than they had done fora very long
time. They were years when agriculture
expanded enormously, and the manufacturing
population increased in that brief period
more than it had done in 15 previous
years. In the old boom days of the
big English loans manufactures were prac-
tically stationary, and agriculture progressed
slowly, and Australasia devoted itself mostly
to the useless haulage of goods bought with
loans, to founding financial institutions, to
drinking the borrowed money, and to mar-
velling at its own genius for bringing about
such an unexampled state of prosperily.
Most of the country’s solid progress has been
within the few recent years when it gave
the English money market something ap-
proaching a rest; and if it had totally
abjured foreign borrowing instead of only
doing so in part, the progress would
probably have been very much greater.
The Commonwealth now contrives to export
£13,000,000 worth more goods than it im-
ports, and the difference pays a good part of
its annual liability to the enormous foreign
creditor without in any way reducing its cash
resources. If it wholly swore off foreign bor-
rowing it would probably contrive to export
£16,000,000 more than it imports, and pay
the whole of its annual liability—also with-
out reducing its local cash resources. If
it decided to begin wiping off the principal
of its foreign debt, it would probably find it
quite possible to export £20,000,000 more
than it imports. It is only necessary to
keep down the imports by a rigid system of
Protection ; to make more boots and clothes,
and to buy less abroad ; to employ more men
in factories and fewer at hauling the produce
of foreign factories off foreign ships at our
wharves ; and to pay no heed to the glozing
voice of the interested importer who rells
this country, for his own mean and selfish
ends, that what it wants is to buy from his
bagman lots of foreign goods sent out by his
London warehouse, and to let local industry
slide. And, on the other hand, if See,
O’Sullivan and co. have their way, and the
foreign-loan policy resumes on a large scale,
imports may soon exceed exports again, as
they did in 1891 and previous years, and
this country’s industries may easily get back
again to the stagnant level of 1891, when
they were not much more than half their
present dimensions. Then the good old
days, when Australia did little real work,
but stood around in a long-tailed coat and a
shiny tall hat founding bogus banks, may
come back again in all their glory and all
their consequences.
“The Bulletin” hears from some
Aldermen.
The Bulletin has received a bulky document
purporting to be a scheme for creating a Federated
Municipal Council for Greater Sydney, while
still preserving the 43 existing municipal councils
with their mayors and aldermen, their separate
bottles of refreshment in the mayoral cupboard,
their gauds, frills, dignities, pomps, and appur-
tenances. Why, in the name of all that is won-
derful, a city of less than 500,000 people should
want 44 municipal bodies passes all comprehen-
sion. It is proposed that nobody shall be eligible,
to sit in the great boss council unless he is already
a local alderman, which is about as effective a way
of blighting the usefulness of the Greater Sydney
Council as could well be imagined. Plenty of
good and capable men would be willing to
go up for election to such a body if they
could get there without first crawling through
a preliminary servitude in a petty back
street body, which is of little account now,
and which, when its most important functions
are delegated to a superior council, will be of no
account at all. The idea is as foolish as if it was
proposed that the billet of Premier should be
elective, only churchwardens to stand for election.
The great object is to get away from the existing
type of alderman ; not to get him in an aggravated
form. But the scheme now on hand has been
drawn up by a conference of aldermen, and their
first idea was evidently to make themselves a per-
manent ornament of the N.S.W. metropolis; to
preserve all their present billets by keeping the
43 existing councils in full blast; and to make new
billets (for which they alone shall be eligible) by
establishing a 44th council as well.
* * * *
It is a sorrowful thing to find the 43 sets of
aldermen buzzing round the question of municipal
reform in any capacity. Municipal reform is
wanted because most of these aldermen are incom-
petent and superfluous. Because they are incom-
petent they can’t be trusted with the work of draft-
ing a reform scheme, and it is impossible to expect
them to recognise their own superfluity. Their
prompt attempt to swell their own dignity by
making only themselves eligible for seats on the
Council of Greater Sydney, and their scheme for
preserving all their existing uselessness, and their
instantaneous howling at the front door of the
Government for financial assistance (for they are
already crying out for pauper aid in the way of a
subsidy) are too melancholy for words. What is
wanted is a Government with grit enough to
ignore the buzzing of the 43 sets of aldermen, and
to refer the matter directly to the people. It
should draft a comprehensive scheme, and then
take a vote of all the municipalities upon it, and
let the aldermen slide. The people come first.
The aldermen come last, if they come at all.
* • • *
The only difficulty in the way of union, apart
from the dismal self-importance of' 43 sets of
aldermen, lies in the fact that the existing muni-
cipalities are not equally in debt, or equally
insolvent. Some have good assets, and some have
only an unpaid gas-bill, and a dead fly and a
broken venetian-blind on the council chamber
window. But even this matter can be got over,
if the persons concerned really desire to get over
it. Each existing municipality might form a
ward of Greater Sydney. According to its
population it could have 20 representatives, or
two, or half a representative—among the very
small ones two could divide a representative
between them. Their assets (apart from roads
and streets, which would be thrown in) could be
valued by an independent commission appointed
bytheGovernment—its members could be brought,
if necessary, from another State to ensure their
absolute disinterestedness. These assets would be
taken over by the Greater Sydney Council at the
price thus fixed, and the amount deducted, from
each ward’s debt. Then in each ward a separate
rate could be levied for the extinction of the old
separate debts. It could be graduated according
to the size of the debt, so that all the old liabilities
would become extinct about the same time. And
when the last of them were extinguished, the old
divisions would no longer be required ; they could
be abolished in one lot, and the city divided into
wards on a more sensible plan. All debts con-
tracted after the Greater Sydney Council was
established would be the debts of the whole metro-
politan area, and it would only be necessary to
perpetuate the existing divisions until the old,
separate liabilities were liquidated
• mm*
Under this system no one of the 43 existing
municipalities would get ahead of the other, or
shove its debts on the other, or have the
other’s debts shoved on it. It would be a
matter of only a few years to clear up
what remained of the existing financial confusion
after credit was given for the assets surrendered.
And it would be possible to immediately abolish
the 43 existing gangs of aldermen, and create in
their stead a Greater Sydney Council with govern-
ing powers over the 43 municipalities and probably
a considerable stretch of additional territory. This
body could be given power to control street-traffic,
water, sewerage and tramways, to acquire
or establish gas, electric-lighting, hydraulic power
and other works, to compulsorily resume property
for widening or straightening streets, making new
streets, and other improvements, to control
slaughter-houses, markets, fire-brigades, &c., to
build bridges, workmen’s dwellings and all manner
of property, to establish baths, laundries, libraries,
museums, recreation-grounds, to run its own
public-houses, its own ferries, its own Mont
de Piete—in fact, its own anything-it-darned-
well-pleased, or thereabouts. It should have
authority to levy rates up to ss. in the £,
and to borrow money for works only —not for
revenue deficiencies—with a sinking fund attached
to each loan. And its accounts should be State-
audited, it being compulsory on the State auditor,
when the year ends with a deficit, to order a
sufficient increase of rates for next year to put the
accounts straight. And this council should be
elected by adult suffrage, and go bodily for re-
election every third year.
* • • »
If the See-O’Sullivan Ministry can rest awhile
from doing big things with big loans attached, and
do a big thing with no loan attached, it might
draft some such scheme as this, and submit it to a
popular vote for acceptance or rejection. The
people have some right to be considered as well as
the 43 sets of aldermen. The 43 sets of
aldermen can’t be expected to agree on any
scheme of union, for the need of a scheme
of union arises out of the fact that the system
of which these 43 sets of aldermen are the centre
is wholly unsatisfactory. No 43 sets of aldermen
can be expected to see that a system which enables
them to exist officially can be unsatisfactory. It
is very seldom in this world that any person—
especially any undesirable person—abolishes him-
self. It is almost as seldom that he recognises
that he should be abolished. The very fact that
the groundwork of the aldermanic scheme consists
in perpetuating the present crowd, and reserving
for them all the positions in the Greater Sydney
Council, and enabling the present crowd to sit in
two councils, when they have already proved
themselves bad enough in one, shows that the
work is being commenced at the wrong end.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Philp’s Plural Vote.
Robert Philp, Premier of Queensland, in his
desire to fish, after an awkward and unaccustomed
fashion, for democratic support, and his hereditary
instinct to give somebody a plural vote for some-
thing, has landed himself in a curious situation.
His new Electoral Bill abolishes any semblance of
the plural property vote, and comes reasonably
near to establishing manhood and womanhood
suffrage. A man or woman may claim a vote on a
residence qualification provided either has lived 12
months in any part of Queensland, and is living
in the electoral district for which the claim is
made. A person owning property can, appa-
rently, claim a vote without the 12 months’
residence—which is a difference, but still not
enough of a difference to be worth making much
trouble about. But the interesting part of
Philp’s Bill comes in here:
Clause 23: Every male elector who is, or has been
married, and who is, or has been, the father of at least
two children born in Queensland in lawful wedlock shall,
subject to the provisions of this Act, be entitled to give
two votes at any election for the district on the roll of
which his name appears. It is immaterial whether or
not (a) the elector is a widower, or has been judicially
separated or divorced from his wife ; or (b) all or any of
his children have died; or (c) all or any of his children
are presently resident in Queensland.
This clause is calculated to bring some unexpected
fame down on Premier Philp. It makes Queens-
land the only State of the British Empire (at
least, the only one within this writer’s knowledge)
which indirectly penalises other British citizens,
and it makes it the only State in Australia where
one Australian is not as good as another. If a
Queenslander has two children born in Queensland,
one of whom leaves the country, while the other is
hanged at great public expense, he has two votes.
If he has two children that die from neglect, h»


has two votes. If he has two children whom he
doesn’t support, or if his wife has to prooure a
divorce on the ground of cruelty and drunkenness,
and takes the two children across the border,
where she makes an honest crust for them by
washing, still the male remnant that she leaves
behind has two votes. But, on the other hand, the
Englishman, Scotchman, Irishman, Cornsjalk,
Victorian, or other white British subject who
comes to Queensland with a sturdy family of 10,
all of whom settle in the State, can never get
more than one vote, unless he has two more
children born locally. Even the naturalised
nigger who has two children born in Queensland,
no matter what becomes of them, ranks higher as
a voter than the white man who imports a family
of a dozen. Evidently the value and importance
of being born in Queensland is something consider-
able in Premier Philp’s estimation. But,
strangely enough, though the father of
these two local offspring gets an extra
vote the mother gets none. The birth
of two female infants brings an extra vote
to the male parent, but not to the female parent.
This is an anomaly calculated to make the latter
feel angry, especially if her husband is out drink-
ing when the event takes place, and hasn’t pro-
vided the baby-clothes. Further, it doesn’t ap-
pear to be specified that the children must be born
alive, though possibly that is understood ; nor that
they must be in full possession of their faculties.
It isn’t likely that much serious harm will result
from these anomalies; still Philp’s Bill is a
curious evidence of that habit of mind which can’t
be content with simple equality, and which
must needs give somebody an extra vote
for something. In a general way it is
much better to give a man an extra
vote for having two honestly-acquired children
than for having a (probably) dishonestly-acquired
block of city property ; and it isn’t a case of giving
an extra suffrage to the rich, for rich people have
no more legitimate children than poor ones. Still,
by way of an amendment, the woman who has the
children should be considered as well as the man ;
the parents who have ten children should have
five extra votes apiece if there are going to be
extra votes : and only live children should count.
There is po special merit in being born ; it is
growing up that is the difficulty!
The Inflowing Nigger.
A paper issued by the Federal Government and
dealing with the arrivals and departures of
Chinese, Asiatics, and other blacks, browns,
yellows and sundries during the past six years,
throws a lurid light on the boast of Queensland
politicians that their Kanaka state takes quite
extraordinary pains to keep out the cheap and
murky alien. The Philp Ministry and its appur-
tenances and hangers-on are given to posing—even
while they indignantly demand that their nigger
shall be left with them—as people who have made
quite unusual sacrifices in the cause of White
Australia. And this is how the sacrifices look
when brought down to details :

Bill, likewise shows a decrease. In fact, wherever
the educational test has been applied, and applied
honestly, the colored population is diminishing.
Whether the device is a straightforward one
there may be some question, and certain
politicians profess to be greatly troubled over this
aspect of the case ; but about its efficiency, when
it is meant to be efficient, there is no room for
doubt. But —solely on account of the three States

reason for pushing on the Immigration Restriction
Bill with all speed. Also—and a still better reason
for making haste to close the back door which
looks towards Asia—it appears that, in later years,
the colored man is actually crowding out the white.
Coghlan only brings his immigration statistics up
to 1899, but there are some startling results even
in this limited patch of information. Take the
total increase or decrease as shown by Coghlan ;
deduct the nigger increase, and see how much
white man is left :

These are solemn figures. In the last two years

the Commonwealth lost 5235 white inhabitants by
emigration, and gained 1947 colored people by
way of a most lamentable exchange. For the last
three years it gained 1212 whites and 2700 niggers.
Truly it is time to reach out for the first efficient
remedy that comes to hand, and consider its
abstract honesty afterwards. Whichever effective
remedy will get there first is the remedy that the
Commonwealth wants.

The Boss Heathen.
The following cablegram appeared in the daily
papers of last Saturday—and the Australian
people are still expected to maintain a sentiment
of respect and admiration for the Empire
BRITISH COLOMBIA AND ASIATIC IMMIGRA-
TION.
A RESTRICTION BILL DISALLOWED.
London, Sept. 13.
At the instance of Japan, Great Britain has per-
suaded Canada to disallow bills of the British Colum-
bian Provincial Legislature imposing an educational
test to exclude Chinese and Japanese, and forbidding
their employment on certain works.
It doesn’t appear for certain whether this means
that Britain, at the dictation of Japan, has gone
back on its policy of permitting an educational
test, or whether the disallowance of the Bill arose
because of the clause forbidding the employment
of Asiatics on “certain works,” but, either way,
it seems possible that a very serious situation
is ahead. The present British Government seems
to be mostly owned by Japan, and its policy is
largely dictated by an Asiatic nation with a
thin veneer of civilisation on top of its Orientalism.
There was a time when Britain was ruler of its
territory and also of a good deal outside, but
apparently the empire has spread out too thin of
late years. It is vastly larger than it was, but
where it used to rely on itself it now relies, in part
at least, on nigger alliances, and it has to placate
its brown supporter. Moreover, the traders of
England want free admission to Japan, and the
traders of England regard it as quite a fair thing
that this advantage should be bought by the free
admission of the Jap. to Australia and the
Canadian Dominion. England itself is too far off
to be personally troubled by a Jap. influx, and
even if Australia or Columbia are overrun by
the brown pestilence the traders of England
feel that they can bear up under the other fellow’s
calamity. This flat refusal of the right to keep its
race pure and its country white is the first instal-
ment of Canada’s reward for the sacrifices it made
in S’Africa. Australia got its exactly-similar
reward a few months ago when the Queens-
land Bill, restricting colored labor in State-sub-
sidised sugar-mills, was returned with contumely,
and some further reward of the same kind may be in
store. In each case, apparently, the reason was the
same ; .Japan issued its orders that the thing was
not to be, and the thing wasn’t. In each case,
apparently, the assistance lately rendered in
Africa with so much enthusiasm and grovel merely
inspired the Salisbury Government with the
idea that “the colonies,” in their intense
Jingoism, wouldn’t mind being sacrificed for
the convenience of the traders of England.
There was a time when Britain was hardly so off-
hand and peremptory in its dealings, but that was
before “the colonies,” by their outburst of abject
reverence in the Boer war days, Convinced it that
their loyalty would stand almost any kind of
usage. It is quite possible now that the biggest
rocks ahead of the empire are Japanese dictation
and Salisbury’s servility. If thepolicyofsacrificing
the outlying parts of the empire for the sake of
buying Japan’s countenance and assistance, and
staving off _ Japanese hostility, and purchasing
trading privileges for England in Jap. territory, is
continued, the time must come when these de-
pendencies will have to refuse to be sold to any
nigger for Britain’s convenience. And a lot of
things are liable to happen in the process of
refusal.

A Strange Tale of the Push.
Despite the shout of derision which arose over
Ambrose Pratt’s strange tale; from the slums
about Australian larrik-inism, and about the
murdera done by the push, and the disappearances
which the push brings about, there is some vestige
of evidence in Prytt’s favor—more than most
people would imagine. N.S.W. is not, in propor-
tion to population, the most push-cursed State of
Australia; and Sydney’s reputation smells a little
better in this respect than Melbourne's. Yet,
taking at random Coghlan’s statistics for ’99, in
that year N.S. W. had a record off 26 murders, and
in addition there were' 37 deaths by violence,
where nobody was seen committing murder—
where the victim was simply found fatally injured.
These were apart from deaths believed to be the
result of accident. This is saying nothing of the
very numerous cases where the victim was found
injured, but not fatally—probably because the
push thought it heard somebody coming.
And against these 63 fatal cases there
were eight convictions. There are often
half a dozen concerned in a push outrage, so it
is quite possible that 100 or 150 murderers
escaped arrest in one year in N.S.W. alone.
Anyhow, in seven cases out of every eight nobody
was proved to be responsible for anything. Also,
in about two cases out of every three, nobody was
even charged with anything. This country is
accustomed to boast of its very excellent record in
the way of criminality, but any country can have
an excellent record of that sort if its police-force
is only weak enough and inefficient enough. The
point is that the criminal statistics deal only with
persons arrested and persons convicted. Under
this absurd system a country where there are
eight murders in the year and eight convictions,
looks four times as criminal, according to the pub-
lished records, as the one where there are 367
murders and only two convictions.

An Appeal to the People of Australasia.
A letter from America :
To the Editor of Tub Bulletin.
Sir,—Representing a very large number of Americans
who consider themselves the sincere well-wishers of
the British people, we venture to rely upon your courtesy
for the insertion of this letter to your readers.
No one can fail to admire the readiness with which the
Australasian States sprang to the assistance of the
Mother Country as soon as it became known that their
help was needed in South Africa. In such a crisis the
rights and wrongs of the war were, quite naturally, over-
looked, or, in any case, were judged hastily and on the
basis of statements put forward by one of the contending
parties. Nor is it our object, at this late date, to raise
the issues of the past. Quite apart from the merits of the
original dispute, the present shows indisputably that the
burghers of the republics are now doing what every
Australasian worthy of the name would do in similar
circumstances: they are fighting for their national in-
dependence.
If Australasia were to be threatened by the conquering
forces of Germany, of France, or of any other foreign
power, we should sympathise with you in your struggle ;
and this we should do no m itter what allegations might
he made against you and your Government by your
enemies. For that reason, and believing that you will
readily comprehend our attitude, recognising that it is
perfectly compatible with friendliness for yourselves and
for the British people as a whole, we do not hesitate to
proclaim our profound sympathy with the Boers in the
straits in which they are now placed.
To expect you to agree with us fully would be absurd.
But it is possible that you may be willing to entertain a
suggestion which has for one of its ends the re-establish-
ment of the good name of the British Empire among the
mass of our countrymen, who have viewed the proceed-
ings in South Africa with disappointment and regret.
One suggestion is this : that you, who have taken such
an active part in carrying on the war that no one disputes
your right to a voice in the ultimate settlement, shall ac-
cept your responsibilities, and shall exert your influence
in England in favor of a broader view of the situation than
seems to be taken there at present.
You were not personally involved in the original dis
pute, as were the statesmen of Great Britain ; far from
South Africa, you do not share in the local jealousies
which have for so long been known to prejudice the
people of Natal and a section of Gape colonists in their
dealings with the Boers (even as, in the past, local jealousy
between the colonies of America, and later, between those
of Australia, influenced their respective histories). Con-
sequently, when we find a cable sent eagerly to London
and New York, containing an extract from an editorial in
a Natal newspaper, in which it is stated that Australasia
will never again help the Empire in its hour of need,
unless the Home Government insists upon the uncon-
ditional surrender of the Boers, we feel justified in sup-
posing that the passions of Natal do not necessarily reflect
the sentiment of Australasia, and that you are entirely
capable of forming an opinion of your own, in the light of
history and of common sense.
Without, therefore, imitating an example which
attempts to guess your views, or to foist views upon you,
we beg that in forming an independent judgment you
will mentally place yourselves in the position of those in
South Africa who still continue their gallant struggle for
freedom. What would pacify you in similar circum-
stances ? What would best promote the future welfare of
Australasia and of the foreign Empire that might chance
to conquer it ? What form of settlement, in short, would
be the most enduring and the most satisfactory to all
concerned ?
We feel confident that by answering these questions
you will be able to present to the people and Government
of Great Britain a view of the South African situation
that must inevitably broaden their view, and that, if
adopted, will promote the best and highest interests of
your Empire, and raise it in the estimation of the whole
world.—We are, sir, yours faithfully,
William G. Davies,
For the Transvaal League of the Eastern States of
America. 22 William-street, New York, July 15, 1901.
Bronson Murray,
Wilmot Townsend Cox,
Edward Lauterbacii,
Edward Reeve Merritt,
E. Merritt Norris.

Legree Wishes to Acquit Himself.
Fearing Federal action the Westralian nigger-
drivers and their sympathisers are making des-
perate efforts to prevent independent inquiry into
the treatment of aborigines. The latest proposal
is to appoint a select —very select—committee of
the Westralian squatter Parliament, whose mem-
bers will inquire into their own characters, and
find them unstained by a single fleck of black
man’s blood, and report accordingly to Australia.
If Mahon, M.H.R., is firm and the Federal Par-
liament refuses to be fooled, the latter body will
start its investigation just the same, and decline
to take any notice of the fact that Legree is hold-
ing a rigid investigation into his own character.
What is wanted is an independent inquiry—
not an investigation by Legree into the
purity of his own motives, and the kind-
ness of his own actions. The inquiry should
be by a Royal Commission, whose members
are high Federal officers, and not Westralians.
John Forrest’s realm is just now divided into two
camps, from one of which come accusations against
the other of loathsome systematic savagery, and
from the other indignant denials. Obviously
neither accusers nor accused should try the issue.
But that common-sense axiom has not penetrated
to the official mind in Westralia. When com-
plaint was made by a certain constable of brutal
illtreatment of natives in his district, the com-
plaint was forwarded to one of the parties impli-
cated for report. The accused tried the cause,
acquitted himself without a stain on his character,
and had the constable sacked for bringing an
absurd charge against a noble and lofty-minded
citizen. The Westralian squatter Parliament
would like now to repeat that method on a larger
scale. It is noteworthy, as showing the spirit of
the proposed inquiry, that the member who calls
for it is a gentleman who says that he made a
point of attending nigger floggings, and never saw
anything in the aspect of the flogged nigger that
anybody had a right to object to.
The Festival of St. Jingo.
On the invitation of the Government representa-
tive, Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords t’other
day passed a simple little Bill indemnifying cer-
tain municipal councillors for dissipating the local
rates in Commonwealth celebrations. The Bill
reached the Assembly last week, and was received
cordially. When it got through it was heavier
by three amendments—to the effect that the law-
breaking councillors are to be indemnified for all
illegal rate-expenditure, not only on the cele-
brations, but also (1) on the royal mourning, (2)
on memorials to Queen Victoria, (3) on memorials
of the war. On the motion of Premier Peacock the
Bill is to apply retrospectively only (15 members
wanted to make it prospective too); so the position
is that councillors all over the State are invited to
break the existing law and illegally vote the
public’s money on war memorials and such-
like absurdity up to the time when the
Bill receives the Governor’s signature, while the
law-abiding councillors who decline to exploit the
public for the gratification of Jingodoin are warned
that their very honesty puts them “outside the
pale,” from a Jingo point of view. It would save
a lot of talk and trouble if the wearisome munici-
pal councillor were given enough rope to hang
himself at once—by allowing him to spend the
rates according to his own sweet will. He usually
does this, anyhow, and a lot of time and money are
wasted afterwards in indemnifying him for break-
ing the law. He did it at the recent
at the late Queen’s demise, at the Com-
monwealth inauguration ; and lie’ll do it again
next year at the Coronation festivities. This awful
councillor person deliberately flouts the Parlia-
ment which makes laws for his regulation, and the
fool Parliament immediately pats him on the back
and says it likes being made a fool of. Only John
Murray, Prendergast, and a few other radicals
protested against this piece of supreme folly.
Majah ReAy —the amusing amateur soldier and
professional war-correspondent—actually wanted
“every municipality to establish a permanent
memorial of the battle of Pink Hill.” In the
name of all that is wonderful what kind of a two-
men-and-a-dog serum was Pink Hill that 208
statues should be put up concerning it ? Among
the 24 events for which war-medals have been
issued it isn’t even mentioned.
