Suspended.
The sudden suspension by the new Westralian
Commissioner for Railways of the general railway
manager and the Commissioner’s own secretary,
and the granting of a compulsory holiday to the
chief traffic manager, are events that have made
politics sit up in a quite unusual manner. The
revelations in the matter of the Perth Ice Co.
frauds, and many other disclosures, showed clearly
enough that the Westralian railway system was
either hideously corrupt or so utterly incompetent
that it was an unfit person to be at large, and the
new Minister, Holmes, had used threatening
language about it on many occasions before his
accession to office. But a small, casual event
hurried on the trouble. Holmes wanted a secre-
tary, and appointed a well-recommended man who
had been formerly in the service, a careful search
of the records having shown that no offence or
complaint had been recorded against the applicant
during his previous term. Then the general man-
ager demanded to know what Holmes meant by
appointing to a responsible position a man who
had been sacked for serious irregularities, and the
newspapers fell on Holmes, and the Public Service
breathed in the ear of Parliament about the gross
favoritism shown by passing over men with good
records in the service, and appointing a sacked
ex-official. The indignant Minister demanded
why the alleged offences were not on record, and
was informed in a helpless sort of way that the
papers were included in a file that had probably
been mislaid ; anyhow, no one knew where it was,
but when somebody came back from somewhere
it might turn up. The Minister demanded why
valuable papers went astray and thus caused him,
for lack of information, to make disastrous mis-
takes, and the Department’s attitude was to the
effect that valuable papers always went astray in
Westralia, and the thing wasn’t worth getting
excited about. About this time the Minister
commenced to investigate a number of other
malodorous matters, and he seems to have brought
up short almost everywhere against the fact that
some of the papers wanted were missing, and the
officials who ought to explain were always out.
The Leake Ministry doesn’t sit very securely, and
Holmes apparently decided that there was a plan
on hand to systematically lose pampers, and spend
the time feebly looking up the chimney for them,
till the old gang, which did all the shady things,
got back into office again. So one morning he ran
amok, and told some of the parties who had good
salaries and seemed to have the most difficulty .in
remembering anything useful for the money, to go
home and take a rest. ' Holmes’s action will
probably either save or break the Leake
Ministry ; it is hard to say which as yet.
Closer Settlement.
Two States, Tasmania and N.S. Wales, are simul-
taneously considering Bills providing for the
repurchase by the Government of large estates,
and their sub-division into small farms. All the
other States are already doing something in this
line. Maoriland and S. Australia are doing a
good deal; Queensland, Victoria, and Westralia
are pottering with the question—under dark sus-
picions, as regards Westralia, that the system is
mainly used to enable the Six Families and their
friends to sell out at a profit, and as regards
Queensland that it comes in handy to relieve the
Q.N. Bank of some of its bad bargains. In Aus-
tralia, where good land is not abundant, it is
essential that those areas which lie convenient to
markets should be put to the best use, but while
the re-purchase system is essential only two of the
States began it at the right end. Any country
which proposes going in for this kind of
business requires a heavy graduated land-
tax first of all. If that tax is heavy
enough, it makes it so much more profitable
to hold land in small than in large areas that the
big holder wants to sell. If he doesn’t sell to
Government he becomes only too glad to cut up
his estate and sell privately. When, through the
difference in land-tax, an area is worth, say,
£IOO,OOO when cut up into 100 small farms, and
only £75,000 when it is in one large property, there
is always a tendency towards subdivision. Under
the graduated land-tax—if it is only heavy
enough—a breaking-up process is always going on
quite apart from anything Government may do.
And such a tax is needed to prevent the subdi-
vided estates getting together again, and forming
more big properties—to prevent the new small
holders being bought out, or crushed out by fore-
closure of mortgage, till the old evil comes back
once more. The Government which begins
remedying the evil that exists without taking the
slightest precaution against its recurrence, is only
going in again at the same hole it came out of.
In N,S. W., Tasmania, Queensland, Westralia, and
Victoria, no precautions whatever are taken
to prevent more big estates forming; there
is no graduated tax to induce the large
holder to sell; there is no power of compul-
sory purchase—in fact, things are so arranged
that the great mass of large landholders look on
the idea as merely a chance to dispose of their
properties to the fool Government at a higher
price than they could expect to get from any .
private buyer. As a horrible example, the Vic-
torian Government is just now seriously consider-
ing the re-purchase for closer settlement of a lone-
some bit of country at £lO 10s. an acre. Land
fetches an enormous price in Victoria, because
Victoria is the most thickly-populated State in
Australasia; because the land doesn’t carry its
fair share of taxation ; and because it is made arti-
ficially scarce by the existence, alongside closely-
settled districts, of huge areas which are locked
up and used mostly for grazing purposes by their
conservative owners. If a graduated land-tax on
the S. A. plan broke up this artificial scarcity, and
forced these large estates on the market in small
lots, the estate above-mentioned could probably
be bought for £5 an acre. The Bulletin is wait-
ing anxiously for the time when the Federal
Parliament will impose an absentee-tax and a
heavy graduated land-tax over all Australia. It
is the only authority which can over-rule the
opposition of property-elected Councils and of
Assemblies whose souls are in pawn to recon-
structed banks. There are many great oppor-
tunities before the Federal Parliament, and this
is one of the greatest among them.
Banana Land Government is again to che fore
with its old device for refilling a depleted Treasury,
viz.—a Special Sale of Lands Bill. The provisions
of this measure are much more generous than
those of previous ones, inasmuch as land may now
be purchased up to 5000 acres in one block pro-
vided it be not less than “20 miles from a navi-
gable stream.” (The number of navigable streams
in Western Queensland is truly appalling.)
Previous measures used to specify not less
than 20 miles from a railway, but the
limitations thereby imposed on the possi-
bilities of grab were altogether too severe
for certain rapacious appetites in the Northern
land of Boodle. Moreover, another delightful
provision allows the proceeds the money
raised, it was stated, to meet the deficit—to be
paid into the Consolidated Revenue and used to
diminish the too colossal proportions of the next
deficit —which is about as certain as sunrise and
sunset. It is interesting to remember that the
first Act of this kind (passed by the Griffith-
MTlwraith Govt, in 1891) left the deficit of that
day just where it was ; the ugly thing had to be
funded, and when this one is funded also Queens-
land will have added something over £2,000,000
to her indebtedness, despite special sales of land
and similar heroic proceedings.
Chapman, of N.S.W., has two motions on the
notice-paper of the Federal House of Represen-
tatives, to the effect that the Federal Government
should, with all possible speed, take over the
State railways and at least a portion of the State
debts—presumably the whole debt of the State
which has the smallest liability per inhabitant,
and a corresponding amount of the debts of
other States. The Bulletin hopes to see both
proposals carried by an overwhelming majority
and with this addition—that all savings made by
the conversion or renewal or consolidation of the
debts taken over, and all savings made in the cost
of management thereof, shall go into a sinking-
fund for the repayment of the said debts, and that
a further sum of (say) £500,000 a year be set apart
for the same purpose. In other words, that the
debt taken over be kept separate from any
new debt which the Federal Government
may contract, and that the whole pre-
sent cost of the old debt, with £500,000
a year added, shall be made a permanent charge
on the Commonwealth revenue till that old debt
is extinguished—the English portion of the debt
being, if possible, extinguished first. This is a
world where something new is always charming.
It is always good to get out of the groove in whicn
we are accustomed to amble, like a small dog trot-
ting along ina deepfurrowand losingall the scenery

A new Melbourne radical sheet, The
Patriot (the name of James Mirams appears as
publisher), fills much space in its first number with
unacknowledged steals from The Bulletin. There
is a class of patriots ‘ ‘ who left their country for
their country’s good.”
The Arcadian Nights entertainment, about
to be presented in Sydney, is a new departure in
the world of art, and, as such, should excite much
attention. Most distinguished patronage is being
accorded the affair, and a list of the patrons will
shortly appear in The Bulletin.
Obit , N.S.W. ex-Speaker Abbott, whose
fine figure and impressive voice carried him far in
politics. For years he threatened to become Pro-
tectionist Premier of N.S. Wales, but his coldness
and want of personal magnetism forbade it. The
ex-Speaker, who was a solicitor by profession, was
an excellent business adviser, and had he stuck
strictly to the law would probably have succeeded
much better fi’om a worldly point of view than he
did by dabbling in politics and company-director-
ships. He was not personally popular with Par-
liamentarians —the Labor-party particularly used
to fume at him—but there is no doubt he was a
fine figure of a Speaker.
There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wjllg, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notifies which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
Grand opera “ Lucia ” again filled Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday night by an
audience that received the Italianised Scotch
maiden, her friends and enemies, with a fervor
warm as the night itself. It was mainly a first-
night audience, so, being familiar with the work,
it began to take notice of things—trifles—behind
the grandeur of the grand opera. It looked at
the backgrounds painted by one Rovescalli, of
Milan, and the Macphersons said that the artist
had not got away from his Italian environment of
atmosphere and architecture. Amber days, oxi-
dised-silver nights, sapphire skies, classic gate-
ways, halls and walls. M'Tavish looked at the
Ravenswood graveyard through his glasses, and
said to Macpherson that no true Ashton
could rest comfortably in such a place. None
of the tombstones suggested a belief in plain,
flat-footed eternal punishment. And the park
entrance, with its back-ground of chaste white
wall and the Roman maiden (Marchi) pacing to
and fro (when she leaves the lovers together)—it
was a picture out of the times of Lorenzo the
Magnificent, and the Signorina seemed a maiden
of the house of Medicis seeking her lover, whilst
cardinals and princes listened to the reading of a
poem somewhere behind the white wall. Macpher-
son disapproved of the tapestried hall. Instead
of maidens, trees, and lutes, the scene should have
been the killing of Red Comyn by the Bruce.
Also, he objected to the name Lucy being changed
to Loochia, and as to Enrico being a synonym for
Henry, the idea made him yearn for Buchanan’s
blend. Both were very indignant with the
Italians for wearing white silk tights™ with
their kilties. Both chortled with delight
over Sapio’s voice, face, _ and figure, and
pronounced her the bonniest Scotch lassie
that ever stepped out of a Walter Scott
story. After the interval they discovered
with glee that the American soprano’s Italian had
a Scotch accent. In the mad-scene, where Lucia
sings to the music of the flute alone, and where
you can scarcely tell Sapio’s voice from the flute
itself, both Scotsmen vowed that, for them, the
bag-pipes were no more. The flute was
Scotia’s national instrument. As for Edgardo
(Dani), they doubted if a man who could
sing so gloriously would be sent anywhere
but to Heaven even if he did commit suicide.
And when the music had died with the death
of the singers, both men went away humming
bits of the opera (the bits that are scattered
through Hemy’s exercise-books, and which
the Macpherson and M‘Tavish children prac-
tise every day), saying that it was haunting
music, and that they’d get the baimies to
learn some.
## # #
“ Arrah-na-Pogue,” played by Maggie
Moore and Co. at Sydney Lvceum, is a
rather cheerful Irish drama wherein there
are no evictions, and where nobody would
get into trouble very much if Maggie Moore
did not try to save Vincent Scully, and
Charles Blake sacrifice himself at great
length for Maggie, everybody getting a free
pardon in the last act after the hero has
swum from prison, arriving in a dry condi-
tion to be forgiven. Maggie and Charles
Blake sacrifice themselves for M‘Cool
(Scully), because they recollect gratefully
that he never asked for his rent, and Blake
considers that he should allow himself to be
hanged in consequence. If the Irish tem-
perament prefers to pay rent in this way, it
is, of course, only a question for individual
taste. The person who writes this prefers
paying up in the usual weekly and un-
romantic fashion, when she has it, and if
she hasn’t it she prefers owing it in the
ordinary ungrateful way. If Miss Moore,
who dresses in bright red most of the time,
could keep the rest of her as girlish as her
voice, it would be some advantage to. the
drama. As things are, however, there is no
need for her lover on his wedding day
to express a fear lest she should unfold her
fairy wings like a butterfly and fly away,
because Maggie does not look that sort of girl.
Otherwise, she is a very taking Irish damsel, and
when she sang the “ Wearin’ of the Green ” on
Saturday night, an Irishman in the gallery
bumped himself severely on the floor by way
of applause, and other persons remarked
“ Hooroosh,” or made corresponding statements.
A feature in this play is the fact that the
peasantry really wear peasant costumes, instead of
the fancy dresses they donned in the previous
Irish drama. J. P. West makes a good study of
Michael Feeney, the spy; and the court-scene,
wherein the oppressed Irish are allowed to make
as much noise as they like, is an interesting side-
light on that most distressful country. Miss
Madge Herrick is amateurish as the more aris-
tocratic Irish damsel in the green riding-habit;
and a kindly mention must be- made of Charles
Blake as the usual simple-minded, courageous son
of Erin, who is willing to die for everybody all
round without understanding very much about it.

Maggie Moore’s season, according to pre-
sent arrangements, will be about over by the
time this issue appears in print. William Ander-
son announces that he will return to the Lyceum
on Saturday to play the “ Orphan Heiress.” The
advt. doesn’t explain so definitely as it might
whether William is coming back for 12 nights
only, or whether the “ Heiress ” will be played
for that period ; anyhow, 12 nights is mentioned.
The “Heiress,” if this writer remembers correctly,
is something about a lady who had a large amount
of money, but as the villain put it in nis pocket
and went away, she was naturally reduced to
great straits, till the lost will being found in a dry
pump, she immediately married and had eight
children, or something to that effect.
m* * *
Popular appreciation of “Lady Hunt-
worth’s Experiment ” appears to grows as Time
rolls on, after its usual globular habit. Sydney
Royal is doing well out of Carton’s pretty
comedy, and the comedy is of the kind which
seems prettier on further inspection. The divorced
peeress who cooks for the parson under suspicious
circumstances ; and the wooden man-servant, who
has honorable intentions and indigestion ; and
bounding servant-girl Keziah, who washes up like
one in a dream, through thinking of the Lady
Ermyntrude in the last number of Home Yells,
are all interesting character studies. So is the
stunted peer who sleeps under the parson’s sink,
and who is pursued while awake bv beer-
born insects the size of a billiard-
table. Parson Brough, also, grows on the
public affections as a lovable old gentleman of
limited intelligence and a weak and fluffy mental
attitude. The stage parson is a sorrowful evi-
dence that the public doesn’t take its vicar
seriously. Very few stage vicars know enough to
come in out of the wet; naif of them, if they are
carried in by a sane parishioner, and deposited in
a dry spot and tenderly blotted, don’t know
enough not to immediately go out again and get
wetter than ever. A tendency to be found in
ridiculous situations, and to try to rush into the
soup tureen for concealment, and then to come
out with a feeble explanation that he was only
composing a hymn in the scullery, is characteristic
of the stage parson. Otherwise he is mostly a
nice, clean old gentleman who absent-mindedly
puts his text out on the front step at night and
prepares a sermon on the cat, and who drops the
Thirty-nine Articles all over the place, and leaves
the Athanasian creed in the coal-scuttle.
** * *
The ballet dancing at Sydney Palace con-
tinues to be the same gracefully grotesque per-
formance as ever, while the voices that go with it
are as small and uncertain as they used to be.
The Rickshaw dance, by Agnes Mahr and others,
is a pretty effect of color and movement. Agnes
Mahr is a filmy, gossamer-like person, with a
knack of quaint posturing. Mr. Henry Lee’s
present lot of personations do not make such a
THE MURDERED PRESIDENT.
favorable impression as his first: they include
Joseph Chamberlain, the evergreen Oom
Paul, Charles Dickens, and Mr. Gladstone. The
Musical Burglar’s gags re his costume, which is
as that of a Domain dosser, and his announcement
to the girls in the audience that the shirt-fronts
he wears are sure to stay in this season are as well
and deservedly appreciated as ever.
* # * *
The list of performers at Sydney Tivoli is
much the same as last week. Cecilia Dubois
is a recent addition to the programme, and
the Sacred Bear of India tries to look as little
like an ordinary profane bear as circumstances
will admit. He is accompanied by certain plain
bears who don’t share his holy calling, and the
difference between the consecrated bear and the
everyday penny bear is less noticeable than might
be supposed. If The Bulletin staff ever becomes
a bear in a future state it really doesn’t care two-
pence whether it is sacred or not.
* * * *
As a rule the amateur Shakesperian show
is a thing to be avoided, but there are exceptions,
and the most brilliant of the class is the periodical
appearance of Mr. F. M. Alexander and his
students in the masterpieces of the Bard who
sleeps with his fathers. The next season will
commence on the night of Tuesday next (Sept.
24th), at Sydney Criterion, and the “ Merchant of
Venice ” and “ Hamlet ” will be played. When
Mr. Alexander staged the “Merchant ” last, in the
Royal, the play was brightly mounted and brightly
played. Mr. Alexander will take the parts of
Shylock and Hamlet. He is a pleasant
sort of Shylock, and almost everybody who saw
him on his last appearance in his gaberdine and
his promissory note will be glad to renew the
acquaintance.
** * *
Sydney Empire has added to its pro-
gramme Mr. Albert Lucas, who is reported in the
advertisement to “hold people spell-bound by his
phenomenal reciting.” But there is nothing very
remarkable about the Empire show at present,
and a real star artist would probably do it a lot of
good.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), indefatigable
Wilson Barrett played two more heroes last week,
but in neither case did he add to the gaiety of this
young nation. “Quo Vadis,” which was very
effectively staged for four nights, is W. B.’s
adaptation of the novel (by Sienkiewiez) that he
had seemingly drawn upon for his “ Sign of the
Cross.” At any rate “Quo Vadis” contains
nearly all the material in -plot, characters, scenes,
incidents and moralisings needed for the building
of “The Sign.” As a melodrama for the masses
“ The Sign,” with its wealth of clap-traptious cant,
is the stronger show, but “Quo Vadis” presents
a more interestingly Roman appearance, and
deserves credit for an audacious tableau, wherein
Mario Majeroni, the barbarian, poses as vanquisher
of a stuffed bull in the arena. The bull is an over-
fed effigy, glued to a wooden stand, and the
dauntless Mario holds it down by both horns.
For this feat of strength he gets a tremendous call
before the curtain every evening. Wilson Barrett
plays a long-winded and highly authoritative
pagan in “Quo Vadis.” He is Marcus Superbus
over again, with the difference that he is not the
nobleman who gets converted by Mercia, or Lygia,
as she is here called. In a series of low-necked
robes caught up at the shoulder with tinsel
brooches, Barrett looked imposingly lady-
like, but his slow deportment and lingering utter-
ances in the limelight grew wearisome as
the play wore on. Ambrose Manning’s Caesar
Nero snarled and stormed just as though his
second name were Cregeen, whilst the ladies in
leading parts spoke their lines depressing! y. Miss
Boswell (the clever Stephanus in “ Sign of the
Cross ”) played a jealous matron with particular
success. Also, Miss Alice Crawford distinguished
herself somewhat as a lovely pagan lady of easy
virtue, who onetime persuaded Barrett to put
rings on her toes.
* * ♦ •
“The Christian King,” latest of all the
works of Divine Wilson, was produced “for the
first time on any stage” last Saturday night.
Assuming it to have been derived from some old,
forgotten drama on the subject of Alfred the
Great (an early Saxon identity who invented the
Cake Walk), one may likewise, assume that “ The
Christian King ” was written mainly by Barrett
for Barrett. No previous monarch in the history
of the stage has ruled his kingdom so conspicuously
as King Alfred Barrett ruled Engle-land
last Saturday night. The drama never ceased
to glorify his royal name. Other characters
came along and talked about Alfred behind his
back. On two occasions an apparition with a
long beard popped- up for the purpose of en-
couraging Alf. to stick to Christianity and destroy
the Danes. A sudden darkness enveloped Alf.,
nothing being visible save the head and shoulders
of the vision. Nevertheless the greatness of the
earthly King could be felt through the gloom, so
to speak, even when the Heavenly visitant was
performing a miracle—a deeply-significant bread-
and - wine miracle. Everybody knew that
Alfred’s temporary eclipse was a mere
act of courtesy on his part. Engle-land had
good cause for believing in Alfred. He knew
no fear. He carried a little harp into the
Danish camp, and twanged it at the invaders by
way of punctuating his pertinent remarks —like
Tweedlepunch, as the Welsh bard in “Florodora.”
And when the Danish King tied him to a tree and
threatened to shoot arrows at him, Alf. smiled as
George Darrell was wont to smile.
** * *
A lot of new scenery by John Gordon and
Stafford Hall gives valuable assistance to “The
Christian King,” in five acts. The plot, apart
from Alfred the Barrett, is a thing of threads and
patches, the principal patch falling to Miss
M‘Carthy. With the exception of a brief dis-
cussion (concerning Alfred) between Atholwood
and Carter-Edwards, no part of the show is very
convincing. The atmosphere of 1000 years ago is
certainly not there. A tableau, labelled ‘ ‘ Alfred’s
Defeat by the Danes,” gives one the impression
that all the Danes and Saxons have perished in a
subdued light, leaving Alfred to shine alone in
the foreground. Jehoshophat! how he do shine !
Also, Engle-land’s “ first great naval victory,”
which the hero speaks of beforehand in a prophetic
“ Rule Britannia ” strain, reveals only a little sea,
a lot of ship, and an immense amount of Alfred
Barrett the Great. And Barrett, at his best, is
not worth all that limelight.
*# * *
“Nadjezda,” at Melbourne Princess’s,
gets no better luck than any other gloomy tragedy
that is both clever and absurd. The nonsense of
“ Nadjezda ” seems to be centred in the character
of a man who wears a shock of blonde, curly hair
in the prologue and a rather thin thatch of straight
hair 15 years later, and who never attempts to
explain what kind of a fraud he is. A gentle-
manly johnnie (W. F. Grant) happens to mention
that the mysterious human chameleon aforesaid
is quite well known to the police as a professional
card-sharper of mock Nihilistic tendencies ; also
the man is being carefully watched by his idiotic
confederates, one of whom assassinates him behind
the scenes in the last act. And this cheap, lurid
impostor, so well-known to the police, and so
dimly suspected by his inexplicable confederates,
is able to keep Miss Crane up to her hereditary
Vengeance by reminding her of nine (last week’s
notice substituted “none” for nine) young men
slaughtered by the Brotherhood for breaking an
Oath. Khorvitch, the card-sharper, runs a private
saloon where American heiresses of noble
character live happily by dropping £lOOO or 1000
dollars per night at the baccarat table in payment
for their board, and the entrance to Khorvitch’s
domicile is somewhere on the first or second floor.
Anyway the Zabouroff whom Khorvitch marked
for destruction 15 years previously makes his first
appearance at the top of a fine scenic staircase,
the staircase which serves for Miss Crane’s sensa-
tional descent in the second act. The lady faints
upon the top stair, and rolls down 14 of them.
** * *
At the Royal there is a great and growing
respect for Brunton’s scenery and the bicycle race.
From a merely remarkable mechanical success the
bike race has developed into a wonderful effect.
Baker is hungry and in quest of a job when Nor-
THE LATE J. P. ABBOTT , EX-SPEAKER
OF N.S.W. ASSEMBLY.
Photo, by J. Hubert Newman, Sydney.
man, the murderer, buys the roof over his head,
and arranges to evict him unless he can pay £3 10s.
due for rent. The bicycle race is Baker’s only
chance of raising 70 shillings at one fell
swoop, but the villain has sworn to beat him if he
starts. Baker, in fact, doesn’t want to start.- He
refers feelingly to his shattered condition, and
dwells on the melancholy fact that he would have
to borrow a machine. Then Miss Ross urges
Baker to win the race on her account, and the
blind girl (Harrie Ireland) begs him to consider
his dear wife and his outstanding rent, conse-
quently Baker stokes his hollow in’ards with
rump steak and roast goose, and gets himself fit.
Albert Norman, who is rolling in wealth, makes
frantic attempts to spoil Baker’s chance by
bribery and corruption, but the conspirators
puncture the wrong tyre, and Baker achieves his
rent with flying colours. The whole world knows
that he was “Riding to Win,” in addition to
which Miss Ireland recovers her eyesight.

“ A Message from Mars ” is getting along
pleasantly at the Bijou. The tramp, as usual,
becomes a dignified, staccato drunk at the expira-
tion of his fourth whisky, and Parker continues to
nurse a natural grievance against Minnie because
she forgot to tell Bella to replenish the lamp.
And Parker’s aunt calls him ’Orace sometimes.
All things considered, it would be only a fair
thing for the Messenger to give Parker a week’s
rest, and turn his attention to auntie in the mean-
time.

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Vanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

• * * *
“ Tinkler ” : The outlook for theM.L. dredging
industry in established quarters has improved during the
past few weeks. The weather, which blocked all work
with high rivers, has settled, and the Molyneux fleet is
busy scooping shekels. For the past month returns from
60 odd dredges have totalled nearly 25000 z. per week, and
indications promise continuance well into summer. The
market, nevertheless, remains stagnant. The public is
still evidently nursing its solar plexus. The real trouble
is that most of the erstwhile speculators were of the
wage-earning class, and the pressure of calls compelled
heavy sacrifices in favor of Fatman, who now looms large
on the landscape. The latter will have a tight grip on the
industry soon. The most recent Molyneux development
is the Monte Christo 00. This was always regarded as a
cert., and proved no pan-flash. Returns of 150 oz. weekly
from the initial scoop show what the river is capable of.
Had this dredge, like all its latter-day competitors,
started fairly off scratch, the div.-list would have been a
feast for investors’ eyes. Unfortunately, in addition to
heavy financial disabilities, many dredges have found
that they cannot bottom on wash in the richest parts of
their claims owing to short bucket-ladders —an eloquent
tribute to the general fat-headedness of consulting-
engineers and directors.
■* * * »
The recent Royal Commission on opal-mining
(N.S.W.) recommended Govt, to buy out the mainly-
English co. owning the principal blocks ; but it is stated,
on first-class authority, that the co. “ isn’t taking any.”
Instead, it will most likely cease letting ground on tribute,
and charge each miner hialf-a-crown a week for the right
to mine. That way it will make precious little ; what the
commission recommended is the best chance ever likely
to come its way. At present more opal is being got on
what used to tie called the “ outside ” blocks than on the
co.’s claims. The price for good gem is again fair ; for
poorer stuff, wretched.

The Wild Cat Column.
It now appears that the N.S.W. £4,000,000 loan
was issued at a fixed price of £94. It was underwritten,
and the usual price for underwriting is about £1 ss. per
cent., so with this and all expenses, the net yield can’t
well be more than £92 —probably it will be a little less.
During the week Westralia has offered a £1,000,000 3 per
cent, loan at £9l. It is also underwritten ; and the under-
writing charges and expenses will almost certainly bring
down the yield to somewhere between £BB and £B9.
Taking the most favorable view of things, these are about
the results :
Debt Cash Tog-
incurred. realised.
N.S.W £4,000,000 £3,680,000 £320,000
Westralia .. 1,000,000 890,000 110,000
£5,000,000 £4,570,000 £430,000
Considering that this loss is really interest paid out of
the loans themselves—that it arises through floating loans
at 3 per cent, when the public credit is only good enough
to enable the country to borrow at 3J per cent., the only
honest course is to create a sinking fund for each loan
which will make good the loss by the time the loan falls
due. Even if the money is all represented by permanent
payable assets, which it won’t be, it is a mean swindle to
hand down to posterity £4,570,000 worth of property with
a £5,000,000 mortgage on it, the difference representing
the fact that the present generation was too lazy and
shiftless and dishonest to pay the full interest on its own
liabilities. The local Fat Man has a lot to say about the
sinfulness of compulsory purchase of his property by the
State at its full value. It is time he said something about
his own compulsory sale to his children of property at a
great deal more than its value. It is also about time that
a great movement was organised to get the signatures of
the coming voter—that part of the community (say
between 15 and 21) which is old enough to understand the
situation, but not old enough to vote—to a huge memo-
rial intimating to the British money-lender that when he
comes of age he will decline to be responsible for the old
man’s debts.
Tasmania is issuing a little local loan of
£200,000, the buyer having the right to take 3J per cent,
stock, repayable within five years, or 3£ per cent, stock at
a longer date. These three loans, and the conversion of
Queensland’s £528,000 deficit into permanent debt, make
nearly £5,800,000 added to the Commonwealth’s debt in
about a fortnight—say an addition of £1 10s. per in-
habitant all over the Commonwealth. Things are pretty
sudden in this countrv.
* • » •
A clause whioh the Senate has added to the
Property Acquisition Bill is absolutely the wisest thing
the Federal Parliament has done up to date. It is esti-
imated that the Commonwealth has to take over from the
: States property valued at £10,445,000. Most of this con-
s sists of buildings and works which the States constructed
with borrowed money, and the most common idea was
i that the Commonwealth should borrow more money
and pay the States for them. And the States
i mostly proposed not to use the money to pay off
i the original debt, but to use it to cover revenue defi-
■ ciencies and the like. The idea was that where the State
; had borrowed £lO,OOO to put up a building, the Federal
• Government should borrow another £lO,OOO to buy the
i building off the State ; and the State should use the
! second £lO,OOO as ordinary revenue—consequently there
would be £20,000 worth of debt, and one building,
■ originally worth £lO,OOO but now considerably dete-
riorated by age, to show forjit. The new clause provides,
ito some extent, a remedy for this swindle. The idea is
i to credit each State with the value of the properties it
] hands over to the Commonwealth, and then debit it, so
: far as circumstances will admit, with the amount it should
■ contribute, in proportion to population, to the Federal
fund, out of which these assets are to be paid for. The
iidea works out thus :
Less £2 7s. 2d.
Due by Com- per head paid by Balance
monwealth to all the people due to
State for assets of Australia to- each
handed over. wards purchase State,
of these assets.

It will take years to convince a certain class of
'local politician, who expected to have a great razzle-
. dazzle with the money received for post-offices, tele-
graphs, customs-house buildings, &c., that this isn’t a
: grievous swindle of some kind, yet it is perfectly fair and
, honest.

The £103,500 which the Commercial Bank of Tasmania
! has written off during the past four years consists of
£90,000 towards general losses and depreciation by way
• of a cleaning up after the boom ; £B5OO written off the
i book value of premises ; and £5OOO this half-year to re-
• duce the bank’s holding of British Government securities
i to £94, being about the value to which they have dropped
iby reason of the Boer war calamity. Considering that
r they are practically certain to reach par again, nine insti-
r tutions out of ten would have let them stay at the old
i book value, and the conduct of the Commercial in being
s the tenth institution—the one which faced and provided
t for the drop— is all the more commendable.
.The profits are equal to £ll Is. 2d. per cent,
i per annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning
■ of the half-year (capital, £141,492; reserves, £102,564;
: total, £244,056). This is a splendid figure, and a remark-
: able improvement on the £6 35.4 d. per cent, of six months
; back. Reserves consist of reserve fund £lOO,OOO, and un-
< divided profit, £5llB. It would have been much better
i if £5OOO of this latter was transferred to the permanent
i reserve, as a guarantee of the management’s intention to
] repair the loss of 1899 as speedily as possible. The bank
1 has £410,221 in cash and Government securities—equal to
•29 per cent, of all liabilities to the public. Of this amount
£184,880 (the entire reserve and more than half the
capital) is in Government securities. Altogether the
Commercial Bank of Tasmania makes an exceedingly good
showing in every respect,

The Castlemaine Brewery and Quinlan, Gray
and Co., Brisbane, Ltd., is a large Queensland brewing
concern in difficulties. The difficulties are diminishing,
but the balance-sheet is so confusedly drawn up that it
is impossible to say exactly either how much they are
now or how much they were before. It was proposed
some four years ago to extinguish about half the paid-up
capital of £154,262 in order to write off depreciation and
the paper item of goodwill. This hasn’t yet been done,
and there is nothing to show the amount that has to be
provided under either heading. The largest alleged asset
appears as “ Freehold properties, buildings, machmery,
plant, goodwill and rolling-stock, &c., £141,447,” but
whether the paper item of goodwill represents £lOOO or
£50,000 of that there is nothing to show. The co., how-
ever, presents its accounts for the last four years thus :
Nominal
Profits. How disposed of. Reserves.
July, 1898 £5,965
„ 1899 8,614 Written off = £5,854 11,351
„ 1900 10,981 „ „ 7,757 14,575
„ 1901 9,537 „ „ 10,184 13,928
Prospects certainly look better, though the earnings are
only equal to £6 3s. Bd. per cent, per annum on capital.
If no extensive new losses were made in the last three
years, and the £23,795 written off during that period all
went in reduction of the losses mentioned in 1898, things
must have improved very materially. At July, 1893, the
liabilities, apart from capital, were £141,580 —mostly bank
overdrafts. They are now only £107,003, so a good deal
has been done at lifting the white man’s burden off the
big brewery. How much depreciation and goodwill there
are still to be written off is pure conjecture, and in any
case no dividends can be honestly and justifiably paid till
the overdraft (still nearly £62,000) and nearly £13,000
still owing of the original purchase money, are cleared
off ; but with a little more patience there should be no
necessity for writing down capital. If the writing down
can be averted it will be a good thing, if only to save
much good beer going in bad law expenses.

In 1892 the Melbourne Permanent Building Society owed
£473,752 to debenture-holders and other outside creditors.
The burden is now reduced to £223,722. Its principal
assets consist of £371,616 worth of foreclosed properties
(vacant land £54,072, and houses £317,544). The house
property now yields £2 14s. Id. per cent, net rental,
against only £2 Bs. 2d. per cent, last year, so it is becom-
ing within the bounds of possibility that it is nearly
worth its book value, and that there are no very serious
losses still to provide for. The earnings of the business
(£3423) are equal to barely 2 per cent, on the paid-up
capital of £177,164, which is the best return shown since
1892. At the same time, despite the improvement, any-
thing material in the shape of a dividend appears to be a
long way off yet. Also the load of borrowed money
(£223,722) is still absurdly heavy in proportion to capital,
and all the earnings should be applied towards it reduc-
tion for some time yet.

The Queen Building Society suffers from the usual trouble.
Out of £36,544 of assets (purely book values) £29,666 con-
sists of foreclosed properties. These are being gradually
realised upon, and as they don’t fetch their paper value

tors and others have been reduced from £15,947 to about
£9OOO ; and when the balance is cleared off, and it is no
longer necessary to force the sale of properties, the Queen
should have a chance to earn respectable profits again.

The Albert Building Society has a paid-up capital of
£58,‘474, so even the greatly increased profits shown this
year only represent £1 4s. 4d. per cent, per annum on
capital, or £1 os. lOd. on the capital and reserves. Under
these circumstances it is impossible to take the present
alleged values seriously. To make the Albert a 5
per cent, dividend-earning institution on the basis of last
year’s profits it would be necessary to write off all the re-
serves and £44,234 of capital, and leave only £14,240 paid
up. But the outside liabilities are only £6060 (mortgages
£5347; deposits and sundries £213), so it isn’t compelled
to sacrifice its properties in a debilitated market to satisfy
depositors, and the values and profits are both going up
again. In the course of four or five years the depreciation
to be provided for may be much less, and the reserves
may have grown large enough to fill the gap. In an., case
the Albert’s position is much stronger than that of a num-
ber of kindred institutions.

ment Society (Melbourne) still makes rather heavy

Though the ordinary business shows better returns this
year than at any other time since the date of the historic
smash, the constant disposal of great masses of property
in a market which, despite its great improvement, is
still a good way below the boom level, keeps adding to
the deficit. The progress of sales has been as follows :
1899 .. £57,849 worth, at 14/7 in the £of book value
1900 .. 84,545 „ 18/10 „
1901 .. 109,347 „ 18/4 „ „
Despite these sacrifices, the process of paying off the
liabilities is not very rapid. At July, 1897, the Modern
Permanent Building and Investment Society owed
£464,803 in deposits, debentures, mortgages and over-
drafts. The liability is now down to £394,868. If the co.
could lie low for awhile and sell no more property till the
market further improved, it might be better for it; un-
fortunately there is no positive guarantee that the
market will further improve. Out of £198,897 paid-up
capital, £187,718 is still intact (on paper, at all events),
and on present appearances a good part of this should be
saved, with moderate luck and careful management.
By way of a side issue: In 1897, the Modern
Permanent B. and I Society ceased to insure its multitude
of scattered properties, and, instead, paid the premiums
into a fire insurance fund of its own. As a result, after
covering all losses, the balance at credit of the fund was
£l2O in 1898 ; £425 in 1899 ; £6BO in 1900 ; and is now
£879. This is a good deal to save in four years. It has
been proposed that the Federal Government should also
be its own fire insurance co. If the idea is adopted, and
adhered to for ten or twelve centuries, the Federal fire
insurance fund might, at the end of that time, go a long
way to pay off Australia’s public debt.

The Malting Co. of New South Wales, a small
concern with a paid-up capital of £21,050, issued its third
annual balance-sheet as at 30th June, 1901. Its works
have been in actual operation, however, for rather less
than two years. Position of the co. is as follows :
Loss on . Preliminary Visible
Business. Expenses. Deficit.
June, 1899 £741 £741
„ 1900 £672 63 1,476
Profit on
Business.
„ 1901 £720 756
This isn’t the whole deficit, apparently, though it is all
that can be definitely traced. The principal asset is des-
cribed as “ Land, building, plant, machinery and goodwill,
£16,996.” How much of this is goodwill doesn’t appear ;
it may be £lO or it may be £5OOO for all the evidence
shows ; but whatever the sum it is a purely paper asset
and should be added to the deficit and written off as soon
as possible. The goodwill item appeared at the very
beginning before the co. had ever started work, and it
would be interesting to know the cause of its existence.
It would also be interesting to know the amount. It
would be additionally interesting to know how the public
would regard, say, the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney
if it put among its assets “ Goodwill, £500,000 ” —though
its business would certainly sell for that much over and
above the visible assets. The fact that nobody can
imagine a reputable institution like the Commercial
Banking Co. calling its goodwill an asset is a pretty good
proof that such an asset can’t be taken seriously.
Especially the goodwill of a new co. which is earning, as
yet, less than 3£ per cent., can’t be taken seriously.

Solgoz—it is simpler to spell him so, and
makes no difference—seems to have been a weak
man driven to the wall, and astonishing the
boldest by his cruel deed. Cabled that his
friends, even his mother, taunted him continually
with, “You’re a coward ! you’re a coward !” So
the dripping of his wife wore out our own Jimmy
Governor’s patience. And Solgoz, casting about
for the most conclusive proof of courage, picked
the biggest man he could find, and killed him.
The unfortunate President’s death needs no
anarchists’ plot to explain it; it may quite ration-
ally have been caused by the desperation of a
sheep.
* * * 9
The cable-operators did some smart work
during the MVKinley assassination sensation. The
message announcing the attempt came through in
130 minutes, while that telling of the death took
a few minutes longer, making record performances
for ordinary cable transmission.

One can only judge of the depths of inde-

•* * * »
A grim joke was played on unsuspecting
Sydney on Sept. 13th by the fire at the Ultimo
power-house, which caused the complete cessation
of the electric train traffic. Though only a few
months old, the city has become so accustomed to
the electric service that the break-down threw it
on its beam ends. Picture the feelings of the
young man who had taken his Leichhardt girl to
the theatre, and he himself living at Dulwich
Hill! The demand for cabs soon exhausted the
supply, and nothing remained to hundreds of
persons but a weary trudge home. Crowds of
swearing men and disconsolate women could be
seen up Red fern way with boxes, bundles and babies,
landed, as usual, miles from anywhere by the late
trains, with the prospect before them of getting
their respective caravans home on foot. To make
matters worse a large ball had been held that
night in the Town Hall, and scores of weary
dancers said things as they plodded homewards.
Tramway Supt. Kneeshaw was on his way to
Broken Hill, but was intercepted at Moss Vale by
a message telling him that Sydney could not get
home to bed, and asking him what he was going to
do about it. Many a tired soul that night sighed
for the despised steam tram—the large, dirty,
roaring vehicle which, with all its faults, generally
got its load safely home somehow or other. There
were many of them lying half-dismantled in the
sheds, and by great efforts some 30 of the old
ruins were on the rails again next morning, and in
a sort of way the traffic was kept going.
• * • *
The railway accident at Princes Bridge,
Melb., on Sept. 13, resembles the Redfern (Sydney)
disaster of a few years back. On that occasion,
the 9 a.m. Goulburn mail collided with an incom-
ing suburban passenger train near Eveleigh
tunnel. The driver on the outgoing train swore
positively that the signal to start was given him.
The signal-man was equally positive that it wasn’t,
and the “interlocking-gear” proved him to be
right. For months together the driver had watched
for 9 a.m., had heard the
station bell, seen the guard’s
flag, had looked up and seen
the arm of the semaphore drop,
and right away ! The co-relation
had become fixed in his mind. Gn that
fatal morning, part of the programme was gone
through as usual, but the incoming passenger was
a few seconds late, and the arm did not drop,
though the driver honestly swore that it did. The
driver of the outgoing train at Princes Bridge is
also sure that he got the signal. But the signal-
man again has the interlocking gear to bear him
out. The accident again shows that no human
device is sufficient to guard absolutely against ac-
cidents.
* • * *
The spectacle of a foot constable and a
trooper escorting a tradesman, his vans and his
takings, to his suburban home, was witnessed on
Saturday week in Melbourne. Mr. Straker, caterer
at the Agricultural Show Grounds, declared that he
would not take the risk of removing his gear and
money to his hotel at Glenferrie unless he had an
escort of police. He wrote to the Chief Com-
missioner :—
A tram has been stuck up by masked highwaymen on
the very track I will have to traverse. I will have
probably £4OO or £SOO of Saturday’s takings, and I will
not take any risks while there are so many desperate
ruffians knocking about. Please let me have an armed
foot constable to sit in the buggy with me to protect me
and my money, and let me also have a mounted man to
bring up the rear with my vans, carrying plant and stock.
Let me know what it will cost.
The caterer’s wish was gratified at the expense of
305., which he gladly paid to enjoy immunity
from attack by armed ruffians. This incident
speaks highly for the local police, and for the
Morality of Melbourne.

When the big Sydney brewing people pro-
tested recently before the Parliamentary inquiry
commission that they positively panted' after the
abolition of the “tied” public-house system, the
other eye was solemnly winked by everyone save
the guileless person who can be made to believe
that Shylock is grieved to the soul to be obliged
to charge such high rates of interest. But nobody
was prepared for the bombshell which publicans’
secretary Monfries exploded in the enemy’s camp
by calmly handing in a communication in which
leading Sydney brewing firms informed him, in so
many words, that one of their chief causes of com-
plaint against his Licensed Victuallers’ Associ-
ation was its opposition to the tied-house system !
Good old Black-and-white ! how often he bobs up
to biff slippery people in the eye.
The decrease in Australian crime, or rather
Australian criminal convictions, about which our
statists boast, seems really due to the increasing
inability of the Australian police to cope with
their duties. All our great cities are wretchedly
policed. In England the rule is one policeman to
every 400 population ; in Melbourne the propor-
tion is one to 650. Sydney is a little better off
than Melbourne in this respect, but there, too,
the force is shockingly undermanned, and is con-
stantly being loaded with new responsibilities,
* * * *
The Sydney suburbs are disgracefully
urder-policed. For instance, Waverley, with 75
miles of streets, is looked after by only 14 men—
two of whom have to watch the 35 miles of streets
included in the Bondi sub-division—i.e.,17J miles
per man for half a day ; during the other half the
place is entirely wn-policed. Yet Premier See
appears to deprecate prospective Parliamentary
opposition to the expenditure of the “ few hun-
dreds ” necessary for an increase of 53 men. A
miserable half-hundred !—when the force—by
reason of the numerous extra-constabulary duties
with, which it has become saddled—practically
requires doubling.
* * * *
A ghostly glimmer is thrown on the mys-
tery of many a sudden disappearance by the
discovery, in a Warwick (Q.) graveyard, of a
corpse, uncoffined, but dressed, booted and spurred..
There was no record of its lawful burial. The
really great embezzler generally plunges into a
city and lives secure by the side of a police-station ;
the artistic murderer has probably long been
practising the art of perfect corpse-concealment
by planting his victims in the cemetery—in
new-made graves. Butler’s dead were delved out
from their remote mountain-gullies, but who
would ever look for a murdered body in a grave-
yard ?
* * * *
Five returned Ballarat cohentingenters
went back to S’Africa t’other day to go to the
front again. The local Courier (whose editor is
a “Kunnel”) mournfully observes: “It is not a
creditable thing to have to say that, in every case,
inability to obtain employment at their former
work is the cause of their return.” Now, this
same Courier was one of the loudest in egging-on
these unfortunate nincompoops to throw up their
billets and go where Glory awaited them. Alas !
for the average returned Australian swaddy.
Billetless, penniless, and in many cases jilted by
the girl who preferred substance to shadow—
he points a powerful moral to future Australian
would-be swashbucklers.

A Melbourne correspondent :—
The capture of Pentridge escapee O’Connor at Sydney
has shed some light upon the methods adopted by the
Vic. d’s. to catch criminals. O’Connor’s wife lives with
her child in a little Collingwood cottage. As soon as
news reached the detective office of the escape of Sparks
and O’Connor, one detective burst out: “ I know where
O’Connor’s wife is living, sir.” Inspector Sharp looked
upon this man with admiration : “If you are sure of it,
take four men with you, surround the place and rush it,
for they are almost sure to be there.” But the old hands
in the office muttered “Rot!” The four young detec-
tives went helter-skelter for Mrs. O’Connor’s house and
surrounded it. A loud knock at the door, cocked
revolvers front and rear of the tenement, and all was
ready to receive O’Connor and Sparks. Mrs. O’Connor
■came to the door, with her sleeves tucked up,
her arms all over soap-suds. “My God!” she ex-
claimed, as she beheld the armed men, “ I am
only a poor washerwoman, and I have got nothing
worth stealing.” “ Hold your tongue,” said the leader
as he sounded a whistle, the signal for the two at the
hack to jump the fence. But the back gate was only
latched, and in they rushed, tumbling in their hurry over
wash-tubs and clothes-props. Then they stormed the
kitchen and went through the house like greased light-

made. Meanwhile the woman looked on in horror, and:
the escapee’s youngster bawled with fright. “ Now,” re-

your husband and his mate ? ” “ They are in Pentridge,”
gasped the woman. “You know they have escaped !”'

“ J.R. ” : Some recent Bulletin pars ap-
pear to have assumed that N.S.W. old age pen-
sions have been given to inmates of Govt, asylums..
None have, as yet, been given to those people,
most of whom are ineligible by reason of the rule ■
that no claim for pension shall be .considered;
unless claimant has been at least a month out of
an asylum, And in any case inmates of asylums
have no opportunity to hunt up evidence in sup-
port of their claims.

Simultaneously conferences of N.5.W...
glassworkers and bootmakers, masters and men,
are in progress. Former are asking for higher
pay and shorter honrs; latter for better terms ■
generally, including the framing of a new log for
all piece-work, and abolition of the severe task,
set for weekly hands. Workers in the N.S.W.
cold stores and freezing chambers allege that they
are sweated ; boiler and fire-hands on shore make •
a similar complaint. Former have formed, and ;
latter are about to start, a union.

* * * *
Two curious recoveries. About two years:
ago a Queensland jockey had his spine fractured,.
lost the use of his legs, and had to be wheeled!
about in a pram. Six months ago the pram was;
capsized, and the man’s spine again fractured..
Strange to say, the accident restored the use of.'
his legs, and he was turned out of hospital almost,
as good as new. Again, one Cooper, a sailor, lost
his speech and hearing through a donkey-engine
accident. Five years afterwards he fainted and!
fell heavily to the floor, and blood flowed freely
from his mouth and ears. On recovering con-
sciousness he found he could again speak and
hear.

obscenities.
A FUNERAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The coffin and the red jaws of the grave,
The silent group bareheaded in the sun,
Far off the moaning of a broken wave—
So her long rest was won.
The deeps of death are pangless and serene—
Foolish it were to sorrow for her sake,
Since Life, since Love to her could only mean
The right to burn and ache
With hungering bosom striving to retain
Large dreams of joy that never might abide—
Melting like clouds above a drought-scorched plain
Agape, unsatisfied.
To one so weary, welcome was the sleep
Dreamless and sound—the falling into dark
As down the calm abysses of the deep
Falls slow the foundered bark.

Big Sydney brewer- Toohey, M.L.C.,
“ believes that Sunday trading would be greatly
reduced by opening hotels on the Lord’s Day for
an hour at noon and evening.” No; what the
great Australian Thirst demands is the oppor-
tunity to shear a hair of the dog from 6 a.m. to 11
p.m. on Sunday.

Why not a N.S.W. Parliamentary Com-
mittee on tied chemists’ shops ? Scores of
chemists are tied to big drug firms, and this
materially decreases the chances of the unhappy
citizen who, poisoned into madness by rotten
liquor, has to try and recover on —well, you may
be sure that the “tied” chemist doesn’t sell the
best or freshest drugs.

Kitchener’s last weekly tally : 67 Boers
killed ; 61 wounded. Usually in warfare the
wounded hugely outnumber the killed. This
excess of killed over wounded tells its own story.

The suicide is reported of returned-con-
tingenter Pooley —making the third returned-
soldier suicide recorded to date. After rioting for
months in blood and burning, the swaddy
can seldom settle down to peaceable citizen-
ship again Two of the three suicides in question
were ex-policemen.

Very many Sydney suburban shopkeepers
ignore the Early Closing Act, by compelling their
employees to “work back ” On Friday nights from
7 till 9, weighing-out, making-up, and getting
deliveries ready for Saturday. This gross viola-
tion of a wise law wants stamping out.
The post-office committee of N.S.W.
Assembly, as the result of its investigations, finds
that Minister Crick used a postal official for a
curious bit of detective work, and “ considers the
proceeding wrong.” The main result of the
inquiry is to definitely connect Father Kenny,
recently of the Sacred Heart church, Darlmg-
hurst, with the eternal Coningham case.
• * * *
A complicated Turkishness in domestic
life was that of one Andrew, defendant in an
Adelaide maintenance case. In 1871 he married
Keturah Templer. In January, 1876, he took
to the altar Annie Leggo ; later in that year he
was divorced from Keturah. In June, 1899, he
casually wedded Mary Gilbert. Annie, of whom
he has leggo, now wants maintenance as a deserted
wife, but Edward (who calmly pleaded his own
bigamy) was upheld by the law in a contention
that she had no claim.
* * * *
The Old Colonists’ Association (Melb.),
founded by Geo. Coppin in 1862, is getting to be
quite a large charity. The endowment and main-
tenance funds amount to £17,000, and G.C. is
now trying to raise another £l5OO to supplement a
recent bequest of £3OOO for the purpose of adding
44 cottages to the homes already erected. Four of
these are to be reserved for old actors, in considera-
tion of the Dramatic Fund management, which
has a few cottages hard by, giving up four acres
of spare land to the Old Colonists’ Assn. A few
years hence, when the new homes are gardened
and tree-shaded like the old ones, the property
will be a pretty little township, and a notable
memorial to Coppin.

Sometimes truth is not stranger
than fiction, but reality successfully con-
tested supremacy with romance in an Aus-
tralian soft-goods warehouse, recently. A
young man, newly arrived from London,
presented himself to the manager of a de-
partment and applied for a situation. His
manners pleased, and his testimonials satis-
fied the manager, who took him on. The
employees remarked how much the new
arrival resembled the boss in everything
but age, and the resemblance was empha-
sised by the fact that both bore the same
name. These remarks reached the ears of
the manager, and lie questioned the 1
assistant. It was then found that they
were father and son. Nearly 30 years
previously the father, who was a com-
mercial traveller, had doubted the paternity
of his son, and had successfully petitioned
for a divorce. The father came to Aus-
tralia; and the mother, who never married
again, had reared the son to become, long
years afterwards, the living proof of her
innocence.
Flanking the entrance to the Vic-
toria Barracks, Melbourne, are two big
smooth-bore cannon, captured from the
Russians at Inkerman—one bearing the
mark of a British shell which burst on per-
cussion. A few years ago, when a Rus-
sian man-o’-war visited Melbourne, the then
Minister of Defence, with an extraordinary
idea of the fitness of things, recalled the
circumstances of their capture by offering
the guns back to the foreign admiral as
a delicate testimony to the existing peaceful
relations between Britain and Russia. The
Muscovite, of course, indignantly refused the left-
handed compliment.

“ Ebenezer ” : The frivols of pious society.
Attended, recently, a “social” in a certain Sydney
suburban Presbyterian kirk, and, turning my
attention to some “conversation-lollies” lying on
the table, I nearly had a fit when I struck the fol-
lowing legend:
If by asking I may wed you,
Gladly then I’d board and bed you.
I called the pastor’s attention to the matter, but
the old man only smiled and said, “Weel, I
never ! You must ha’ brought it with you ! ”
“F.” : The Christian Science fad is
not altogether- new in Australia. The
craze paid us a visit some 15 years ago in
the guise of Faith-healing. Theapostle
was one Woods, who made his head-
quarters, and many converts, in Ade-
laide. Dowie, now of Zion, U.S.A., was,
I think, a disciple. A church built by
the sect in suburban Hindmarsh now
stands as a monument of the forsaken
faith, for, with the death of the chief
adherents, the rest returned to wallow
in the patent medicines of their particu-
lar fancies. It was a humorless cult,
anyhow, that solemnly prayed for and
anointed a lame horse, and pronounced
it cured—though to ungodly eyes the
crock went as lame as ever. And it was
a cruel cult also, in that the children
suffered for the “ faith ” of their fathers.
I knew one case in which an infant fell
into a fire and was severely burned ; yet
the parents only prayed for it, doing
absolutely nothing to. alleviate the hor-
rible pain or cure the raw sores that
formed. The helpless mite had to endure
* months of agony that might have been
shortened to weeks by the use of proper
remedies. But, of course, its ultimate
recovery was cited as proof of the efficacy
of faith. The Children’s Protection
Societies will do well to keep a piercing
eye on the “craze” if it again reaches
Australia. Woods, the apostle, returned
to England, where, according to evi-
dence subsequently given in a Police-
court case, he posed as Solomon, and
had women dancing round his thror
(set up in his church).
Here is a story of the courtesy shown by
the Federal Government to the Victorian Govt.
The Victorian Observatory had the use of a small
room at Parliament House for the purposes of
cloud photography. It was in an out-of-the-way
corner and in nobody’s road. This room had been
used by the Observatory for years past. A few
weeks after the Federal Govt, took possession of
Parliament Houses, the officer concerned witli
cloud photography went to the room as usual, but
found, to his dismay, that it had been turned into
a private lavatory for the convenience of a very
high official ; although the general lavatories were
but a few yards further down the corridor. On
making a search the Observatory officer discovered
that all the apparatus from the room had been
thrown into a confused heap in another corner of
the building, some of the instruments being seri-
ously damaged.
* • * *
The foreigner who was fined ss. for kissing
his wife in a Sydney street recalls a Melbourne
case of some years ago. A young Clifton Hill
butcher, delivering meat to a pretty young mar-
ried woman, was so affected by her beauty that he
seized her in his arms and planted several linger-
ing kisses on her lips. The lady kicked vigorously,
and, as soon as she struggled free of the amorous
meat-man, went to the local police-station and got
out a summons for assault. The butcher threw
himself on the mercy of the magistrates when the
case came on, and the elderly Bench gloated over
the lovely plaintiff as long as the business of the
court could with decency be delayed, and then fined
defendant £2. The unusual character of the case
led to its being reported in full in the dailies, and
through this the butcher, who had been unsuccess-
fully sought for years, was found by solicitors who
had £3OOO awaiting him as heir of a forgotten
relative. Moral —

Concerning “ the cost of a baby ” :
Dear Bulletin, —“ A Father in Israel ” must be offen-
sively rich—or very unlucky in the choice of his doctors.
There is said to be a Robin Hood fashion in the medical
profession of robbing the rich so as to treat the poor for
nothing, and certainly doctors grade their fees by the
purses of the paj’ers. A “middle-class” father, my ex-
perience has been : Baby No. 1, medical and nursing
expenses, all told, under £lO ; baby No. 2 (services of one
of the best gynaecologist specialists included), £25. Nine-
tenths of the little strangers are not worth more than a
few guineas to the doctor. On the other point. The
great trouble with the city mother is to cope with the
dirt which taints houses, clothes, and atmosphere.
Septic infection in child-birth is the danger, and that the
modern scientific nurse knows, and the old Sairey Gamp
type does not know, how to avoid. I should advise “a
father in I ’’ to, next time, consult a good doctor as to
"the choice of the nurse, and he will have nothing to com-
plain of.— Sydney.
THE QUEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sea is my bath,
And my towel the sun ;
I dive and I swim
Where the white waters run,
And, tiring, I lie and grow dry in the sun.
There’s another that shares
The waters with me ;
He glides through the deep,
A grey gleam iD the sea,
But his teeth, ah ! his teeth are a menace to me.
The flowers and the trees
Where I wander at whiles,
They are mine, and I deign
To requite them with smiles—
Requite them the pleasure they give me at whiles.
There’s another who walks
My garden so fair ;
I was king, but she came,
And, ere I was aware,
She was queen over me and my garden so fair.
She is warm . . . she is cold . . .
What is this that I seek ?
O Heart, let me tell!
O Soul, let me speak 1
0 Girl, it is love, it is love that I seek !

The death of William M’Kinley, President
of the U. 9., was only what the public, in its secret
heart, fully expected. Abdominal wounds are
almost always mortal, and the bulletins pub-
lished about rulers who lie at the gates of death
are invariably optimistic to the last. M’Kinley
was a man whose private life was without a stain
and he was also intellectually far above the ruck
of rulers. He made a great name in politics before
he reached the dreary but glorious eminence of
the Presidency, and the regret at his slaughter is
something very different from the perfunctory
lamentation that goes with such occasions. No-
body dreams for a moment that any personal
animosity led to his murder; it is merely that
lightning has a tendency to strike the highest
object, and the homicidal lunatic is just like
lightning in this respect. M’Kinley makes the
third U. 9. President murdered inside 36 years.
Three murders out of eight men elected to the
Presidency is an appalling average, but the U. 9.
President is the least guarded of all great rulers,
and the fool-anarchist has a habit of taking the
line of least resistance. It is difficult to remember
any other civilised country that has lost three
rulers by assassination in so short a period. At
the same time it is certain that an epidemic of
assassination has set in all over the white man’s
world of late years, and rulers and royalties have
been falling very fast. Two of the United 9tates’
murdered Presidents, Lincoln and M’Kinley, were
men of special mental capacity, and Garfield was
reputed to be so also. And the intermediate
mediocrities Hayes, Arthur, Cleveland and
Harrison—bore charmed lives.
The Right-Thinking Woman—and she is
quite plentiful these enlightened times —is domin-
ated by a desire to always look her best. The
happiness of maid or matron who is aware that
her beauty is adorned to its best advantage shines
in her face, and sheds the light of joy upon all her
surroundings. Thus is the P.D. corset such a
benefactor to both sexes. The P.D. figure bestows
upon its happy possessor the divinest joy under
heaven, and thus indirectly inspires most of the
good works of her admirer—man.*
Summer seems to be swooping down like
a sea of fire upon this continent, and what to
drink and how to drink it becomes the most im-
portant scientific problem of the hour. A sound
rule is to depend more on the quality of one’s
liquid than the quantity. D.C.L. is as safe as
that rule is sound ; taken at proper intervals and
in proper proportions, it quenches the thirst
whilst fortifying the imbiber’s frame against heat-
exhaustion. It gives good value for one’s bit of
“silber,” and never eventuates in “hot coppers.”
