A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, September 17, 1901.
My Dear Abini,—
Never died a spring so young as this first spring
of the Twentieth Century. It lived a week, no more.
Summer that transformed the gardens into a blaze
of forced blooms and smote the snowdrops out of
life has also forced us direct from tailor-mades into
muslins. Our faces already have that red, moist
look that is associated with mid-summer. “What
god is this imperial Heat ? ” exclaims an American
poet. We, grieving over our week-old dead spring,
sagging collars and prospect of eight-months long
laundry bills respond, “ Rats ! ”
Recessional: The tumult and the shouting at
Randwick have died. Our visitors, men, women
and horses, have departed. The ball-room lights
are out. The waltz-music is stilled. Last Satur-
day saw the end of the race-week gaieties. Not
much difference in the dressing at Randwick.
The Viotorian baronet’s wife was generally voted
the smartest-looking woman. Her white, frilly,
gauzy gown and floral toque were not better than
many other toilettes but they were worn with an
“air.” This “ air ”is an intangible, exasperating
charm. Scores of women think they can acquire
it, but you can no more acquire it than you can
the charm of voice and smile. To know how to
wear your clothes ! What is the immortality of
the soul compared with that problem ?
Miss Darley and the Madden visitors looked
down from the Vice-regal box with the impassive
faces of persons accustomed to be stared at. It
must come hard on those who acquire the right to
be stared at unexpectedly or late in life —I don’t
mean the staring you get by right (or wrong) of
notoriety, but that which comes to you by way of
fame, as for instance, if you are a mayor’s or a
simple legislator’s wife, and suddenly become a
lady by patent. I have seen such a one front the
crowd on her reception day for the first time, a
patented lady, and her eyes and mouth worked
like those milk-shake machines that are now
jerking all over this sun-struck city. A trinity of
captains, Watt, Vallange and Darley, helped to
furnish the box. Two of them looked as if they’d
grow demoralised and crawl under the bed from
the enemy—a regiment of him. The third —find
the missing identity—looked truculent enough.
Skirts were worn so long all round that they
and their wearers were in mutual danger. To
avoid treading on your own miles of chiffon
flounces you must assume a curious, cat-like,
padding walk. Quite a different pace to the
Sydney walk, referred to contemptuously by other-
Staters. The unevenness of our pavements, every
flag set as if the setter was drunk in 20 different
ways whilst setting it, is responsible for the Svdney
walk, I think.
Earrings were plentnul. Lady Clarke wore long
ones, regular jaw-smackers. Very good-com-
plexioned women went in for turquoise or pink
coral. Mrs. Norman Menzies (Dorothy Vane)
wears the latter set in hoops of gold, but her ears
are not pierced—the rings just clip her lobes most
cunningly. “La Poupee” Dorothy, by the way,
looked radiant, bright-eyed, almost chubby, m
white silk, a sea of chiffon, and a big pink hat.
She and adoring hubby are now staying at the
National Park. Mrs. Bracy has been so long used
to wearing long earrings that she actually doesn’t
feel draped without them.
Tulle boas —there were a few, —are immensely
becoming but, of course, very perishable. They
stand right up almost to the brim of the hat and
quite frame the face which (if it’s very young and
cherubic) looks like those cherub faces framed in
clouds.
Bags fashioned of gold or silver beads or fancy
leather clipped to a waist-belt proxied the long-
lost lamented pocket, and that tricky little fashion-
let introduced by Grace Palotta of wearing a paste
buckle or star on a rosette of black velvet or
chiffon was copied by a few girls.
Such a smart-looking audience at Her Majesty’s
on Saturday night to see Lucia and her Edgardo
die for love of each other. It was the first hot
night, so the circle brought its fans—huge fans,
some, but mostly funny little ones no bigger than
a pigeon’s wing. I lost a bit of the fine opera
through a neighbor springing a surprise on me
and a comb on herself from a stick of her fan.
She furtively combed up the back-hair displaced
by someone’s fanning, then pressed a spring and
the comb disappeared.
A few gowns that had debuted the night pre-
viously, at the Union Club ball, glorified the
circle—one (a hand-painted chiffon) you’d give
your eyelashes for.
The Milan harpist whose playing makes you
think of the slow drip of golden rain, has infected
some who have heard her. They are learning the
harp, or intend to learn, and are going round seek-
ing sympathy for the “ terrible price ” they’ll have
to pay for a hjirp—“Fifty pounds? I thought
about ten ! ” ?
Persistent opera-goets are getting to watch for
little details, such as the deep round hollow, like
a gigantic dimple, that comes into the Sapio’s
throat for an instant when she takes her breath
in the more strenuous phases of her singing. And
Dani’s two frowns, one of anger, the other of
anguish. The latter is employed, for instance,
when he hears of the death of Lucia, and is
reminiscent of that contracted, anguished brow of
the Mater Dolorosa in some Italian pictures.
The tenor’s square collar (in “ Lucia ”) also meets
with the approval of several girls, and they have
copied the ba,ck of it for a sailor blouse. And the
flute to which Sapio sings reminds some of
Emerson’s “ The flute can do heard farther than
the cart.”
By the way, Fred Leist is making a sketch of
Signorina Marchi in the white and crimson dress
she wears as Alioe in “Luoia” for the forthcoming
exhibition.
St. Vincent’s Ball had the benefit of the
Australian Club’s Ball decorations, so if it wasn’t
numerous it was decorative.
Big balls every night of the same week rather
handicapped it. Miss Darley opened the dance,
and the Minister for Works walked about absent-
mindedly, and when some dancers bumped him
he thought it was a deputation for lighting the
Domain, and said, meditatively, “Well, Mr.
Broughton, it shall have my best attention.
One of the funniest items at the welcome to
“A” Battery, last Monday, was the “Tommy”
door-guard trying to keep out artist Souter as a
ticketless derelict while Battery and welcomers
were waiting for his lightning-sketches. Souter
had a little Glengarry cap on his ear, a briar pipe
in his face, and, when he got angry, he developed
a Scotch accent, and a cultured “ Tommy ” said,
“ Let him in ; he must be Edgardo Ravens wood.”
The Lieut.-Governor, who was unwell, did not
come to present the battery’s medals—Minister
See did so after the long speech which he has de-
livered so often that we know it by heart. The
G.O.C. also spoke, and all the time 250 pairs of
eyes were turning tenderly towards where the
whisky lurked. Captain and Mrs. Henderson,
the Sees, and Mrs. JB. R. Wise were on the plat-
form. Miss Stella Taylor’s brilliant pianoforte
solo brought the Battery away even from the
whisky. A fine basket of roses, sent by an admirer
to Miss Taylor, was put by an officer for safety
under the table in the committee’s refreshment-
room. Then the putter disappeared, and not
until Miss Mylott was singing were the flowers
recovered. The Amusement Committee, which
consisted of Lieut. D. K. Inglis, had the maca-
damised face to suggest that the basket be pre-
sented to Miss Mylott, as it was a pity not to give
publicity to such fine flowers. Miss Taylor
laughed and agreed, so Miss Mylott, who had got
a tremendous reception, also got the flowers and
a large encore, one “Tommy” proclaiming that
“ there were no Evas of this sort in South
Africa.” Mr. Fites and the Feld wick quartette
contributed items.
Miss Holroyd’s concert was packed. The
beneficiary, clad iii blue, was in the best of voices.
Mr. Suttor made a sympathetic speech on behalf
of the departing songbird, who goes to Milan
with the Steffanis. Miss Florence Schmidt,
although we rarely hear of her, “is in constant
work,” to quote a London paper. She, and for
that matter a score of other artists, are under
engagement to an agent named Newman, who hires
out his stipendiary songbirds all over the United
Kingdom, if he so wills. Miss Holroyd’s friend,
Nellie Cook, sang for some of the Italian opera
principals in the green-room last week, and they
rapturously embraced her, prophesying fame and
gold for her notes.
Friends of Mr. Robert Garran are congratulating
him on his engagement to Miss Robson, of the
Girls’ C. of E. Grammar School.
At the Quirindi balcony semi-tragedy a nice
girl collapsed with the rest, and, though unin-
jured, thought for the moment that she was frac-
tured all over. As she sat amongst the ruins
sobbing, an old gentleman ran up and asked her
if her legs were broken. “I don’t know,” she
wailed, “my head aches.” He felt the top of her
skull—the wire hair-frame moved under his hand
—then, with a horrified exclamation, he called for
a doctor, saying, “ The top of this poor girl’s head
is split right off.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The vice-regal pen at the Royal was a thing
of beauty last Wednesday night when Lady
Hopetoun conferred her patronage on ‘Riding
to Win.’ Barring the Coppin matinee of the
previous week, this was her first theatre ex-
perience since going into official mourning, and
she positively beamed upon the lively spectacle.
Dog Rover gave the box a particular treat by per-
forming the tank act in great style. The loyal
creature took to the water without the least
demur, and, instead of making straight for the
wooden shore when Florence Seymour grabbed
him by the collar, he put on a ‘ Queen knows
best ’ air and swam the full length of the tank
with the actress in tow. Seeing that Rover had
beaten his record, the professional pedallers in
the bicycle race were bound to put on some extra
sprint. The Countess's astonishment at these
novel effects found voluble utterance when man-
ager Christie Simonsens was piloting her off the
premises. She wanted to know all about the
tank. It was explained that the stage Yarra was
enclosed in canvas, 7ft. deep at the place where
the villain hurls his victim into it, and so spacious
that the fireman’s hydrant takes 90min. to bring
the river up to high-water mark.
“ The only diversion the Gov.-Generaless allows
herself from the round of dreary public duties is
hunting; the inquiring-eyed lady rides her hack
to hounds almost every time they meet. Mrs.
Corbet has cancelled all engagements; she will
disappear into the retirement of Guv’ment-House
for a solitary term, and in her absence the Excel-
lency will get along solus. The charms and claims
of Sassiety women of her ken interest her not.
There is nothing Irish about the Countess as a
hostess. No one would take her for a country-
woman of the philosopher who decided that
* Contintion is better than loneliness. ’
“So far, ‘Quo Vadis’ is the quaintest play of
present Wilson Barrett season. W.B. has a pretty
slave (Miss Latimer), given to nestling her head
in master’s corsage. I call it 4 corsage ’ because,
in this play, plump Wilson’s clothes are mostly
dowagerish—low necks, short sleeves, gathered
waist-bodices, and . long skirts. In view of
the goings -on of Petronius, I’ve lost some
of my old sympathy for slaves. Eunice,
as represented by Miss Latimer, does
nothing for her bread and butter but bill, and
occasionally coo, to the accompaniment of a dumb
lute. She is not gifted as a songstress, but her
vocal efforts are an improvement on those of Maud
Jeffries, who had no more ear than a hen. I shall
always remember Maud’s song in ‘ Ben-My-
Chree,’ because a rude old man near me exclaimed,
as soon as she’d finished, ‘Well—blow me—she
hasn’t got a fid-dle in her interior.’ He didn’t say
‘ interior, ’ but never mind.
“A warm bit in ‘Quo Vadis’ is the scene
where Basil Gill’s Christian fiancee is stripped
and bound to the horns of a mad bull and turned
into the arena. Basil is too delicate in mind to
want to watch how the young woman is getting
on, so Wilson Barrett (Wilson lias always all the
fat) reports progress to his frantic kinsman.
Under the circumstances, one would expect W.B.
(who is supposed to be a cultured Pagan) to sup-
press harrowing details, but he isn’t taking any
suppression. Beginning with the red sparkles in
the eyes of the bull, he piles on the agony all he
knows. In this, the play travesties the book.
Then there’s a debauch in the halls of Nero.
Christian Lillah M‘Carthy has been dragged to it
against her will, and her Pagan admirer (Basil
Gill) gets drunk, and pesters her with warm
attentions. Lillah takes to the proceedings very
kindly, seems to me. In fact, she seems to be en-
joying herself fairly well when a Christian brother
rushes in and abducts her in an interfering sort of
way.
“ The Crane-Power management of Melb. Prin-
cess’s made two changes in the cast of ‘ Nadjezda’
ere the show was four days old. On the Tuesday
night Miss Richter played an unaccountable
Yankee girl with decidedly more vim and natural-
ness than she had shown on the previous Saturday
—and she was, in fact, a success. Yet next
morning’s papers announced that, in consequence
of Miss Richter’s indisposition. Miss Raymond
would play her part for the remainder of the run
of ‘Nadjezda.’ Also the kiddie in the first act
was changed from Stella Dodds to Dorothy Vane.
Already another Dorothy Vane ! What has Mrs.
Norman Menzies to say to this ?
“Women really have the gift of intuition.
Have you noticed that even young, unthinking
girls are hot against second marriages ? In their
innocence, these buds imagine their opposition
arises from sentiment. In reality, instinct is
telling them that widows are a spinster’s worst
foes, and that if no woman was allowed more than
one husband in a lifetime, the ‘young person’
would be more successful in the struggle for ma-
trimonial existence. Since Spring came in, one
widow after another has been heard of, in connec-
tion with re-mating and re-pairing. Mr. M‘Kean,
son of big, kind, dead and gone lawyer M‘Kean,
has lately wedded the former Mrs. Spencer,
an unusually rich lady, who inherited her
thousands, and her red-brick ‘ House of the seven
gables ’ from her first husband. Musical Mrs.
Bruce Sargent, of Moonee Ponds, is to be married
(Sept. 17th) to dark, handsome Mr. S. Samuel, of
St. Kilda, brother of Mrs. Agar Wynne. Far-
ther afield, George Egerton, author of ‘Keynotes,’
and a native of Melbourne, has, I’m told, wedded
her third husband, Reginald Golding Bright, an
English journalist.
“ Girls anxious to turn an honest penny, with-
out deserting the privacy of ‘ home, sweet home,’
should try superior ‘ plain-sewing.’ Fancy-work
is a drug in the market, because even Mrs. Dives
and daughters make their own tray-cloths and
d’oyleys, stimulated by the fact that mere man
likes to see a woman ‘delicate with her needle.’
But there is always sale for hand-made under-
garments and baby-linen. Unfortunately, few
amateurs are competent to turn out dainty white
work fast enough to make it pay. The blouses of
the moment play into the hands of the seamstresses,
with their rows of fancy stitching, like the ‘ vein-
ing ’ and ‘ knotting ’ on mother’s old sampler.
“ At the Ferguson-Glasgow wedding Lady Alice
Boyle’s bridesmaids had gowns of white glace-silk,
much addicted to open-work ‘ herring-bone ’ stitch,
while waved lace-insertion added to the light
effect of one-flounce skirts and sailor-blouses.
Pale pink chiffon sashes fell in long ends at the
back, and wreaths of wild pink-roses with tulle
veils, helped out dainty toilettes. A going-away
gown of pale-blue voile, guipured and white-
chiffoned, was also rich in open-work stitches,
while the burnt-straw hat, threaded with blue
ribbon and wreathed with roses, had lining of
drawn chiffon.
“ Mayoress Gillott has pulled round and is in
harness again; she feels strong enough now to
pump-handle the thousands who will turn up to
her ‘ Come one—come all ’ reception, so the ordeal
is fixed for Thursday next. The community
respectfully expresses a wish that the Mayoress
will in some way set a distinction between her twin
sister and herself, for the mistakes already made
in congratulating and gushing over the wrong
lady have left half of our womankind chagrined.
“ Swanston-st. enjoyed a rare sight on Wednes-
day last, when a riding-party, consisting of Lady
Janet Clarke, Magistrate Panton, with little Ivy
Clarke as chaperone, cantered along on prancing
steeds. Of late, the straight-backed magistrate,
whom Professor Tucker holds to be the most
chivalrous and elegant figure in Melbourne, has
been the chief Mentor of the youthful dowager.
Once it was Byron Moore and Prof. Norris, but
now Dr. Maudsley is in the cabinet of ‘ Cliveden,’
and Mr. Panton its prime minister. The magis-
trate is a widower, by the way.
“Last week caddies made the proverbial ‘for-
tune’ out of kit-carrying, tea-making and ball-
finding on Sandringham links. The Royal
Melbourne tournament kept a host of fevered
golfers trekking over the sands, called by courtesy
‘ the links,’ from Monday till Saturday. A
Geelongite—Miss Guthrie—cut the sprucest figure
on the course, but she failed to follow up her
Sydney run of luck. Miss Raleigh, who won the
championship of Victoria, is a cousin of the
Family Herald hero, Ernest.
“For the last three or four weeks, the amours
of an elderly solicitor and his type-writer have
administered to the entertainment of a gross of
clerks in a big financial office down Collins-street.
One small Ganymede is put on the watch by the
mischievous clerks, and at a signal from this imp,
the pens are flung down, the high desks deserted,
and a row of craning necks are to be seen along
the transparent part of the half-stained windows.
The same performance goes on every day, but
neither the lovers nor the lookers-on weary of the
sport. The old buck is ardent, and the girl full
of capers ; so their ‘ Kissing Duet ’ is something
special. Tiny struggles, playful slaps, ear-
pinches and back-strokes accompany the duet in
its course and enliven the proceedings.
“ Once more a supercilious figure-head of Sas-
Biety mourns the departure (temporary only) of
his lady-love. The affair has been hanging fire
for four years, and is as far from being clinched as
ever. The religious question—not the lady’s
coldness —is said to be the stumbling-block, or
rather the barbed-wire fenoe.
“ The Findon Harriers Hunt Club meeting all
Moonee Valley promises to be just a little more
toney than the club’s previous record. The
Countess, who is always at her happiest among
the gee-gees, will present the whip to the rider of
the Bracelet Stakes, for which interesting event
all the extremely nicest girls and matrons have
nominated something or other, in the hope that it
will sneak the bit of jewellery.
“ Poor old Anglican Bishop Goe —who lost his
wife quite lately—has announced his intention to
resign the Melbourne see at the end of this month.
The rumor of his approaching retirement was
strenuously denied in more than one quarter, but
inasmuch as the weary widower is suffering from
a painful ear-abscess and other ills, he couldn’t be
expected to remain longer in harness. ‘Field
Flowers Melbourne ’ has been a bishop of no im-
portance for 14 years. His personality was quite
unsuited to gaiters, and his early profession of
Evangelicalism established a prejudice against
him among the casual church-going men who were
the most valuable admirers—in a financial sense—
of his predecessor, Dr. Moorhouse. As a matter
of fact, Dr. Goe was a broader-minded man than
he promised to be when he first came to Mel-
bourne. He could preach a very good sermon.
But the godless man-in-the-street never took the
trouble to hear him.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
The Dead President.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Ruler of the mightiest State
That Earth has seen since Time drew breatb
Has passed from life to join the great
Divine Democracy of Death.
A Nation’s best-embodied Thought,
Her Captain brave ; her chosen Man,
Has left his greatest plans unwrought—
But God has still the greater plan.
Above the Thrones of Chiefs and Kings,
Of President and Sovereign Lord,
Death hovers with vast viewless wings
And sharp, unseen, suspended sword.
The Drowsy Kings are disenthroned,
And dead and gone with dreams of yore;
Now he who shall be monarch owned
May smile —but he shall sleep no more.
And Kings and Presidents may well
Remember in their hours of pride
llow well-beloved Lincoln fell,
How Caesar in the forum died.
There is no death : beyond the bars
Of Time and Space he keeps his guest—
A star amidst your brighter stars,
O Great Republic of the West!
Where, under near or distant skies,
Your flag unto the winds is spread*
Its banner-stars they are the eyes
Of all your great immortal dead.
The youngest union of the years
To you shall send in sign of grief,
For your dead President no tears—
No tears but this slight laurel-leaf.
VICTOR J. DALEY.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
One Federal Cabinet man is addicted to
telling pressmen confidentially just how little he
thinks of the Prime Minister.
The envious say that if you want to find
the reason for a recent much-discussed Sydney
appointment, cherchez la femme.
A Sydney solicitor holds a unique record.
In five criminal cases in which he was engaged for
the defence the accused were hanged.
Sydney’s “Home for Working Gentle-
women ” is named St. Bernard’s—in honor, future
chroniclers will tell, of St. Bernard Wise.
“ Banjo ” Patterson is sending excellent
copy to S.M. Herald from the Black North ; so
far he does not seem to have a muzzle on.
A non-Italian member of the Williamson
opera co. is said to have a tough time with her
colleagues. It may be a question of nationality—
or of press-booming.
M‘Kin'ley owed much of his success to
being cartooned, on the strength of a fairly good
resemblance, as Napoleon I., when the Bonaparte
boom was raging in America.
On the point of tubercular infection :
A.M.P. doctors now ask applicants for policies
whether at home or at work they are thrown into
direct contact with any consumptive.
Brisbane Courier credits R. E. O’Connor
with being the most popular of the Federal Minis-
ters, an “ideal leader of the House, with great
decision and a marvellous perception of character.”
Gov. General Hopetoun will complete his
41st year on Wednesday, September 25. He was
almost as much a boy as Earl Beauchamp when he
was first sent to Melbourne to govern, a dozen
years ago.
It is not bar-work alone that keeps G. H.
Reid from the Federal Parliament; he is awaiting
the limelight of the tariff discussion. The great
political variety-artist knows something of stage-
management.
Millionaire Carnegie allows £250 a year to
several distant relations and old schoolfellows in
Maoriland. One of them recently went to Scot-
land to personally thank him. The pawky city of
Dunedin now breathlessly awaits the result of an
appeal to Carnegie in aid of a free public library.
The irony of Fate ! Last week the
N.S.W. Amateur Fishermen’s Association sent a
vote of sympathy to poor President M’Kmley.
Just think of it! An Amateur Fishermen’s Asso-
ciation inflicting its sympathy on the lineal suc-
cessor of George Washington, the Man Who Never
Told a Lie!

Bishop I ield I lowers Goe gets his curious
Christian name from his father, who was a Lincoln-
shire solicitor. For long after his arrival in Mel-
bourne, private correspondents addressed the
Bishop as Field Flowers “Grow,” whilst the
popular pet name for the prelate was “Buttercups
and Daisies.
Roosevelt does not promise to make a good
President: he is a Colonel Mackay built on some-
what larger lines. In politics the professional
soldier has always proved a failure (barring one or
two of the Caesar type of supreme genius) ; the
amateur, advertising “literary” swashbuckler a
still more pronounced frost.
Waxed Madden’s inevitable message to
McKinley, awaited with breathless interest by the
whole civilized world, contained the usual rich
array of Maddenese, culminating in a defiant
allusion to “this ruffian murder.”
“I was at the opera when the message
was received,” said Premier See, “and the
terrible news completely upset me.” This was
about M‘Kinley. Will N.S.W. ever get a Pre-
mier with a sense of the Congruous ?
Captain Staughton, a returned cohen-
tingenter, has been returned unopposed to fill
Bourke West seat (formerly held by his father) in
Vic. Leg. Assembly. Here is a pocket borough
existent in the democracy. Let us hope that the
“ Captain ” has something young coming on to fill
the seat in succession to himself.
The most cruel criticism of Seddon’s
budget speech came from Geo. Fisher, who
strongly advised the Premier to permanently
employ a sub-editor to judiciously compress his
utterances; also Fisher asked how on earth com-
pliments on the amiability of the Duchess found
their way into a financial statement.
A queer thing is that almost every other
prominent Australian politician will tell you, con-
fidentially, that his rival has Bright’s disease and
can’t possibly last another 12 months. And it is
always Bfight’s disease. Reminders of that
malady are everywhere. Just look at all the men
you see drinking milk-and-soda at the clubs.
George Turner (he still keeps the old brass-'
plate on his gatepost) is the most unsocial man in
Australian politics. Nobody has yet seen him
take a drink with aliybody. Since joining the
Federal Government, he has been more of a lone-
hand than ever. David Syme is another man who
mostly goes alone, because he loves good company.
Anarchist Johan Most, just gaoled in New
York, is chronically arrested when there’s any
trouble in US. A. Most’s personally-dirty habits
were responsible for the once popular gibe that
the anarchist was sworn to destroy society, and
never to wash himself. Certainly, Australia’s
best-known Anarchist is the dirtiest man whom
any human being ever got to windward of here.
According to Jack Want, Mr. Wise and
Mr. Suttor (neither of whom, Jack avers, believes
in woman-suffrage) tossed up to see who should
move the second reading of the Women’s Franchise
Bill in N.S.W. Council. With Mr. Want, Mr.
Wise and Mr. Suttor eagerly interested in her
welfare just now, woman should be all right.
She may not get the franchise, but she will have
a lot of attention.

I. R. Cohen, novelist, financier and poli-
tician, who fights Mahony for the Annandale seat
before N.S.W. Elections and Qualifications Com-
mittee, maintains that he was the founder of
Sunday journalism in Sydney. George Howe,
printer and editor of Sydney Gazette, published
his paper for 40 years on Sundays—from 1803 to
1843.

The bearded Fysh was once—when Tas.
Treasurer—boasting in Hobart to a visiting Age
reporter of the magnitude of Raspberry Land
official finance. “ Why, last year we got £38,000
out of our income-tax,” he proudly remarked.
“My word !” said the Age man, “in about 20
years you’ll be getting nearly as much as my boss
makes every year.”
It is now said that Mr. Henry Gullett’s
occupancy of Sydney D.T. editorial chair is “only
temporary.” The Bulletin shouldn’t wonder.
Mr. Gullett has toiled as a journalist for over 30
years, and has made a competency. The man
who, in his sixties, would take-on the permanent
editorship of a daily paper would visit Sheol for
pleasure in his summer vacation.
Melbourne friends of Mr. Murray Smith
are glad to hear that the hard shell Freetrader is
returning from England shortly in improved
health. Murray Smith was said to have been
under suspicion of cancer before he made the trip,
but one hears of a good many Melbourne men
clearing themselves of the same suspicion. Too
much cancer is talked nowadays.
Nolan, the White Cliffs (N.S.W.) police-
sergeant, who appears to have done first-class
work in connection with the sticking up
of the coach and the mail robbery, is
one of the good-many men who have had
practically all their police experience out-back.
He got his first lift through the reports of that
good officer Saunders, now inspector at Orange,
but then at Broken Hill, whose right-hand man
he was for years.
George Burnett Barton, the Federal Pre-
mier’s elder brother, who died last week, was
retrenched by Treasurer M‘Millan out of the
billet of official historian because the calico-man
didn’t think that N.S.W. could spare £IOOO a year
for brilliant literary work. And the pawky Mac.
thought Barton too slow. History, he evidently
considered, should be turned out by the yard
daily, like invoices.
Two years ago, while conversationally
traversing some statement by a local medical
official, the able and modest Dr. Goode, of Syd-
ney, contended that bovine was not identical with
human tuberculosis ; he knew that from the fact,
ascertained by his own observation, that the
bovine bacilli continued to live under conditions
which were fatal to the human variety. The
great Koch now announces the same conclusion
from his investigations.
Already M.L. prophets are forecasting the
Seddonian apotheosis. One Parliamentary corres-
pondent prophesies that Seddon is already pre-
paring for a final departure from Maoriland. He
will go to London for the Coronation, will then
receive a commission as Governor of the Orange
River Colony (late Free State) ; then will come a
peerage. But the blusterous and bathetic Seddon,
though he is the sort of man whom the Jingoes
use for all he is worth, is not the kind of person
whom they make into a peer. Their public-school
and university prejudices forbid. If Seddtn’s
ambition is to become a “Lord” he will die a
bitterly-disappointed man.
The memory of Robert Lowe
(Viscount Sherbrooke), of N.S.W.
early days, was revived in a Sydney
police-court the other day through
a plumber stealing lead from one of
Lowe’s houses in Paliner-st., Sydney.
It is nearly 60 years since Lowe
landed in N.S.W. His pink eyes,
rapid utterance, and extensive know-
ledge brought him at once • into
the ranks of marked men. As a
barrister he was pertinacious, as a
politician he was one-half rank Tory,
and the other half forward Liberal.
But he was thrifty, and to
deal in houses and lands. The
Palmer-st. house above-mentioned
was one of those primitive"bargains.
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lee
has lost in avoirdupois since taking
over his shattered battalions. Lee
is really a backblocker, with a store-
keeper’s view of the world. He stands
to a political principle exactly as lie
stands to the ruling price of starch.
When particulars of the White Cliffs
(N.S.W.) mail-coach sticking-up and robbery
reached the capitals, the detective-inspired papers
traced in it the hand of a bold, daring, cool, cun-
ning, experienced criminal; and they put it down
forthwith to Sparks, the Pentridge escapee. And
Tomlinson, in whose clothes-box the loot was
afterwards found, and against whom a black case
awaits his answer at next Broken Hill Circuit
Court, is a 22-year-old country bumpkin, never
out of the bush, never before a court hitherto, and
the soft, dull butt of the “gougers’” camp in
which he lived at White Cliffs. Thus far, so much
for the papers’ “circumstantial evidence’’—the
sort of thing with which they habitually poison
the public mind !”
The world can show few so
meteoric careers as that of “ Teddy ” Roosevelt,
President of the American Republic. Three years
ago he was classed as a society dude. Volunteer-
ing for active service in Cuba during the war
against Spain, he organised and commanded the
“ Rough-Riders,” who, at the Battle of San Juan,
in June, 1898, fought the only serious land-action
of the war—a tin-pot “battle,” but screamed up
by the American Eagle to the level of a Sedan.
Teddy came home a hero, and his popularity was
better nursed and so outlasted that of Dewey,
Hobson, and other idols of the one-sided struggle.
His party gave him the nomination as Vice-Presi-
dent last year as a polite shunt. A profound
belief in himself, an aptitude for advertising, and
almost eerie luck have made RooseveP U.S.
President.
Dr. H. G. A. Wright, M.R.C.S., Eng.,
just deceased, practised in Sydney since 1853, and
during 48 years supervised the births of many
thousands of Australians. The old doctor had
a marvellous faculty of putting-in his sleep on the
short-instalment plan. Jogging in his carriage
through the Rocks, Darling!)urst, and Pyrmont
on an all-night round, he would enjoy 40 winks
between patients, seeing Mrs. Smith through her
trouble after a refreshing sleep of seven minutes,
snatched on the way from Jones’ croupy baby, and
so on ; and then turn up as fresh as paint in the
morning. Some years ago, the old man had a
marvellously-lucky escape from a cold-blooded
conspiracy organised against him by a set of male
and female scoundrels, and was warmly felicitated
by a gathering of his brother doctors on the vindi-
cation of his honor.
The most interesting press characterisa-
tions of Commonwealth Parliament members
come from the pen of Mr. Peters, Sydney
D.T.’s representative (a man steeped to the lips
in the literature of the Elizabethian era). For
instance, concerningP. O. Fysh :
He is one of the quaintest orators in the Chamber.
There is a flavor of the seventeenth century in his long,
ingeniously, and almost inextricably, involved sentences.
He doesn’t say that the clause provides that letters shall
not be delivered. He says : “ The clause wherein we find
the most precise wording wherewith to meet the desires
whereof we have frequently heard expression, and of
which there shall be much argument yet uttered there-
about before there shall be a final determination reached
in the course of our deliberations, this clause herein
included gives power wherewith to prevent or prohibit or
inhibit, or what may have been called and may be called,
stop that which we long have known and whereof'much
more may hereafter be known and which future genera-
tions may dominate letters.”

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
A case of suicide by an aborigine is re-
corded by “ E.S.S.” -Towyan was a splendid
black, on the Wooroowoolga (Richmond River)
run—a squatter kingdom over which Towyan’s
tribe had long wandered. Wooroowoolga lost its
glory as a principality. Its best frontage lands
were cut up into farms, and selectors flocked like
crows upon its interior. This partitioning of the
noble area, followed by a big sale of cattle, so
preyed upon Towyan’s mind that he suicided by
walking into the river and cutting his throat with
a razor. That happened but a few months ago,
and is, I think, the first instance on record of an
aboriginal suicide.

“E. G. B.” : Re that “tired feeling”
supposed to dominate the Australian youth.
Several of us recently watchedaN.Q. youthloading
sludge from the ground to an ordinary quartz
dray—a high6-tonner—andhelifted 3 tons 17cwt.,
weighed over the mill weighbridge, in 25 minutes.
Yet he only weighs 7st. 41bs., and is not 14 years
old. This, too, without knowing the watch was
on him.

“ C.8.C.” : Was coming down from
Orange-way, and was in a second-class with one
other person. We were about to leave Orange
when a “swaggie ” swung his billy and bundle in
and followed himself. He struck up an acquaint-
ance, and told us that he had a ticket only for a
station a few miles on, but meant to go through
on it to Sydney. Asking us not to give him
away, he stowed his swag and billy under one
seat and himself under the other. All went well
till a trooper and a couple of youngsters for a
reformatory boarded the train, and you can
imagine swaggie’s feelings when he found the
trooper’s spurs within a foot of his nose. Myself
and the other passenger agreed to quit the com-
partment, as things were getting too mixed, and
did so at next stop. The trooper and his charges
also alighted here to join an escort party, and
their places were taken by four nuns, in whose
company our “planted” friend travelled right
through. Tickets collected at Stratlifield. Saw
swaggie—covered with dust —on Redfern plat-
form. He winked solemnly at us and departed in
peace.

Letters recently received by a Queensland
firm of solicitors :
To Mr. Blank and Dash.
It’s keeping on very bad still. My husband don’t stop
that cursed drink and the big rows. He is soon every
day drunk, and idles by night until 2 and 1 o’clock, and
then when he come home he don’t give me a night’s rest;
he don’t work, and he spends every penny that comes in.
And he says always he wants to drown himself or me. I
am not sure of my life no more. And he treats me very
bad, like a dog, curses and swears over me. The best
thing is to get separation and be separated from him
althoughter. And if you don’t help me I will clear away
from him, because Iye left know about all. I can’t help
it. And I suppose ye won’t perscute me, because I can’t
hold out, and it’s no use to stay with him. That man
wants good couple of months’ gaol for treating me so bad.
Please nndly see what is the best for me. Please I wrote
this letter without him know anything. Please don’t let

“ Meera ” : The Grafton
bore (8., 31/8/’01) is now down
over 3650 ft. and indications of
water are becoming less favor-
able. Graftonites, in fact, have
given up all hope of getting
water from the bore, but Govern-
ment steadily cold shoulders any
suggestion of taking up another
scheme of supply. The Rich-
mond River people say that
Premier See keeps pegging away
at the bore with a view of ascer-
taining how far his constitu-
ency is from Hades.

“ Trapper ” : Not long ago
in Victoria I was busy making
a “scrape,” in which to set a
rabbit-trap, when I heard a
“cooee !” and, looking round,
saw a little girl running towards
me from a selector’s hut a little
distance away. “What’s the mat-
ter, my girl ?” I asked. She panted out:
“Please, mother sent me over to ask you not to
set any traps under that wattle, for two of my
little brothers and one of my sisters are buried
there.” The Graves of the Household 1 I packed
up and moved further on.

* * * *
“ W.S.T.” : Australia has evolved a sheep-
dog which, for intelligence, activity, and suitability
to the climate, runs rings round any other type of
collie. Tweed, winner of the late Sydney sheep-
driving contests, is a good specimen. No shouting
or calling is required for the best of this class of
dog. The raising of a hand or the pointing of a
riding-whip gives them sufficient cue. To get to
a point where a turn is needed, if the flock is
closely packed, the dog will run over the sheep’s
backs like a gymnast, wheeling wide on return to
heel. The chief fault (perhaps due to a dash of
dingo blood) is that the Australian dog is inclined
to be cruel and punish the sheep with the teeth.

“Scotty”: Of course, we all know the
awful state of some shearers’ huts. Asa general
rule they are a little better than the rouseabouts’
barracks. N.S.W. Minister Perry, in his Bill, is
mean—very mean. Why did he not make it a
bush-workers’ measure? By the Bill, buck-
niggers, Red Indians, blackfellows (Australian),
and Fijians may sleep with shearers, but not so
Hindoos or Chinese. Then 240 ft. is the sleeping
space. Allow Oft. for height and 7ft. for width.
That gives less than 4ft. along the side for each —
man, bunk, passage and all.

* * • *
“ Yarrum ” Be tuberculosis : An intelli-
gent South Coast (N.S.W.) dairyman tells me
that, within his experience—45 years—there has
not been a single case of consumption among the
dairy workers in these southern districts, which,
considering the number of milking-cows suffering
from tuberculosis, fits in with the lately-announced
Koch theory. My informant, however, attributes
the immunity of milkers to their beooming in-
oculated, per medium of sore hands—they mostly
have sore hands —whilst milking. The old chap
also instanced the case of a boy with a strong con-
sumptive tendency, who became robust and
“strong as a horse” after six months’ milking.
The milkers frequently drink milk hot from the
cow, and I have myself seen girls grasp the teats
of doubtful-looking animals, and squirt milk into
their mouths.

The most humorous sticking-up incident
in Australia belongs to the history of Dunolly,
(Vic.). A single-handed robber bailed up Cobb and

Co. near there and went through the passengers,
dropping all the loot into a square of canvas
spread on the ground. There was in the coach a
stout, big-boned female, her face swathed in red
flannel. She displayed quite a keen interest in
the highwayman, and never took her eyes from
his face, and presently, sweetly convinced, uttered
a squeal of triumph, and dashed at her man,
snatching the mask from his eyes. “It’s you,
Ben,” she cried. “Oh, you villain, I’ve found
you ! ” Ben glared at the lady. “By , so
you have ! ” he gasped. Then, tearing himself
free, he rode off as if Hades were at his heels, for-
getting all about his booty. The woman declared
that the robber was the long-lost husband whom
she had vainly sought on every field in Victoria.

Over the White Cliffs mail-robbery the
most disgusted people, it is said in whispers
locally, were three men who had arranged to do
the same job, but ab a point about 35 miles
further on. Altogether that lumbering coach,
with but three unarmed men and a girl on board,
carried through the wilderness that black night
over £3OOO in money and gems ! Single-police
escort or not, there’s almost sure to be something
more to report on the same road before long.
* • * *
Hyde Park is the wonderland of Sydney,
and there are more curious sights to. be seen there
in one year than in a whole life-time of circuses.
Last week a gentleman who had been indulging
in tied-house refreshment went into the park to
scrape the fantods and tarantulas off himself.
Unfortunately they got inside his clothes and a 3
the only way to get at them was to take his
clothes off, he took them off, every one of them,
right there in the park. Then a policeman took
a hand in the business, but the naked one strongly
objected, and the next act saw a naked man
sitting on a policeman’s chest flicking imaginary
insects off himself. While he attended to the
fantods the officer bucked him off his cbest, and
would have fallen upon him, but the clothesless
one mistook him for a spider and fled, just as he
was, across the park, and the populace stared
aghast at a large constable in a helmet, heavy
boots and stuffy uniform chasing an individual
in the lightest marching order. The small boys
yelled, “Go it, de Wet—Kitchener hain’t got
no show ! ” but the policeman was on his mettle,
and just then the naked one fell over an inoffen-
sive unemployed who was sleeping in the sun,
and ten minutes later he was back into his clothes
and on the way to the watch-house.
• * * *
A particularly aggravating custom in
Sydney dailies is the printing of tags from tinpot
centres after reports on events of national im-
portance. Thus:
Tin Dog, Sep. 9.—Great indignation is expressed here
at the attempted assassination of the American President.
Baldy Face, Sep. 9.—The news of the attempt on the
life of President McKinley caused great indignation here.
Toggi-Toggi, Sep. 9.—The announcement that an at-
tempt was made to murder President M'Kinley was
received with great indignation here.
and so on ad nauseam. The seoret of it is that
the energetic country correspondent who gets his
‘ ‘ expression ” into print makes from twopence to
fourpence out of it, and so a dead notability
soothes a way-back thirst. But to a sanely-con-
ducted paper it ought to be worth as many pounds
to leave the trash out.

Letter sent to the British Postmaster-
General and printed in the Post Office journal,
St. Martin’s lb Grand
Post Office Steppes, Tasmania,
Jan. 15, 1900.
To Postmaster-General, London.
Dear Sir,—Wishing to become acquainted with a young
English woman, views matrimony, good look, good cha-
racter, with dark hair, medium height, well-formed, dark
eyes, good teeth and health, not over 30 years, plain
dress, good scholar, spinster, or widow not objected to,
but must leave her family with friends. Some young
ladies might write soon as possible. lam a young man
26 years of age, farmer and laborer. The young lady
must be saving. Oan her friends pay her passage out
here after been accepted? She must not be a flirt. From
counties preferred. The right type of an Englishwoman.
Send photo. A good home in a cold climate, bush-home,
Tasmania in Australia. I want a well-behaved young
woman, with all her faculties. Good temper. Here is a
chance for a good young lady. I have not been married.
It takes about a month or six weeks for letters to reach
here.
George H .
P.S.—Hoping to be successful with a good wife.
• « • *
Item from a N.S.W. northern town (name
of town carefully disguised, lest the principal
female character in the story should come down
and hit this paper with her umbrella): Up at Big
Dust lately a blushing Jack (aged 30) and his
newly-acquired Jill were so severely bashed by
the bridegroom’s mother that amicable arrange-
ments for their entertainment were permanently
“off.” Related of same lady that when news of
her eldest son’s marriage reached her, she
promptly set out at midnight, through eight miles
of bush, to administer similar gentle tokens of
her disapproval, and successfully smacked
both parties. There was another son—a pale,
frail youth who made bashful love to the girl next
door until her father demanded what his inten-
tions were. He said if they weren’t honorable he’d
shoot him, whereas his mother said she’d extermi-
nate him if they were. The situation was too
much for the timid lover, so he died. That un-
fortunate event spoiled the old lady’s record. But
for it she might have been remembered as the only
local mother-in-law who flogged her three sons
and her three daughters-in-law on their respective
wedding-days.
* * * *
The aborigines collected by Q. Govt,
from the West, and domiciled at Woodford, north
coast district, will be killed off by the humid
climate thereof almost as quickly as by the vile
Chow charcoal opium of Western towns. The
poor nigs, suffer much from chest affections and
rheumatics, and complain that “ Altogether too
much water tumble down.”

Those people who argue as if the Transvaal
war arose from a British determination to make
the dirty Boers clean should clamor for hostile
operations against the country towns of N.S.W.
In the bush a traveller—exhilarated and vivified by
the purity of the atmosphere—recalls with
scepticism tales of the heavy typhoid and diphtheria
mortality of the town to which he is bound ; but
when he arrives the smells and other insanitary
horrors of the place promptly remove his doubts,
Parkes, Forbes and Cowra are hotbeds of disease
and many of the smaller towns are even worse.
GO ON THE LAND , YOUNG MAN.
PROSPECTIVE BUYER : “ And have you a good
rainfall?”
PROSPECTIVE SELLER : “ Well, it jest rains when-
ever we wants it!”
Thus the father of a N.S. W. young woman
who had increased the population only two daya
after her marriage
“ I tell you it’s a record ! There’s nothing wrong
about it, ’cos my daughter wouldn’t do anything like
that. It’s just one of the wonderfullest things that ever
happened. It’s a record ! ”
The present scribe gives his affidavit that this is
an absolutely true bill.
* * * »
A neglected Q. sight is the Burdekin Falls,
where the river literally forces itself through the
coast range to reach the Pacific. Approximately
the falls are about 100 ft. high, and below them is
a series of rapids for miles through an almost im-
passable gorge. The watershed area drained by
the Burdekin is about 60,000 sq. miles, so that the
flood-time grandeur of the cataract may be
imagined.

Gallant Soldier in Danger.
A gallant soldier of the Empire, Sergeant
M'Namara, late of His Majesty’s 82nd Regiment,
has given to a reporter, upon request, some par-
ticulars of his life.
“You were present at the Siege and Fall of
Sebastopol, were you not ? ” said the reporter.
“Yes,” said Sergeant M'Namara, “ I was then
in the 82nd Regiment. For that campaign I re-
ceived the British Medal and Clasp, and the
Turkish Medal. During the Indian Mutiny _ I
took part in the relief of Lucknow under Sir Colin
Campbell, for which I received the Lucknow
Medal and Clasp.”
“I would like you to tell me, Sergeant, what
you consider the most trying experience you have
gone through.”
“Well,” was the reply, “that occurred in
Australia in this wise. At the time —several
winters ago—l was confined to bed through a
severe cold, which developed into influenza. I
suffered from pains in shoulders and limbs,
heavy headaches, loss of appetite, sickness, and a
heavy drowsy feeling, although I was quite
unable to sleep properly. The pains in my
head were so bad, that I could not lay the back
of it upon my pillow. Soup and tea were the
only nourishment I could take. Later on I had
another attack of influenza, and after that I had
two every winter. I was so weak and ill with it
that considering my advanced age (sixty-six) I
should not have been surprised if it had termin-
ated fatally. To add to all my troubles, rheu-
matism affictod me in the left shoulder and in the
foot, causing gnawing, aching pains. I had
medical treatment and tried many advertised
remedies as well, but they proved useless.”
“You no doubt took the one remedy which
invariably wards off and cures influenza,” re-
marked the reporter.
“Yes, I did,” said Sergeant McNamara. “I
took Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale people.
After using them for two weeks I felt consid-
erably benefited. I continued the treatment,
and now, notwithstanding my age and the late
inclement weather, I am hale and hearty and
have been free all this winter from influenza and
rheumatism. I have a good appetite, sleep well,
and am stronger and brighter than I have been
for years. I strongly reccommend Dr. Williams’
pink pills to all those suffering from influenza
and rheumatism.”
Influenza may be warded off by taking Dr.
Williams’ pink pills, which cure its after effects
by enriching the blood, toning up the nerves
and strengthening the constitution. In • this
way they also cure rheumatism, neuralgia,
sciatica, bronchitis, consumption, weak heart,
diarrhoea, consumption of the bowels and lungs,
fits, scrofula, paralysis, etc. Sold by chemists
and storekeepers and by the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and
nine ; six boxes fifteen and three, post free.
Sergt. McNamara resides at George-street,
Burwood.*

* * * *
“ Jaynol ” : “ The haunt of the bell-bird ”
is more than a mere phrase, as they adhere to
certain localities—possibly on account of the sugar
or “manna” on the gum-leaves, of which they are
very fond. Their mysterious notes come from
various quarters, but you seldom catch sight of
one of the little drab objects.
“R. 8.” : Recent Bulletin boyang dis-
cussion recalls the fact that (8., 13/7/’01) E. S.
Sorensen wrote of a “pack-horse drover’s cook.”
Where is the paoker-dude who carries a cook ?
* * * *
Cairns papers tell how a number of local
Chinese lately left that town in a sailing-boat,
amid much uproar of crackers and beating of tom-
toms, to make a trip towards the mouth of the
Barron river. Certain fellow-countrymen were
drowned there some time ago, and it is necessary
to visit the spot, burn candles, scatter joss-paper
and throw provisions overboard by way of pro-
piating and assisting the departed. Really, the
only interesting people in Cairns are the Chows.
MISS EDITH CRANE IN "NADJEZDA”
[?] by the Swiss Studio.
The Australian merino, an expert alleges,
is “coarsening in character,” and soon its wool
will be all blanky, or thereabouts. The affection
of squatters for robust, strong-fibred animals for
breeding is set down as the cause.
* * * *
From the annual report of the Protector
of Northern Territory aborigines :
At the annual distribution of blankets over 300 natives
attended. The proportion of children was very low. The
aborigines here are gradually on their way to extinction.
. . . Between Port Darwin and the Katherine are over 60
half-caste children.

“Q.E.D.” : Talk about plurality ! I know
a man in an Australian township who is all these
things:—
Cemetery inspector, census collector, clerk to council,
clerk at elections, dog-tax collector, drill inspector,
forest ranger, general agent, inspector of weights and
measures, Inspector of fire brigade, librarian, officer Odd
Fellows ; poor-relief commissioner, pound-keeper, rate-
collector, secretary Masonic lodge ; life assurance agent,
secretary (travelling) new political association ; shop-
keeper.
* * * * *
The finest timber in Australia (N. Queens-
land cedar) is being wasted in a scandalous way.
Few people have any idea of the immense propor-
tions of Northern cedar. Only recentlv a tree
felled on the Barron took the timber-getters a
whole day to bring down, and yielded no less than
14,000 ft of timber. Whole houses, churches and
hotels are built of cedar in that district.

“Steff”: “ Itan ” (B. 27/7/’01) re tele-
graph-poles with a list, reminds me of King
Cacombau’s monument at Suva, Fiji, which took
a decided lean after it was put up. Every-
body was wont to point this out to the unfortunate
town-clerk. But that official used to get even
with them. He’d say: “The list is purely an
optical illusion. If you take your view of the
monument from the front it is perfectly straight.”
Then he would take the person to the edge of a
smellful swamp, and say: “Look at it carefully
from here. A little further this way, please,”—
and suddenly the gazer would flop to the middle
in stinking, black mud. The town-clerk would
then suddenly remember that he was wanted back
at the office.

they wouldn’t have

when all the time
the poor wretch has
been doing no more
than trying to keep
his miserable billet.

THE POET AND THE EDITOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He’d been writing various verses
Singing sweetly Of the nurses
(’Tis a weakness patients have when gaining health)
And they vowed he was a poet,
And that all the world should know it;
And that fame awaited him and lots of wealth.
So he wrote a love-effusion,
Breathing passion in profusion,
And he praised the pretty nurses to the skies ;
Told the same old, old sweet story—
Which by this time should be hoary—
And their eyes he called a glimpse of Paradise.
Well, the editor perused it,
And he never once abused it,
As from page to page he read the poem through ;
And the Poet’s brow was burning,
As the man of cult and learning
Cleared his throat to speak—they generally do.
“ Twenty pounds ! ” was all he uttered,
And with joy the poet muttered :
“ Oh, it’s more than I expected, I confess.”
“ Shades of Tennyson and Browning ! ”
Quoth the editor, now frowning—
“ Why, I mean I wouldn’t publish it for less 1 ”
TAS.
J. A. WALAR.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

without looking at the Bill to see if they are true or not

through the mouth, and, unless attended to, will result in a life-long annoyance

Victorias Gold Jubilee Exhibition.
18 51 BE 3NTDIG- O 19 OX
To be Opened at Market Square, Bendigo,
In NOVEMBER Next
and remain open until the
following Easter, 1902.
To Commemorate the Discovery of Gold in a manner that will rank as a
Memorial to the First Anniversary of the Australian Commonwealth.
Under the Ausi Dices of the GOVERNMENT OF VICTORIA, and the patronage of Sir JOHN f
MADDEN, K.C.M.G., LL.D., Lieutenant-Governor of the State of Victoria.
PRESIDENT :

Hon. Secretary : H. W. O. Smith, Secretary Chamber of Manufactures, 30 Queen Street.
The Exhibition will be interstate and international cope and character, and will comprise a vast exposition of
the world’s progress in the arts, sciences, manufacture products, inventions, discoveries, and industries. Fitting
prominence will be given to the gold-mining and other mineral resources, and phases of mining in Victoria and the
other States, Great Britain, Canada, America, and all parts of the old world, from their earliest stages to the present
time, while special courts will be erected for the display of manufactures and industries, wool, agriculture, dairying,
and machinery, &c.
A SUM OF £lOOO HAS BEEN SET APART FOR AWARDS IN THE VARIOUS DIVISIONS.
THE SCHEDULES.
A special gold medal will be awarded for an essay on the discovery of gold, the rise and progress of mining in
Australasia.
A special gold medal will be awarded for an essay on the Bendigo Goldfield.
DIVISION I.—Mining, its Phases and Resources—Early Mining and Mining Methods—Mining Products, Raw and
Prepared—Mining Systems and Engineering—Prime Movers and Mining Machinery—Ore Reduction and Dressing
Machinery—Mining Tools and Appliances—Fuels—Blasting Appliances and Inventions —Lubricators and Lubricants—
Mineral and other Oils, &c.—System of Lighting and Ventilating—Sciences Applied to Mining.
DIVISION ll.—General Resources, Industries, and Possibilities of Production Other than Mining—Raw and
Natural Products—Pastoral Resources—Agricultural Resources—lnventions —Discoveries—Viticultural Resources and
Wine Competitions—Beers, Spirits, Mineral and Aerated Waters —Horticultural Resources —Manufactured Products
—Machinery, Apparatus, and Processes Used in Industries—Chemical and Pharmaceutical Products.
DIVISION 111. —Sciences —Zoology—Botany—Ethnology—Chemistry— Electrical Technology, &c. —Telephonic
and Telegraphic Apparatus—ljontgen X Rays—Working Installation of Wireless Telegraphy—Dynamos—Optics-
Signalling Apparatus—Search-light and Heliograph, &c.
DIVISION IV. —The FINE ARTS. Sub-section 1 —Loan collections from Melbourne, Sydney, Adelaide, and
other centres. Sub-section 2—Oil paintings, water-color paintings, and various drawings, sculpture, and die-
sinking, other than those specified in the foregoing. Sub-section 3—Engravings, etchings, lithographs, and other
classes of tone and line-work. Sub-section 4—Architectural drawings. Sub-section s—Photography : (a) amateur,
(b) professional. Sub-section 6—An Old Curiosity Shop, showing old colonial prints, newspapers, documents, early-
day photographs, proclamations, early publications, and all kinds of objects, weapons and other things of colonial
historic interest. Sub-section 7 —A gallery of applied art, (a) of colonial manufacture, and (b) of foreign manufacture,
such as Mosaics, carvings in ivory, bone, tortoiseshell, &c., woodwork, furniture, &c. ; metal works, coins and medals,
arms and armour, silversmith’s work, jewellery, and goldsmith’s work, enamels on metal, earthenware and stoneware,
porcelain, glass vessels, &c. ; painted and stained glass, leather work, bookbinding, &c. ; textile fabrics and em-
broidery, lace, &c.
A NATIONAL GALLERY PICTURE.
An award of a special gold medal will be given for the best picture in oils exhibited. Judges will be selected
from the art galleries of Melbourne, Adelaide and Sydney.
NOTE.—The committee of the Bendigo Art Gallery are prepared to purchase a suitable picture exhibited in
this competition of which they approve, up to £lOOO.
THE EXHIBITION ART UNION.
A genuine Art Union will be conducted by the Exhibition Commission for the distribution of £lOOO worth of
pictures. The prizes will be pictures of the value of —Ist, £350 ; 2nd, £250 ; 3rd, £150; 4th, £100; sth, £5O ; 6th,
£25 ; 7th, £25 ; Bth, £l5 ; 9th, £lO ; 10th, £lO ; 11th, £lO ; 12th, £5. The winners of the various prizes must select
pictures to the value of the prize, and the committee will be responsible to the exhibitors for payment. The tickets
for the art union, in which there is no equivalent, are issued at ONE SHILLING each.
COMPETITIVE AMATEUR ART SECTION.
Prize of 20 guineas and diploma for the best set of three paintings in oils—(a) figure composition ; (b) nude figure
from life, not less than 30in. one way of canvas ; (c) head from life, life size. Second prize, £2 2s.
Prize of 20 guineas and diploma for the best set of three models in clay, wax, or metal— (a) decorative figure,
subject to be stated ; (6) figure in relief, or bust from life, or the antique ; (c) a decorative foliated panel, to be
stated. Second prize, £2 2s.
Prize of 20 guineas and diploma for the best landscape or maritime view in oils, not less than 60in. one way (sub-
ject to be selected in the States). Second prize, £2 2s.
Prize of 15 guineas and diploma for the best painting in water-colors, any subject. Second prize, £2 2s.
Prize of 5 guineas and diploma for best set of three studies of still life (in which flowers may be introduced) in
oils. Second prize, £2 2s.
Prize of 3 guineas and diploma for the best chalk drawing or monochrome of head or figure from life or from the
antique. Second prize, £1 Is. > .
Prize of £2 2s. and diploma for the best set of six pen-and-ink or wash drawings, suitable for reproduction.
THE WOMAN’S COURT.
A Woman’s Court, showing the progress in Women’s Work during the past century, with Competitive Sections
in all classes of work.
DIVISION V.—EDUCATION AND INSTRUCTION.
The primary education of children in the Public schools ; organisation, methods, and appliances for superior in-
struction ; exhibits of school work, maps, plans, drawings, &c. ; technical education in all its branches, as carried out
at the Victorian Working Men’s College ; printing, books, &c. ; stationery, bookbinding, painting, and drawing
material; musical instruments ; exhibits of work done at the Deaf, Dumb, and Blind Institutes ; curios, novelties,
jewellery, &c. ; philately ; domestic appliances ; toys, children’s games, &c. ; ladies’ fancy and needle work.
THE EXHIBITION CATALOGUE AND GUIDE BOOK.
A Picturesque Catalogue and Guide Book of Australia, of an attractive description, will be issued by the com-
mittee, and advertisements will be received by, and all information may be obtained from, Mr. W. S. Calvert, Little
Oollins-street, Melbourne; Reuter’s Telegram Company, in Sydney, Brisbane, and Melbourne; Mr. Powell, in Ade-
laide ; Messrs. Gordon and Gotch, in New Zealand and West Australia ; or Mr. G. V. Allen, Exhibition Offices,
Bendigo.
THE SPECIAL EXHIBITS and ATTRACTIONS will Include
The largest collection of Pictures, Statuary, Art Work, and Applied Art ever seen in Australia since the Centen-
nial Exhibition.
The Government dairy plant in daily work, with lectures on the dairy industry, fruit-preserving, and the freezing
of products.
A loom will be shown manufacturing flannels and blankets, from Messrs. Collins Rros.’ Union Mills, Geelong.
The latest mirror maze, brought especially from America.
An electric railway, carrying passengers round the gardens, through the various courts, over a route of 2500 ft.,
erected by G. Weymouth Propy., Ltd.
An Eiffel Tower, 150 ft. high, carrying passengers on an electric elevator to a look-out at the top, from which a
search-light will be shown at night, constructed by Messrs. Dorman, Long, and Co.
A Woman’s Court, showing the progress made in women’s work during the past century.
A golden Pyramid, illustrative of the era of the golden fifties, and representing 20,000,0000 z. of gold.
Underground workings, showing a saddle reef; quartz-mining and crushing. A shaft will be sunk in the
grounds, and a patent-safety cage will carry visitors to inspect the various drives, &c.
An armament court, with all the latest appliances of war.
South African war trophy, with special court exhibits of war material, captured by English and colonial
troops in the Transvaal, Free State, Natal, and Cape Colony.
Two band rotundas, in which military and string bands will play every afternoon and evening.
Fountain, rockeries, and waterfalls.
Gardens and pleasure-grounds.
Fern promenades and palm groves and grottoes.
Steam and electric motive-power, driving novelties.
An extensive dairy show will be held in the Exhibition-building on specified dates.
A special flower show will be held in the Exhibition-building on specified dates.
There will be organised a special regatta, under the auspices of the Exhibition Commission.
A special fruit and vegetable show will be held in the season.
A special wool show will be held, showing the best fleeces of all the various States.
Special marine and railway excursions will be organised for all the States.
A series of first-class concerts and musical carnivals will be arranged during the course of the Exhibition.
A bit of picturesque Old England, in which Shakespeare’s house will be shown.
An Australian scene, showing a bush camp and surroundings, will be exhibited ifi the grounds, under the control
of “ Scotty the Wrinkler,” of The Bulletin.
A butter trophy will be exhibited in connection with the dairy show.
There will be an ice trophy of frozen products.
Trophies of New South Wales and Victorian coal—black and brown—along with the products of brown coal sent
from Germany by Mr. J. Stirling.
A collection of native weapons from the interior of Australia and the Islands.
There will be an inter-State band contest on a large scale.
One of the most interesting features of the Exhibition will be an up-to-date Chinese Court and stand of arms,
captured by the Victorian Naval Brigade during the recent war in China.
A diamond drill at work.
Models of warships and the maritime navy will be exhibited.
A grain elevator will be worked in the produce section.
The printing press—old and new—at work in the machinery court.
A working laboratory from the Bendigo School of Mines, showing the latest scientific methods in the chemistry
of mining.
Every known system of electric power and lighting will be practically illustrated.
A mono-rail railway will be shown by Messrs. Bennie, Teare, and Co., similar to that now being constructed at
Birmingham, at a cost of £2,500,000.
At night the gardens and grounds and cool palm walks will be brilliantly lighted in colours by electricity, oil,
gas, and acetylene gas, to display the merits of various lighting powers.
There will be the most marvellous collection of machinery in motion ever seen together in the Commonwealth.
Early application should be made by intending exhibitors for space, to allow of proper provision being made in
the buildings, the arrangements in connection with which are now being carried out.
GEO. V. ALLEN, General Secretary.
Exhibition Offices, Market-square, Bendigo ; or Office of Melbourne Commission, Chamber of Manufactures, 30
Queen-street, Melbourne.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Deakin : “ ‘ A White Australia ’—that ii
the Monroe doctrine of the Commonwealth.”
There is an official hatred of the purely
Australian flag of the Southern Cross, because
Lalor flaunted it at the Eureka, where a police
man tore it down, and trampled on it.
Rupert Carington, J.P., with jurisdiction
over N.S.W. territory. Rupert Carington to be
M.L.C. presently. What is the N.S.W. Labor
party doing about the manual laborers ?
“ What this country wants is prudence and
economy and a big loan.”— Premier Seddon, oj
Maoriland, condensed. Seddon, in his old age. is
getting the loan disease as badly as the worst
boomer of them all. There is very little left now
of the old, self-reliant idea.
In the last two years the handful of
merchants in S. Aus. have paid over three times
more in income-tax than the 20,000 or 25,000
farmers. Go on the land, young man—and
“ graft ” for the octopus of the city.
Why should N.S. Wales go beyond its own,
or Australian, territory for a State Governor?
Take the case of Weld, who was a colonist,
Minister and Governor ; and take the case of Grey.
But, if there were no precedent, why not make
one ?
Long ago Australia had to face the ques-
tion—“ Shall we allow England to send convicts
to our country?” Australia’s answer was No.
Now, Australia has to face the question—“ Shall
we let England send niggers and Japanese to our
country ? ” And the answer will again be No.
Eighteen railway lines in N.S.W. don’t
pay their way. The total shortage for 1900 was
£324,230. Lines in the coastal districts were
responsible for £64,996; Central, £222,266; and
the West, where some misguided politicians seem
to think all the unprofitable lines are, £36,968.
The folly of “giving reasons ! ” Premier
Barton explained that one of the causes leading
to the choice of the selected Federal flag was
“ that it could be easily seen as a signal of dis-
tress.” Ominous that a nation on the threshold
of life should be reminded of the necessity of a
workable distress-signal.
Austin Chapman’s proposed Federal
members’ picnic in search of a Federal capital
is a transparent piece of poli-
tical mumming and might
be passed over with con-
tempt but that it will cost
the already-burdened Com-
monwealth thousands of
pounds. The selection of
a capital is really scien-
tific work. What does
the average member
know, when he stands
on a mountain and
looks round the hori-
zon, about the real
essentials of the
case? He has to
fall back on re-
ports, which he can
read just as well
in Melbourne. The
Chapman notion
rests on the same
basis as that politi-
cal fraud, the Public
Works Committee. Men
without a single scientific
qualification tour N.S.W.
and determine whether
this or that piece of
engineering shall be
undertaken, and return-
ing to Sydney, send in
a huge bill of expenses
which periodically
drives the country to
demand their extinction.
If it is a fact, as Premier Barton believes,
that under the Constitution no State Government
can make a Federal official pay income-tax, so
much the more reason for making the income-tax
a Federal impost. Then it will reach all classes,
and no privileged section of the community will
dodge its fair share of the public burdens.
“ X. 0.” has a good, useful theory :
The steel industry is one of the finest arguments in
favor of the railways. Questionable whether
any one State could support a State steel works, but
undoubtedly the federated States could. The bulked
rail, boiler-plate, bar and mild-steel needs of the railways
would be a first-class start for a federal steel plant,
situated where coal and iron-ore abounded. The demand
for steel for railway purposes and the private market
(with a proper duty imposed) would ensure such steel
works being a good Commonwealth investment.
‘ A.” writes from Adelaide :
onow’s Tram-Grab Bill will certainly pass S.A. Assembly
as the Country party—numbering nearly two-thirds of the
members and including most of the Ass. party—has
decided to vote solidly for this unspeakable scheme.
Premier Jenkins has now announced that his Ministry
will oppose second reading, but if defeated will support
the Bill with certain amendments, and this weak position
has disgusted democrats. The only unbending opponents
are._ the laborites, who will vote for nothing but
nationalisation.
O’Meara, of Pahiatua, proposed recently
in Maoriland House of Reps, that, by way of
assisting the birth-rate, every man with six chil-
dren should be entitled to a free land-grant. The
proposal was treated with scorn. The great diffi-
culty in all such cases is that the man and wife
who decided to make an effort to qualify would
never have any guarantee that they wouldn’t stick
fast at five, and there is no means by which they
can get something on account.
A request made in Queensland Parliament
for information as to the names of the persons
who held mortgages over the properties purchased
under the Closer Settlement Act made the Con-
tinuous Government very angry and full of plain-
tive cries of “Whaffor?” Of course, every-
body knew “Whaffor.” Inmost cases it would
almost certainly be found that the Q.N. Bank
held the mortgage. Somehow, no Queens-
land politician wants to be found anywhere near
the famous Q.N. Bank nowadays.
John Haynes’ bad luck with his N.S.W.
Abolition of Capital Punishment (in certain cases)
Bill sticks to him. Last time he submitted the
measure the Breelong blacks committed their
series of murders and other crimes, and, with half
the country seeking to slay them, the Bill got
short shrift. On this occasion the murder of
President M’Kinley was almost simultaneous
with the motion to introduce the Bill, and the
public detestation of such a crime will again make
itself felt in the fate of Haynes’ measure.
Casual remark from a recent S.M. Herald
leading article :
We have many interests to consider. ... We have on
one side N.S.W., wi h its thorough observance of Free-
trade laws, and at the other extreme we have Victoria
with its adherence to Protectionist doctrines.
Whatdaisies these Freetraders are! Fancy N.S.W'.,
with enormous protection on beer, wine, tobacco,
cigars, cigarettes, biscuits, confectionery, news-
papers, sugar, preserves, &c., as an example of
“thorough observance of Freetrade laws ” !
The decision of the N.S.W. Government
to start a factory of its own for the manufacture
of clothing used in the various State departments
is a good move, but it comes rather late. So
many departments—including the Defence branch
—have been taken over by the Federal Govern-
ment that now the State authorities don’t want
much more clothes than Adam, and the fact that
the few clothes which the State requires are made
under decent white-labor conditions will only
touch the uttermost fringe of the sweating evil.
“Bob Ridley”: Ever since, in a weak
moment, a member of the See Ministry said that
the next batch of N.S.W, Leg. Council appoint-
ments would probably include a couple of farmers’
representatives, the aforesaid Ministry has been
