From a report on the awful administration
of the Westralian railways : —-
A sale of rolling stock was said to have had unusual
features. Two locomotives and 28 trucks had been
bought by the Dept, from a contractor for £2980. After
hundreds of pounds had been expended in repairs, they
were all sold to another contractor for £1520. Subse-
quently one locomotive and 17 of the waggons were hired
by the Dept, from the purchaser, at the rate of £3 a day
for an engine that cost £550, and £45 per annum for
trucks for which they paid £2O each.
“ Unusual features” is surely too harsh a term to
apply to such a characteristically Westralian
transaction.
POLICE PROTECTION IN MELBOURNE.
(Premier Peacock and the Chief Commissioner point
out, in answer to a deputation, that the police are a fine
body of men, but they can’t he everywhere.)
PEACOCK : “ Certainly he’s not numerous, hut
he’s very fine and large.”
YOUNG VIC. : “ True; hut wouldn’t a smaller
policeman go a longer way?”
South Australia is just now suffering nadly
from swelled head. This is due chiefly to S.A.
Advertiser, which loses no opportunity of im-
pressing upon its readers that S.A. has sent by
far the strongest team to the Federal Parliament.
In fact many S.A. people now believe that S.A.
runs the whole show—that the other representa-
tives don’t count. The slightest advantage gained
by a S.A. Rep. is hugely glorified, as are also the
anaemic jokes of State-humorist M‘Gregor. The
defeat by S.A. of the N.S.W. lacrosse team and
the Vic. footballers has lent fuel to the flame and
accentuated the poor opinion entertained by S.A.
concerning the rest of the Oommonwealth.
If the 48 Rich Landlords of Victoria had
any sense of humor, or even of common logic,
they would raise a row that would soon shift the
Peacock Ministry off the Treasury bench. Ever
since Turner imposed an income-tax running as
high as Is. 4d. in the £ on unearned incomes, the
Man with a Stake in the Country has been de-
claiming against his grievous burdens, and now
the allegedly needy Peacock proposes an income-
tax of six shillings in the £ on the very poorest
and most helpless of Victoria’s citizens —for that
is what the reduction of Old Age Pensions
amounts to.
The De Beers Mining Corporation lately
handed the British Govt, its little bill for ex-
Eenses (£300,000) incurred in defending Kim-
erley. But even the Tory Govt, said it would
like the opinion of the Commons on certain of the
items. Some of them: £7BB, for native runners
bringing Mr. Rhodes’ newspapers from Modder
River; £25 for carrying a private letter from Mr.
Rhodes to Mafeking; and £l9 10s. for a wreath
laid on an Imperial officer’s grave ! The House
held its nose with both hands and streamed out
into the lobbies to use language about the patriot-
ism of this impudent gang.
Perth Morning Herald is of opinion that
it might be good to have only one House in the
Westralian State Parliament, as is the case with
six out of the eight divisions of Canada, but says
that “ public opinion is not yet ripe for the
change.” How would it be to take a popular
vote, and find out whether opinion is ripe or not ?
The saddest wail of the lazy and incompetent
legislator, who knows what he should do, but is
too tired or too scared to do it, is ‘ ‘ the time is not
yet ripe.” The fiction that time is a thing which
grows on a tree, and is hard and green in its early
stages, was invented to enable dull men to look
wise, and cowardly men to look as if they backed
down from the noblest motives.
An enthusiastic Melbourne pressman :
The more one sees of the Federal members of both
Federal Houses, the stronger is the conviction that
“ Dick ” O’Connor stands head and shoulders above them
all as a leader of men. Quiet, urbane, straightforward,
he has the backing of a reserve force that would shake
mountains. He is never crass. Sensible suggestions
from the other side are accepted with thanks, and he
wastes no time fighting hopeless battles. His speeches
are models of brevity and clearness, and his interjections
few and cutting. O’Connor is worth a dozen such as
Barton, whose speeches only tend to confuse the matter
at issue. It seems impossible to imagine that this man
was elected by the same people who put in Gould, Puls-
ford, Millen, Walker, and Neild; and, if the fiscal issue
was responsible, N.S.W. Protectionists should thank God
they are not of the same intelligences as the Foreign-
traders. O’Connor should be in the Reps., at the head of
the Government or thereabouts.
N.S.W. agreed that its soldiers in S'Africa
should receive the amazing wage (for a soldier) of
4s. fid. a day, and, as the British Government sup-
plied Is. ?d., this meant that N.S.W. had to put
up the other 3s. 4d. But the contingenters trucu-
lently demand ss. Bd.—4s. 6d. from N.S.W. in
addition to the British Government’s fourteen-
pence—and Premier See, fearful, perhaps, lest
some small boy should call out “pro-Boer” at
him in the street, has decided to pay up, at the
country’s expense, on the ground that “there was
a misconception.” It would be interesting to
know what would happen if Mr. See, in his private
capacity, hired a man at 30s. a week, and the man
had a misconception that he ought to get 375. 6d.,
and wrote to the papers about it.
There was a time when the cane
sugar-industry in N.S.W. was a purely
white-labor industry. It is now steadily
becoming a nigger industry, and the tale
that “ white men can’t work in the cane-
fields ” begins to be heard along the
northern rivers. Also, it is beginning
to be explained that white men won't
work in the fields, and that, in any
case, the industry can’t pay white men’s
wages. The facts that white men did
work there without difficulty and wil-
lingly enough, when the work was avail-
able, and that the industry did pay
white men’s wages, and that sugar didn’t
go to any outrageous price by-
reason of these circumstances, are
gradually getting into the back-
ground, and in time they will get
so far back that they will only be
discernible per telescope.
If is nearly time that Premier
See hustled in re N.S.W. Civil Ser-
vice Superanni ation Fund. Income
in 1900 was £40,034, and out-
goings £126,291, so the fund
decreased £86,257. The balance
(£212,163) will just about keep
things going till the end of 1902, and
then the aged ex-civil servant will
have to make a bee-line for the
benevolent asylum. Apparently this little item
will mean another £90,000 a year or so for the
suffering country to pay. Bygone Premiers who
wanted to retrench sacked public servants whole-
sale, and, instead of paying them whatever was
due out of the revenue, “ put them on the Fund.”
It made the revenue look better at the time, but
every curse comes home to roost one day or an-
other, and some curses go to bed very early.
“ Tyranny of the most despotic kind it
would certainly be to fine or imprison a man
because he refused to work at a rate of wages
which he thought to be too low, if he had not
agreed beforehand to do so,’’says Ashton, M.L. A.,
in N.S.W. Assembly. That shows an entire mis-
conception of the situation. By assenting to the
Compulsory Arbitration Bill, by working to make
it an Act, by threatening Government and Oppo-
sition with political extinction if the Act be not
speedily forthcoming, the workmen most solemnly
agree beforehand to accept the rate of wages fixed
by the Arbitration tribunal. A mere lawyer’s
agreement between man and man is a bit of flimsy
when compared with the charter between class
and class which the Act will establish.
Of the six seats in Victoria’s House of 48
Rich Landlords vacated the other day by effluxion
of time, not one was contested ! Nary a one of the
six obese nonentities went near his constituents,
or even condescended to address them per news-
paper, yet when they throw out the State Consti-
tution Convention Bill shortly all will point ta
their victories as proof they really represent the
electors. By the way, a question lately elicited
the pleasing information that only 1900 persons in
the State are eligible to sit in the House of Land-
lords. And yet, if Peacock proposed to restrict
the represemtatiomof the people in the Assembly
to the 3700' woocren-legged men or the 3900 red-
haired men in the State, how the flabby °’des of
Aggregate Fat would roll with laughter !
Every Tory or interested politician who
urges S.A. to job away the Adelaide tramway
rights to the Snow syndicate points out with
enthusiasm that the State will have the power of
purchase after 21 years (just as if the whole text
wasn’t the impossibility and foolishness and un-
workableness of State ownership), and the co. will
agree to surrender the lines free of charge after 55-
years. Of course it will —if it doesn’t get its lease
renewed for, another 25 or 40 years or so by the-
votes of tr'amway-shareholders in Parliament.
Long before 55 years are up it will almost certainly
happen that a lot of the shares will be distributed
in the right place to secure an extension. In
fact, it is pretty certain that a good number are-
distributed in the right place already. If an adroit
pickpocket could only “go through” some of the
patriotic gentlemen who are supporting the Snow
syndicate he might possibly find a whole lot of
their motives in an inside pocket.
Extract from the Federal debate on the
restriction of colored immigration :
Mr. Cameron (Tasmania), said that, looking at the
matter calmly and dispassionately, he felt they ought not
to do anything that would lead to a disruption of the
relations between Great Britain and Japan and
Afghanistan. Therefore he believed in the admission of a
certain number of aliens.
That certainly seems reasonable. If Japan and
Afghanistan are of any use to Britain then the ad-
mission of a certain number of Afghan and
Japanese aliens (to Britain) is a fair thing. Let
them be admitted by all means. It would be-
darned foolishness to disturb the friendly relations
over a little thing like that. But it is much more
important to preserve the friendly relations
between Australia and Britain, and if the Asiatic
insists on coming here the end of those friendly
relations isn’t far off. By the way, the Federal
Govt, admits that it has received a message from
Chamberlain imploring it not to be too rough
on the Jap., as’his co-operation is likely to be very
useful to J. Bull.

iHE STATEMENT that the JEolian will
play any piece of music ever composed
is so broad and sweeping that it many
times fails to impress the reader with
its wonderful significance. As a means
of affording a better idea of the enormous
repertoire of the ASolian we present herewith
partial list of the works of a single composer,
BEETHOVEN, that are included in the HUolian
music catalogue.
These are all arranged from the full orchestral
scores, complete and unabridged. Many of them
are never attempted upon the piano, and are played
only by large orchestras ; yet it is possible for any
one to play them on the Aiolian correctly, and with
all the changes of tempo and expression.
Visitors always welcome. If unable to call, send for
catalogue C, giving full description.

VITADATIO
—i In Doing.
Unsolicited Testimony
from Fiji.
Three Doctors Failed to Diagnose his
Disease, hut VITADATIO cured him.
Levuka, Fiji, January 8, 1900.
S. A. PALMER, Agent for VITADATIO.
Dear Sir, —At the time your letter, with invoiee for
case of VITADATIO arrived, I was on my back under the
doctor and his knife, so I was unable until now to attend
to any business. I previously wrote to you for one bottle
of medicine, and informed you that I had been for months
suffering from a succession of abscesses in the groin in-
capacitating me from activity. Experiencing some slight
effect from the one bottle, I sent for a case, but prior to
its arrival I was so seriously ill, and fearing the abscesses
might break inwardly, I sent for a medical man,
who commenced cutting me. Two days after his
first operation I received the case of VITADATIO, and
without informing the doctor, I commenced taking it,
but I could not send the medico away as I had so far
submitted to his operations. Within three weeks he
operated on me three times, as he put me under chloro-
form, and worked at me for an hour, but, as he confessed,
without much hope of success. As neither he or two
other medical men of repute could diagnose my disease,
medicine was prescribed and supplied, but I took only the
VITADATIO. The doctor was surprised ; he could not
get much pus after opening me. Three days after the
first opening, and about ten days after I had commenced
taking VITADATIO, nearly a small cup of pus passed
from me, and daily for four days small quantities ; then
commenced the healing process, and the medico was
delighted, saying he was much surprised at the rapid
decrease in the swelling’of the gland, which he had cut,
and proclaimed on several occasions that his medicine
was producing the cure. I asked him if he really thought
it was the medicine or his operations, and he replied,
“The medicine has done you more good than any opera-
tions, continue taking it for some time,” and he then
asked me if I did not remember how it commenced doing
me good from the very first time I took it. I have not yet
told him I threw his medicine away and took only VITA-
DATIO, but I purpose dping so shortly. lam now, after
seven months severe illness, feeling well and daily getting
stronger, and I am sujre it is the VITADATIO that has
produced the present state. I have been for 30 years in
Fiji; my system was run down ; I am well advanced in
life and I am convinced' that I am (apart from untoward
circumstances) likely to enjoy renewed health again for
some time. Some of my friends look upon my case as a
very strong testimony to the healing properties of your
medicine, and I agreedwith them. I am obtaining per-
mission from the Government to sell VITADATIO, and
when this is obtained, will order from you such quantities
as I think may be sold.
I have written you fully, as I think you should be
pleased to hear the result of a bona-fide cure.—Yours
faithfully, (Signed) ALEX. EASTGATE.
For further <j particulars ,
S. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street , Sydney.

A pleasing respondent was mentioned m
Melb. Divorce Court lately. When served with
the citation for divorce the lady danced a cheerful
jig, carolled jovously “I’ve got a free leg.once
more,” slapped her husband’s face with the papers,
and expectorated on him three several times —for
luok.
An English ex-soldier has been charged at
Brentford with stealing milk from a cow. He
was caught lying down catching the milk in his
mouth, and pleaded hunger as his excuse. This
may be a tip for the returned cohentingenter—
when he is tired of trying to milk the State cow
for a job on the tramways.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for hirh not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received frbm
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only .claim’to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if-you allow the disease to run riot in your systenij you
afe guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration toy Government
Medical Boards of New South. Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Great Britain
and America Open to Inspection by Dr. Wallace, our Physician-in-Chief.
Late Consulting Staff, Homeopathic Hospital, Melbourne.

HOME TREATMENT.
—lf you liveout of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive
you of the benefits of our Wonderful treatment, because in the new
system of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro- Medical borne treatment is provided for. Scarcely a
day passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a per-
sonal visit could not be made; Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining your feelings and symptoms as
clearly as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of
writing, and twopence postage to get the opinion and advice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.
DO NOT BE DRUGGED TO DEATH BY QUACKS
When there are so many quacks and impudent pretenders in the field of Medicine, who arrogate to themselves 1
the monstrosity of giving counsel and advice in matters of which they are profoundly ignorant, and who have
nothing to recommend them but their audacity, sufferers cannot be too careful whom they consult. Although
liberty of choice belongs to all, discrimination is necessary. In consulting THE FREEMAN AND WALLACE •
ELECTRO-MEDICAL AND SURGICAL INSTITUTE patients may feel assured that they shall receive the advice
of those fitted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and unparalleled
success as Specialists entitle us to your confidence.

“Caught a microbe ’s morning,” said
Macgillycuddy to a friend, as his usual train
steamed out of the station—“ made me late.” He
hauled a small pill-box out of his waistcoat pocket,
opened it carefully and handed it over for inspec-
tion —the microbe reposed in the bottom of it.
His friend had seen the species before; Towzer
used to scratch them hurriedly off his stomach on
to the front door-mat. “Female typhoid germ,”
said Mac, sententiously, “ grown to enormous
di-mensions. Pretty dead, isn’t she? Tillin-
ghast’s,” he added, with the air of one giving the
countersign. “ Why, if you just say ‘ Tillinghast’
in earshot of those germs, they curl up and die at
once. Seen ’em do it, down to weeniest, most
invisible little beggars invented. Kills smells,
too, without putting up a bigger one of its own ;
has none—and no color. Never struck such grand
stuff as Tillinghast’s Disinfectant. Got it at the
usual place—M'Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., Mel-
bourne.”*

NEW YORK NOVELTY CO,
X7lB George-street, SYDNEY.
H. L. HUTCHINSON. Manager.
New Zeala”'! Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
report, for week ended Sept. 14th, that marsupial skins
lacked animation, and heavy grades gave way slightly.
Medium and light weights show little or no change, and
’possum skins were in great demand and sometimes sold
at 3d. per dozen higher. The inquiry for sheepskins was
decidedly strong, and although no increase can be re-
ported the market showed a distinctly upward tendency.
Hides were in only moderate supply, and demands for
desirable parcels were free—for N.S.YV. parcels especially ■
prices firm. Tallow is in keen demand, and the co. dis’
posed of a fair-sized catalogue at an advance of 15s. per
ton. The private demand for merino wools is very strong,
and sale prospects are good ; but the recent rains having
retarded shearing, it is not anticipated that much of the
new clip will be offered on the 17th and 18th. London
cables that a slight improvement is anticipated in cross-
breds and about 5 per cent, advance on merinos.
“ The Weak Spot,"
If you are sitting in a
restaurant or in a shop
where waitresses or lady
clerks are in attendance,
you will note, now and
again, some young woman
will put her hand to her
back and straighten herself
up, while her lips are
tightened as if by pain.
It’s backache ; yet all day
long she must be on her
feet, lifting, carrying,
hurrying. That weak spot,

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A.J.C. Spring meet was a great and terrible
day of slump to the people of the Noses. On Derby and
Metrop. days they had a balance to the good on one race
Son A™ leviathan “ Oxy ” is said to have disgorged
£zu,ooo, and a prominent Vic. layer swore it was the worst
day he d struck at Rand wick for 30 years. The bulk of it
we j?t into the pockets of a small lucky or knowing crowd,
and the general public only had a rather thicker shower
of crumbs. The g.p. never wins anything to rave about.
Altogether, Mo. felt so badly about it that he’d no
heart to push peeziness on Caulfield and Melb. Cups.
♦ ♦ * £
A.J.C. trifles: Hautvillers’Derby was the first
won by Baile3’*s absolutely own nag ; he was only in-
terested in Camoola and Stromboli. Horse and owner
have never met, and Mr. Bailey has not been seen on a
course for years. The premier sire of this spring’s
meeting was Gozo with four wins (San Fran three and
Gauleon one). The winning trainer was Tom Pay ten, with
five victories. Of the 35 Metropolitans run the majority
have been won by 4-year-olds : Bylong (5.7), Amendment
(5.8), and Lamond (6.4), in ’66, ’77, and ’BB respectively,
were the three-year-olds who won it, and why Scobie
started Hautvillers (a three-year-old) for it with 7.12, or
221 b. more than Lamond carried, is a mystery. The
public followed jockey Kuhn’s mounts as the Huguenots
followed the white plume of Navarre. Where he rode
went the public wealth, and out of 24 races he won nine,
including Epsom Handicap, Spring Stakes, Metrop., New
Stakes, Duff Memorial Stakes, and Plate. He affects the
Tod Sloan style; and far behind him trailed Regan,
Lewis, Harris, and Godby with two wins each.
* • * #
Lepper Linden in the Second Hurdles at
Randwick, after parting with his jock, jumped the four
remaining hurdles, flashed past the winner on the flat,
and ran the thing right out in first class style—right in
front.
• • • •
At the Bathurst picnic races last April Bill
Kelso took Melossi up to show the natives a thing or two,
but the bumpkins had a rod in pickle which they called
Lucknow. Kelso afterwards bought the rod, and at
Randwick Spring meeting the way-back nag whipped in
a couple of wins for his new owner.
* * • 9
San Fran’s running last week gives Wakeful a
mighty good chance for one or other of the Cups. Wake-
ful, in April last year, was trying to give the son of Gozo
121 b. when he finished a length and a-half behind him for
the Sydney' Oup. Two days previously he had won Don-
caster Handicap in a canter, carrying 7.10. Wakeful, at
8.10, looks well handicapped for Melb. Cup.
• * * »
Some of the cross-headings to racing reports in
the dailies are a positive encouragement to in-and-out
performances. Take such a heading as “ Hautvillers
and Grasspan in form.” This appeared above Melb. Agb’s
report of the third day’s racing at Randwick. Said Haut-
villers had won the Derby four days earlier, and Grass-
pan meanwhile had reversed his Derby’ running with
Caravel. Was there any special reason for going into
hysterics over the fact that those two horses were in
“ form ” ?
* * * *
Ancient 12-year-old WTiakawatea, winner of
Kensington Stakes at Randwick, looks good enough for
another year or two. He started as a two-year-old, and
won nine out of 20 starts. At the end of last year, out of
151 starts, he had scored 26 wins and a big list of
“ drums.” Of his 11 seasons his seventh was the only
unfruitful one. An equine hero.
* » . *
Re luck : A man who was pretty well mixed
went, the other day, to Randwick, and gave a friend
what he thought were four £1 notes to put on Gauleon at
10 to 1. Instead of giving £1 notes he accidentally gave
tenners, with the result that he drew £4OO. Wherefore
he made £360 by being drunk. Sobriety may be a good
thing, but even it can be bought too dear.
* * • *
A Sydney suburban medico bad the Sequence-
San Fran double going for £lOOO. Last year he backed
the same double for £2OOO, and the year previous to that
he won a Tatt.’s sweep.
* * * *
Ritualist, the S. A. Derby winner, is a well-
named colt. He is by The Englishman out of Convent.
* • * *
Ten starters for the Spring Handicap at
S.A.J.C. meeting t’other Saturday, and the winner paid
a tote div. of £36. Otherwise it was a backers’ day, seeing
that the average div. on the other five winners was £6
155., the lowest being £3 35., and the highest something
under £lO. In Melbourne or Sydney a backers’ day at a
small meeting means an average winning price of 2 to 1
or thereabouts. And then there is the labor of rushing
from Ikey to Ikey to get “ the best of the market.”
* * * •
Patronage, if he hadn’t gone out with the first
scratchings, might have proved as hot a pot for Melb.
Cup as was Ruenalf in ’94, when their full brother,
Patron, took the prize. The Grand Flaneur—Olga stock
evidently improves with age. Patron was not a genuine
stayer until he got into his fourth year, Ruenalf showed
his best form as a 5-year-old, and Patronage was still a
maiden when he ran San Fran a close race for the Metrop.
By the way, the Melb. handicappers showed their wisdom
by giving Patronage 91bs. and lOlbs. more in Caulfield and
Melbourne Cups than the 6.11 he carried in the Metrop.
But for which precaution he would probably have been
among the acceptances for both events.
* • * *
Late Mr. Forrester’s stud was dispersed the
other day, and gave the idea that the possible wiping out
of Tatt.’s sweeps was likely to send promoter George
Adams back to the turf. He bought 14 lots, including
brood mares Charming, Rita, Rose Flaneur, Saraswati,
Spray, and Uproar; a yearling brother to Aquarius for
205g5., a two-year-old gelding by Gaulus from Chaplet for
255g5., Glenister (another two-year-old Gaulus gelding)
for 115g5., and four more by the same sire, with Rosary,
dam of Calculus, Aquarius, Chaplet, &c. Mr. Roberts, of
Murrumburrah, N.S.W., took stallion Niagara for 115 gs.
The Watchdog started at 250g5., and soared up to 410 gs.
before he fell to “J. North.” Later, George Adams
resold Carnegie and Glenister to Tucka Tucka Jack
Smith.
* * * •
“ Tattersall’s ” 10 per cent, commission last year
totalled nearly £50,000, the public having sent in nearly
half-a-million sterling. The decadence of gambling !
* • * •
Parson Woollnough, in Tas. Assembly t’other
day, made out a good case in favor of the Govt, impound-
ing “ Tattersall ” unclaimed prizes, and dealing with the
amounts similarly to moneys of the Perpetual Trustees
Co., intestate persons, and the like, but the House was
unsympathetic. It transpired during the debate that, in
respect of lotteries drawn in 1898, prizes amounting to
£3918 remained unclaimed after a year and a-half, but,
owing to the efforts made by Tatt.’s manager, prompted
by Tas. Auditor-General, the unclaimed money did not
now amount to much over £lOOO a year. Wooll-
nough’s motion for a Govt, trust fund was. negatived—
partly because a paltry £lOOO a year wasn’t considered
important enough to warrant special looking after, and
partly because the mover valiantly refrained from includ-
ing unclaimed bank-balances in the same category. The
average Tas. legislator doesn’t quite know whether he
loves Tatt. or the banks best.
* * • *
Satan recently had an idle hour on hand and
prompted someone to resurrect the long dead N.S.W. and
Victorian inter-State pigeon -shooting' matches. N.S.W.
won by 38 to 34. Three on the winning side slaughtered
their full number, while only champion A. E. Smith
did so for Victoria. Hard to say who was most dis-
credited, winners or losers. Pigeon-shooting is a very
low-down game. .
* * * •
Latest champion pigeon butcher, “Locksley,”
is of the Neeld family of Wyalong goldfield pioneers.
Last week, after winning championship, he made another
record by murdering 30 consecutive birds from 34 yards,
and, all told, had a run of 42 kills from 28 and 34 yards
before letting one go. Imagine a man who kills little
pigeons at 34 yards with a modern shotgun having the
impudence to call himself “ Locksley ” —after the great
outlaw who is said to have split willow wands with a
cloth-yard shaft at 200 yards !
* » * *
Westralian “Pug” Pike and bruiser Denny
Molony gave a fearful and bounding battle at Sydney
Gaiety A.C. last week before an enthusiastic audience.
They flung their arms and legs anyhow and anywhere,
and then they heaved both their selves up and flung them
at one another. Sometimes they bumped on the posts
and round the ring till the momentum was exhausted,
when they gathered themselves up and rushed at each
other again with eyes shut fast and mouths wide open.
It looked. as though the game was to swallow each other.
Pike did indeed clinch and hang on—it was the only way
to escape Denny’s whirling smites that were falling
like a hail of steam-hammers ; and then Denny got down
low and romped wildly round the show in the hope of
running over “ Pug.” Mouths got displaced, ears were
shifted, and both men’s heads began to swell till their
sagging knees could hardly carry them. When they were
reduced to the limpness of baggy pants, the “ coppers ”
interfered, and the referee called it a draw, though Pike’s
anatomy was not quite so inextricably mixed up as that
of Denny. As both are thirsting for revenge, another
meeting is to be arranged, at which it is fondly hoped all
previous geographical marks will be finally and utterly
obliterated.
* * * •
A light-weight pug. who promises to sweep
through his class in Australia is Jock MTvor, the Port
Melb. lad who knocked out clever Tot Higgins t’other
night. MTvor, who is a wonder at both taking and
giving, started fighting about 18 months ago when he had
no science and was pitted against the very small fry of
his weight. He now knocks out a man of champion class
and challenges the miniature South Melb. giant Jack
M’Gowan. MTvor, being Scotch, should (unlike the
typical pug. of Irish blood) make some ultimate profit
out of his cleverness.
* • • •
Amateur boxing threatens to boom in Mel-
bourne. At the recent reception given by the Melbourne
Athletic and Rowing clubs to the officers of the Italian
warship Puglia, one enthusiast offered to put up £2O for
middle and light-weight amateur trophies. Lesser bids
followed, and over £5O Was promised from good marks.
* • • •
Football never had a bigger grip on Melbourne
than at the tail-end of this season, and on the Saturday
evening after Oollingwood lost its chance of the premier-
ship to Essendon the democratic suburb was temporarily
“ broke.” It had risked its last sprat and missed the
mackerel, and local puhs.-were deserted as a result of the
“motherless” condition of the electors, while shop-
keepers stood at their doors and cursed the 20 whose bad
play had driven capital out of the suburb.
• • *
M‘Laren’s cricket-team will very soon consist
of the'men who aren’t coming. Fry, the leading batsman
this season, and Foster, have, now decided to stay at home.
M’Laren, who has been a great frost himself this year,
will presently have to approach Murdoch, Spofforth, Tom
Richardson, and other old-timers who have reached the
ballet stage of toothlessness.' Melb. 0.0. must be medi-
tating seriously over the possibilities of a frost, and even
now people begin to wager .that the team won’t come at
all. ,
* ,
Cycling records are falling like mercury in a
southerly buster. Pulford (hon. sec. N.S.W. Cyclists’
Union) lowered Arthur Smyth’s Australasian record for
five miles, from 10.461-sth to 10.21. Last lap was cut out
in 35sec. on a machine geared to 103 with 7in. cranks.
* • * •
S. F. Edge, who represented England in the
big continental motor-car races, says his own oar is
geared up to 103 miles per hour at 10,000 revolutions per
minute, but, unfortunately, he can’t get more than 80 miles,
because the car isn’t heavy enough and presumably wants
to fly. With a car weighing 20001bs. 40 miles per hour is
easy, but to touch 60 the car must be twice as heavy to
keep down. At 50 miles per hour travelling is fairly
comfortable, but at 65 the wind is unbearable ; and the
horses along the road, who don’t mind a car flashing past
at 50 miles per hour, go mad with fright when one whisks
by at 65.
• • • *
The fifth edition of the “Book of Billiards,”
issued by Alcock and Co., is about as generally instruc-
tive a. thing as any amateur can hope for. It lectures on
the history and ethics of the game, and teaches all that
can be learned from print re the best means whereby a’
“mug” player may develope into an artist. The greater
the mug the more wrinkles he will gather from this
Alcockian volume, but even the ordinary capable practi-
tioner who thinks himself well-versed in the fundamental
principles of the “ higher game ” is probably ignorant of
many simple points here explained to him. As for the
fact that all other games—outdoor or indoor—are inferior
to billiards in health-giving properties, it ought to be
clear to the foggy understanding of the puffiest capitalist.
Nevertheless, it is worth while to remind every dyspeptic
person, with lots of money and no billiard table, that an
hour’s play involves a three-mile walk and a splendid
assortment of mild athletic exercises for the arms, body,
and wits of the performer. Alcock’s cables on their
average merits are the best English billiard tables in the
world, and their excellence dates from as far back as 1860.
* * * *
The frame of a Massey-Harris bicycle is built
up of selected Mannessman seemless steel tubing, and
the front forks are specially tested before being embodied
in the machine. This is why the Massey-Harris bicycle
will withstand the wear-and-tear of constant riding over
the roughest roads.
• • * •
The Dunlop road - race, to be held from
Warrnambool to Melbourne, next October, is having a
beneficial and stimulating effect on road-riding through-
out Australasia. New Zealand, New South Wales, South
Australia, Tasmania, and Western Australia are all hold-
ing qualifying test-races to select their respective repre-
sentatives, the Dunlop Tyre Co. having arranged to defray
the expenses of the selected inter-State representatives
who visit Melbourne to compete in the big Warrnambool
event. Entries close at the Dunlop Tyre Company’s
offices, 108 Flinders-street, Melbourne, on Saturday, the
31st inst., at 12 noon.
At the Last Moment.
When this issue went to press Australia was still
awaiting definite information about the British
Government’s new attitude on the Nigger Exclu-
sion Question. This is the present situation :
For a number of years the British Government has sys-
tematically vetoed Bills which professed to keep out the
nigger simply because the two races didn’t mix to
advantage. But it has systematically assented to Bills
which kept him out obliquely per medium of an educa-
tional test. The educational test has come to be regarded
as the British Government’s settled policy.
Therefore the Federal Immigration Restriction Bill was
based on the educational test, as usual. Experience has
shown that when this test is applied with sufficient energy
it can keep out almost every Asiatic that was ever born.
Australia has already largely adopted this device, so the
Federal Government simply followed the existing track
of Australian precedent.
Now it is reported by cable that, at the dictation of the
truculent and overbearing Japanese person, who seems
to be running the show just now, a Bill passed by the
British Columhian Legislature, “imposingan educational
test to exclude Chinese and Japanese and forbidding
their employment on certain works,” has been vetoed.
Now Australia wants to find out by the promptest
Eossible cablegram whether this Bill was vetoed
ecause it contained the educational test, or
because it forbade the employment of Japanese
on “certain works.” If it was the latter the
position hasn’t altered much. If the former, then
it seems that the British Government, which weak-
ened over direct exclusion many years ago, has now
gone back on the educational test also. In that
case the situation is so completely changed that
the Federal Parliament can’t be too prompt in
throwing out the educational test and adopting
the proposal of Watson and M'Millan that the
Immigration Restriction Bill shall simply shut
out every Asiatic and African in sight—shut him
out flatly and peremptorily without asking any
questions whatever. If there is one kind of
restriction that can’t be got without a fight, while
another and almost equally efficacious kind can, it
is policy to accept the latter, in order to save
time. But, if it is impossible to get either kind
without a fight, then we may as well fight for the
most definite and uncompromising kind.

Victorian item :
Four live rabbits were found nailed by their feet to a
deal table in the hut, and appeared to have been placed
there by the boy for amusement.
The boy in question has already stuck-up an hotel,
and has been under the fire of the police. He
seems to have a glowing future before him.

At Poverty Point.
One of the Sydney Palace comedians has his teeth set
with diamonds. They would be handy for chewing glass
with ; but, then, he isn’t that sort of comedian. What
would be a real gift on the ever-drunk variety of
“ artiste” is thrown away in this case.
Georgia Hodson, widow of the late William Saurin
Lyster, died in Melbourne last week.
Tyrone Power made the most strenuous effort to knock
Melbourne with “Nadjezda.” T.P. is said to have in-
herited something like £30,000 since coming to Australia,
and he put a lump of it into Barrymore’s drama. Mount-
ing, painting, dresses, etc., are all done “regardless,”
and the actor-manager went to the extreme with full-
column advts. in each of the dailies.- A full-col. advt. is
probably not worth a cent more than a Jcol. in the
“Amusements” department; however, Power was
“going it.” But “ Nadjezda” is a rather disappointing
play. There’s a heap of good materials in it, but other
people seem to have made good plays of them.
Gerardy, the celebrated violoncellist, has unearthed at
Wellington, M.L., a 14-year-old lad, who, he prophesies,
will be a world’s wonder on the ’cello. Gerardy has
now engaged to return to Auckland, later on, and take
the youth with him to Europe for the best possible
training. ■-
The Bulletin’s London correspondent: The slender
and attractive Hilda Spong that Australia once knew has
grown into an imposing woman of fine figure and stately
carriage. After a trip to “ mommer ” and “ poppa,” who
love London and won’t leave it, she has returned to U.S.
to star on tour in “ Lady Huntworth’s Experiment.”
Hilda has unmistakably “ struck ore ” thick, and should
yet go much further and fare better, thanks to judicious
parental tutoring. London managers can’t even guess a
good thing, but the ’Murkan scents and tracks it out.
“ A.0.H.” : “ Jim ” (8., 29/7/’l) says “ You don’t find a
Dolores referring to a piece of music as she goes along.”
Now, in Adelaide, Mdlle. Dolores did so, and she told an
interviewer that when singing modern ballads she invari-
ably took the words with her—owing to the verbal
resemblance in sentimental songs.
Painfully “previous” par. from British Australasian,
Aug. 10 : “ Miss Carrie Moore, who was married at Sydney
on June 27 to Mr. E. Tyson, comes to London shortly to
try her luck at the Gaiety Theatre, where she believes
that Mr. George Edwardes will give her a chance. If he
should not, the popular young Australian is now wealthy
enough to bear such a disappointment with equanimity.”
“ Message from Mars ” recalls to mind an Australian
“dream’’-play which George Leitch wrote many years
ago. It was produced in Sydney—title, “ A Touch of the
Sun.” Leitch fell asleep in the first act, and dreamed
that he had a gory time, murdering his friends^and rela-
tives. The third act found him asleep, like Parker in
“The Message,” and Rosette in “ Ma Mie Rosette,” and
old Dick in “ Uncle Dick’s Darling,” and other stage
visionaries.
Pollard’s opera co., in Maoriland, has scored a record
run with “ Florodora.” The musical-comedy puts up
records everywhere, because it has a charm which even
the tricky W. S. Gilbert forgot to introduce into the more
humorous and infinitely more musical Gil.-Sul. operas—
the charm of a widely-distributed personal interest.
Gilbert gave all the “ fat ”to the principals. The chorus
never scored a notable success unless at least one of the
principals was present to take the encore. In “ Floro-
dora ” twelve minor characters make the biggest hit of
the show with a double sestet, composed on the co-
operative principle. The charming “ Don’t go ” chorus
in “ lolanthe ” is called to mind by “Tell me, pretty
maiden,” but there the girls had the music and two of the
men principals figured in the foreground at either side of
the stage, with two principal fairies appealing to them.
Edwin Geach goes into active management again right
away, catching on with Arnold’s co. immediately Arnold
lights out. The reformed co. will trail “ The Wrong Mr.
Wright ” all over the face of the Commonwealth. Lohr
goes into private life with the intention of opening a pub.
Good luck to Lohr and the profits 1
The Bulletin’s London correspondent:—Who can
remember the Cheevers and Kennedy team who toured
Australia in the seventies? Well, Joe 0., young and
sturdy to-day, with his wife—who probably displays an
album of “ notices ” from Austral papers, many of them
long since dead—run a Strand hostelry much frequented
by pros, and pro-esses. Comic-song writer and singer,
Walter Keen, of “Up went the price of meat sky-high”
fame, forced back home to Tassy by ill-health, is being
benefited at Brixton Empress. His brother was the dead
Lance Lenton. “An extremely robust Australian ban-
tone,” Hamilton Hill, is raising the root at the Empire.
The Lonnen benefit at London Gaiety Theatre realised
£453, and a subscription list totalled something like
£2OO.
“ Oldtimer ” : I must protest that Wilson Barrett’s
advt. of his “ Silver King” show makes me squirm. It
doesn’t condescend to mention the names of the authors
of the famous melodrama with which the “ eminent actor-
manager ” had the luck (and the judgment) to be iden-
tified 20 years ago. It repeats eight times the large-
typed line —“ Wilson Barrett in The Silver King.” Then
comes “ Wilson Barrett as Wilfred Denver,” followed
by “ Lillah M‘Carthy as Nellie Denver, and Wilson Bar-
rett’s Complete London Company.” Ten repetitions of
W.B.’s name within 14 lines ' Egotism in exceisis.
Reading that advt. one is reminded of Fred. B. Norton’s
yarn about Barry Sullivan and the young “ utility ” actor
who knelt before him during rehearsal at the Theatre
Royal, Belfast. The stage directions didn’t require the
young man to fall upon his ma - row-bones, so Barry Sulli-
van inquired majestically—“ What means this attitude?”
and the worshipper replied, “O, Mr. Sullivan, my father
had the honor of acting with you ten years ago, and he
told me that I was to kneel the first time I should find
myself in your presence.” The tragedian, slowly nod-
ding his august head, knitted his brows as he spake
again—“ But did not your father also teach you to kneel
to One who is—perhaps—greater than I ? ”
Wilson Barrett has not quite recovered from some
influenza that befell him ere he left England, but the
burly actor suffers nothing in stage effect from being a
little “ pulled down.” Miss Lillah M’Carthy, too, had a
faint the other day for the first time in her life. Probably
they were all suffering nervousness re the approaching pro-
duction of Barrett’s new play, “ The Christian King.”
A small French opera and a small French comedy were
dished up to a toney Sydney audience at the Criterion
last Monday. The first, “Le Passant,” was described on
the playbill as a “dialogue between two souls.” The
souls, depicted by Mme. Aengenheyster and Mme. S6gur,
were well draped in flesh, also in excellent millinery, Mme.
S6gur in addition sporting a costly satin train which
the minstrel, who came to sing to her, ungallantlv
kicked across the stage, in revenge for which she forth-
with sat down on his mandoline. “Le Chalet,” an opera
comique, in one act, gave an opportunity for Mr. Burns
Walker, in white kid tights, to show a young and
gentle maiden the errors of her ways in refusing the
protection of a husband. The young lady in ques-
tion (Miss Nana Macredie) sang in a pretty voice and an
inflexible countenance. Her lover, Daniel, Mme.
Aengenheyster, came in attired in a sort of bathing
costume (but more elaborate) and said in a shrill soprano
that he would seek a soldier’s grave if the lady w;ere not
kind. The whole two performances, except the
portion that consisted of the lady who couldn’t be heard,
were as good as a lesson in Ollendorf. At time of writing
two more opportunities are to be given the Sydney *>ublic
to take a lesson in parley-voo.

On an historical tomb, recently removed
from Devonshire-st. Cemetery, Sydney, the fol-
lowing inscription records a forgotten tragedy :
Within this tomb was interred, on xvii November,
mcleccxxxvi, the remains of seventeen human bodies.
Discovered, after the most diligent research, in the island
of Aureed, in Torres’ Straits, by Mr. C. M. Lewis, com-
mander of H.M. colonial schooner Isabella, and, by satis-
factory evidence, identified as the mortal remains of cer-
tain of the officers, crew and passengers of the bark
Charles Eaton, who, after escaping from the total wreck
of that vessel xv August, mdcccxxxiv, were savagely
massacred by the natives of the islands on which they
landed. Hi's Excellency Sir Richard Bourke, K.C.8.,
Governor-in-Chief of this colony, by whose command the
expedition to ascertain the fate of those unhappy persons
was undertaken, caused the last offices of piety to be dis-
charged toWSrds them by directing the interment of their
remains with the rights of Christian burial, and the erec-
tion of this monument to record the catastrophe by which
they perished.

RESCUED FROH THE
JfIWS OF DEATH.
A STATE OF NERVOUS AGITATION.
A LONG DYSPEPTIC TROUBLE .
A RATIONAL REMEDY BRINGS RAPID RELIEF.
The Case of Mrs. J. ANDERSON.
(By a Dunedin Reporter.)
M .ny of the Wonderful inventions per
fected during the present century owe
their originality of conception to men
who lived in past decades. The improve-
ments on old-fashioned ideas in respect
to machinery have effected great
changes throughout the world, but the
present and forthcoming generations
profit by the progress made. _ Axx in-
teresting study is found in noticing the
genual development of most scientific
matters, and the reader will find his at-
tention absorbed in tracing the follow-
ing compilation of events, emanating, as
they do, from what is frequently con-
sidered a trifling and unimportant
origin, and ending in a climax of con-
siderable magnitude. The narrative ap-
pears in the words of Mrs. Jeannie
Anderson, of 14 Smith-street, Dunedin.
“In my earlier days,” began Mrs.
Anderson, “ I was troubled very much
with indigestion. Like most persons
addicted to this complaint, I thought it
was only a trivial disorder of a tem-
porary nature, and that it would go
away on its own account. The symptoms
were certainly very annoying, but for
some time they were not sufficiently
serious to call for medical aid. That was
my opinion then, as I only had a feeling
of discomfort after food, but after a
while the pain gradually increased until
it became a severe wearing or burning
sensation. I then commenced to think
that I was in for a bad spell of sickness.
Severe pains in the back started to tor-
ment me shortly after taking my meals,
which were now indulged in in a very
delicate fashion. I was very careful
what I ate, and took but little, as I
had only a meagre appetite. If 1 took
a cup of warm milk I suffered as much
as though I had eaten a pound of steak,
the pain and uneasiness at the pit of
the stomach being just as great. My
face was deathly white, with dark lines
under my eyes, the sight of which was
greatly impaired. Sometimes the pul-
sation at the wrist was so feeble that
scarcely a throb could be felt. A sense
of great oppression was present in my
chest, a matter which affected my
breathing. I was always sleepy and
worn out. As soon as I got up in the
morning I felt inclined to go back to bed
again, and for all the good I was I
might just as well, for I was only in
other people’s way. Constipation be-
came a chronic complaint, and I became
so weak at last that I could not sweep
the floor.”
“It is a wonder you did not lie up,
Mrs. Anderson.”
“So I did, and shortly afterwards I
was seized with agonising pains in the
bowels, attended with great prostration
of strength. I could not raise myself
up in bed without becoming faint and
giddy. These symptoms were quickly
followed by the extension of pain of a
more violent character than ever over
the stomach, then came a miserable
filing of nausea. My skin became cold
and clammy, and my feature® were hor-
r|jjly shrunken. For days I did not eat,
neither could I get out of fed. On one
occasion I managed to pflt my feet on
the floor, intending to stand up and try
to walk, but I quickly fell and was
doubled up in agony for some time be-
fore anybody came to my assistance.
Whatever food I succeeded in swallow-
ing was promptly thrown up again. If
I took but a tablespoonful of milk, or a
small wafer biscuit, I vomited them at
once, and brought up, besides, large
quantities of what the doctor called
‘ digestive blood.’ There was not a par-
ticle of food which passed my lips but
what it came up again, and always ac-
companied with volumes of blood. This
incessant blood-vomiting induced a
burning, swollen feeling in the eyes,
combined with blinding headaches. If I
tried to stoop, my head became dizzy
everything in the room appearing to
swim round and round, and to make
matters worse, my nights were more
restless than ever. The trying ordeal I
went through every day made me feel
dead to the world, but in spite of my
terrible weakness and weariness I could
not rest either night or day. I can
honestly say I did not sleep for four
months, and it is a great wonder that
I retained possession of my senses, for I
was in a frightful state of mental
anxiety all the time. The noise made by
the children when playing about the
house was most exasperating. A shud-
dering thrill went through my enfeebled
system at the slightest sound, as well as
being sent into a state of nervous agita-
tion.
“ Under such tribulations you must
have become exceedingly melancholy ?”
“ I cannot say that I was at all de-
spondent, because I was past being wor-
ried by anything. I had not sufficient
strength to properly realise the awful-
ness of my condition. I was between
life and death, with the balance hanging
on a delicate thread which was likely to
snap at any moment. The hospital
seemed to be the only place where I
might get a little better, so I went. Up
to making this move the treatment I
had been receiving was not the right
kind. As soon as I got into the hospital
I was examined, and shortly after I was
informed that an operation was indis-
pensable. It did not matter to me what
they did, as I was heartily sick of my
life. Anyway, I was in the hospital for
three months, and in the meantime I
had undergone three operations. How
I survived them is a mystery to me, for
I had not the strength of a kitten.”
“ What did the doctors say about your
case ?”
“ The doctors said I had ulceration of
the stomach, and that I could not live
a fortnight. They injected morphia and
kept me plentifully supplied with medi-
cines, which did no good. The doctors
and nurses were exceedingly kind and
attentive, and I am sure they did every-
thing in their power for me, but their
efforts were inadequate. As they coulci
not cure me I went home nearly dead
I tried another doctor, under whose ad
vice and treatment I made no improve-
ment. I was daily getting less able to
stand the agonies which were torturing
me to death. The doctor gave me up
at last, saying he could not cure me. I
was suffering so acutely at the time
that I ordered him out of the house
because he admitted his inability to do
me good. To seek further for relief now
seemed but a waste of time and money.
I could only be relieved by the coming
of death, for which I waited patiently.
I was aroused from this despondent
frame of mind by hearing of a case like
mine being successfully treated with
Clements Tonic I started on the same
remedy that day. I could only take a
teaspoonful at first, a portion of which,
like everything else, I vomited. 1 took
another dose of Clements Tonic later
on, which remained in my stomach, and
I have never vomited since. After the
failures of endless medicines and several
doctors, after living on ice and milk for
four months, Clements Tonic rescued
me from the jaws of death. I was able
to increase my food gradually, and the
soothing influence of Clements Tonic
brought peaceful repose, besides restor-
ing strength and vitality. Thus I con-
tinued with that wonderful medicine till
I was well enough to- discontinue it. To
all sufferers I heartily recommend Cle-
ments Tonic, and I wish you to publish
these facts in any way you deem ad-
visable.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Jeannie Anderson, of 14 Smith Street, Dunedin,
in the Colony of New Zealand, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of three folios and
consecutively numbered from one to three, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements, which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the prory ons of an Act of
the General Assembly of New saland, intituled
“ The Justices of Peace Act, i88w”
Declared at Dunedin this thirty-first day of Decem.
ber, one thousand nine hundred, before me,
ISAAC SET.BY, J.P.

About 35 per cent. (1 in every -2'86) of the
people of S. Australia have deposits in the Savings
Bank. Spite of hard times, the number of de-
positors last year (to June 30, 1901) increased
6897, and the amount left in at the end went up
from £1,727,806 to £1,918,708. When all is said
and done S. Australia’s hard times are a good deal
like Israel’s invariably bad time over every race-
meeting.
Sydney Telegraph item :
Melbourne, Thursday.
One witness, in giving evidence before the Factories
Commission to-day, disclosed conditions of sweating not
generally owned up to in Victoria. He was speaking with
regard to wages in the clothing trade, and the effect the
Wages Boards had had on the industry. He knew of
cases where women were paid Is. for making a pair of
trousers.
One shilling ! And in N.S.W., where there is no
minimum-wage law, we have lately been hearing
of the same work being done for as little as four-
pence /
Is there in your city (writes the London
correspondent of Sydney D.T.) anyone weary of
loneliness and stranded on the shores of time,
who yet lives in the lap of luxury ? If so, let him
or her take heart of grace, and reply by cable to
this pathetic advertisement in the Times, and no
more exclaim with Moore—
When true hearts lie withered,
And fond ones are flown,
O 1 who would inhabit
This bleak world alone ?
Here is the appealing advt. :
A WOMAN of position, fortune, and well-connected,
but much alone, desires to meet another indivi-
dual who may have a fine house, with all its accom-
paniments, but may be weary of loneliness ; in fact,
one who by circumstances is stranded on the shores
of time, desirous of sympathy and friendship, to
whom advertiser could go, as it were, on a visit,
nothing binding on either side. She would read
aloud, accompany in drive, &c. No salary, merely
for companionship, London or its vicinity. Apply,

“Mamshaft” barracks for Chillagoe: Par.
in Bulletin, 24th Aug., says of Chillagoe—‘For a time,
only two furnaces will be put in blast. Why ? Is it be-
cause the others will not be ready in time ? ’ Of course it
is ; even two smelters will not be ready till the middle of
this month (Sept.), and, having just returned from the
field, I am in a position to state that there is no such
thing as an immediate scarcity of ore to be considered.
Stewart’s estimates of tonnage are not attacked his
values are. The railway is a fine piece of work —should
pay profits on public traffic alone, excluding the proprie-
tors’ traffic. Then Garnet Freehold is building a line,
Stannary Hills Co. is building a line, and the permanent
survey of the line to the Einasleigh has been started.
Chillagoe line, as a profit-maker, will be a bigger concern
(and will show greater profits per mile) than Silverton
Tramway Co. The 100 miles of line provide about 400
miles of good fuel along its route, and coke and mine-
supplies will have only a third of the haulage Broken
Hill had to pay. Present plant, when com-
pleted, will treat about 650 tons of ore daily
—say 190,000 tons a year, and on these figures there
is quite years’ work in sight: a statement confirmative
of Stewart’s figures, which will be found to have been
conservative from the beginning. The attemptsat mine-
development have been of a “book” description, dic-
tated by a hard-and-fast rule laid down when the condi-
tions of ore-occurrence were unknown. The original
owners’ style of mining was sneered at ; said sneering
being a rather foolish proceeding in view of the fact that
the original owners got on to ore, and stayed in it, and
smelted their winnings. The present management sank
big shafts off the lodes, and generally behind the lodes,
when for all purposes of prospecting the obvious course
was to get on to the ore already exposed, to deepen old
workings already in ore, and generally to prove a lode
before sinking permanent shafts in the country. Wher-
ever the original workings were developed as in the Mun-
gana Oo.’s mines, the results proved big bodies of ore ;
the only mines whose permanence is in doubt are those
which have the very good excuse that they have not been
sunk on—for there is not a case of cross-cuts having been
put in the right direction from these ill-advised “ perma-
nent ” shafts without striking ore.
“The Mungana Co., with the results of its
deepening of the old Girofla shaft and the developments
under the present management, is certain to be a big con-
tinuous div.-payer as soon as it gets well under way in
producing. The Red-cap group, where shallow workings
have proved one of the most continuous ore-channels in
this continent, have been almost utterly neglected. A
shaft was put down in ore on the Oruba of the Zillmanton
group, and a lode has been proved from the surface to
170 ft. worth 5 per cent, and 21ft. wide. Drives at the
100 ft. level give a length of 270 ft. with 5 per cent still in
the face; and at the 160 ft. the proved length so
far is 80ft. east and 100 ft. west. Easterly the face
is in a ferruginous clay carrying crystals of red
oxide and veinings of native copper—westerly the
ore is a black sulphide in kaolin. The Shannon lode
has been opened by shallow shafts and cuttings for 1050 ft.
On the Hensey a shoot of good grade ore 400 ft. long by
sft. wide by 80ft. high was abandoned and a big shaft
was sunk in the granite away from the ore. On the
Boomerang the late management sunk a shaft and drove
a tunnel behind the lode and in garnet rock This is the
more un-understandable when it is stated they, the
original owners, sunk a shaft which went out of the ore
body, proving its rapid southerly dip. The old pro-
prietary followed the ore southerly and caught it in a
winze,' still dipping rapidly south. The new shaft
on the Harper was sunk on the wrong side of
the outcrop and 900 ft. away from the old shaft,
where ore was still showing. This old shaft was un-
watered for the first time in three years, yet it has pro-
duced a large quantity of 10 per cent, ore, and a body of
6 per cent, ore is still standing in the lower workings,
estimated at 5000 tons. On the Hobson and Harkins
the three valueless shafts sunk in the country have not
altered the quantity of the ore available since the O.R.
and M. Go. took over the properties. On the P.P.A. East
a shaft was sunk in tne ore body to 50ft. Opening out was
started here, and a crescent-shaped drive went around
portion of the ore body a distance of 220 ft., so that at
the face the drive is now within 25ft. of the surface. On
the P.P. A. an enormous quantity of ore'is exposed by the
stripping of the overburden for railway banks. The face
is about 70ft. high and 150 ft. long. Rails have been laid
into the face, which will be worked by two benches. The
open cut, sunk further west, proves this lode to be 80ft.
wide at a depth of 30ft. from the top of the outcrop, and
from the bottom of the cut a shaft is down 40ft. in
5 per cent, ore, with good silver value. The shaft
sunk by the late management on the P.P. A. was started in
the lode. At 70ft. the lode went northerly out of the shaft
which was continued into the granite footwall. Although
the lode is patently underlying north, a crosscut was
started south, and continued for 166 ft. in granite. A
return was then made to the shaft, and a crosscut was
started northerly. It struck the lode in 23ft. The Otho
shaft was sunk almost in the creek bed. On the Atherton
the open cut—a fairly deep one —and the shafts, which
were both in ore, were abandoned, and a shaft was started
in granite—the mullock being tipped into the open cut
and the shaft which had been sunk in the ore. The new
shaft has, however, proved the lode at a shallow level —a
crosscut at 50ft. being now in low-grade ore. This list of
errors can be extended by the new workings of the
Ruddigore, where the work of the original owners is
shown by two open cuts which have produced great
quantities of ore ; 9000 tons, worth over 3£ per cent., was
stacked by the original owners. The late management’s
two shafts are too far east, and an unnecessary quantity of
dead work has been done. But there is plenty ore for
over two years, and the new system of development
should repeat the successes of the Mungana end of the
field. Most great mines get bad treatment at some time
or other of their lives; Chillagoe R. and M. Ltd. is good
enough to live not only by reason of good management,
but in defiance of bad. In justice to Stewart’s manage-
ment, norice must be taken of the contention that many
of the now seemingly valueless ‘permanent’ shafts will
prove valuable when more work is done in the way of
extending cross-cuts—which work, according to the late
management, was suspended by the directors owing to
the co.’s shortness of money.”
• • • •
Cardwell mine, 12 miles from Mt. Garnet, and
on the Freehold Oo.’s railway route, claims to have
100,000 tons copper-ore worth 3 per cent., and a little
silver in sight. The ore is 27 per cent, silica and carries
but a trace of zinc. Stated that Garnet Freehold has
arranged to buy the Cardwell ore.
* * * •
The new copper rush midway between Nyngan
and Oobar, and six miles south from Mt. Boppy Railway
Station and mine of that name, promises to pan out
great things, The Bulletin is told. Principal blocks, 40
acres each, held by Oobar syndicates, are opening up
well with 20 per cent, ore, but the pick of the lot is a 6ft.
lode held by Longworth and Saunders, which shows grey
ore, oxide and carbons running to 35 per cent, copper.
This is only sft. from surface, and making. Longworth
(Oobar’s boss copper man) is early in the field, and likes
his chance greatly.
* • • •
Mt. Boppy G.M. Co., 30 miles south from
Oobar, is a mine of much promise, and the chief owners
—so far unlucky Gallymont shareholders—have strong
reason to congratulate themselves on the prospect of a
recovery. Big reef running to an ounce ; present treat-
ment 40-head battery and cyanide, with slime-plant
shortly to be erected. Ore, although hard, is easily won,
and upon 1800 tons milled monthly, returns should show
a handsome profit on £95,000 capitalisation.
* * * *
With the recent scarcity of water at Broken
Hill, comes a revival of the local Co-operative Water Sup-
ply Co., the promoters of which were severely handled by
Public Works Committee last year. The co. asserted it
possessed certain water rights, and urged that its scheme
was the only practical and adequate one for the Hill.
Harry Levien, then a member of the committee, tackled
in police-court fashion the agents of the co. who gave
evidence, and fairly tied them up. D. 0. Maclachlan, of
Mines Department, gave evidence to the effect that the
co.’s title had expired and was not worth a dam ; and yet
the dry bones are rattling again.
* * * •
They wouldn’t be happy at White Cliffs
(N.S.W.) opal field till they got a commission to inquire
into their grievances, and they aren’t any happier now
they have got the Commission’s report. This report,
as printed here lately, recommended that the English
co.’s blocks—the principal on the field —be resumed by
Government, the latter paying £B4OO, equal to the market
value of the shares, with 20 per cent, added. The held
is asking why one set of blocks, and not the whole lot,
should be dealt with in this fashion ; and the field is put-
ting a sensible question. The only suggested explana-
tion of the present one-legged proposal is that it is desir-
able to deal with the big co. before bargaining with the
holders of the less valuable, but more numerous, other
leases and 4-acre mineral claims. To resume the whole lot,
co’s. and all, would probably cost from £12,000 to £14,000,
though one estimate of £20,000 is made. None of the
leases have more than about nine years to run.
* * * *
The coal-mine that will probably be acquired
and worked by the Maoriland Govt, is one at Point Eliza-
beth, some four miles from Gre.vmouth. There are
already millions of tons in sight. The mine, which has
hitherto been much mismanaged, is held under lease
from Govt. It is at present in the hands of certain
prior-lien debenture-holders, who have put in a receiver.
* * • *
Croydon notes to Aug. 26 : Important develop-
ments in deep ground likely to cause great revival in
mining here. Diamond - drill at Iguana Consols lease
pierced gold-bearing quartz in reef formation at depth of
1230 ft., proving continuance of Iguana reef beyond
fault, and the auriferousness of Croydon lodes in deep
ground. Rich leader discovered also in deepest part yet
worked of Golden Gate and Nancy Lee United. Crush-
ing from upper levels of this mine lately gave 2oz. per
ton average. G. Gate United crushing continuously
with 15-head stampers. G.G. 3 and 4 South, which in
three years produced two tons of gold, going to recom-
mence crushing, after long stoppage, owing to bad water.
G.G. 5 South, known here as Cuthbert’s, has also been
crushing continuously from big faces for regular (livs.
Walsh’s G.G. Block going to resume sinking, with favor-
able chances of striking reef. Morgan’s Block, heretofore
the most stupendously mismanaged mine in N.Q., going
in for a Worthington pump, to keep the shaft free of
water, so as to see what reef is like ; reef alleged to be
close at hand, but it will need to be nearly solid gold to
keep the pump going.
* • * *
“Fletch”: We are told that practically the
only water-communication between the Stewart River
(port for the Coen and Hamilton gold-fields, N.Q.) and
Oooktown is by schooners; and although the run up is a
matter of hours, during the S.E. season it takes the
schooners sometimes as much as three weeks to get back.
Indeed, at any time during the south-easter season, it is
odds that a man humping his swag will reach Cooktown
sooner than if he travelled by boat from the aforesaid
fields.
• * * *
“ Kersha” : At Ebagoola, Cape York Peninsula,
the alluvial is about rooted-out. Never was much good,
although a very few did well; and patches may yet be got
—probably by accident. The reefs, however, have given,
and are still giving, surface-crushings up to 10oz., and
there is plenty of easy -to- be - got surface-stone, which
would go about loz., but, as carting and crushing would
run to about that, it cannot be touched at present. Stores
give 555. for alluvial gold on the field, and the assay-value
of the reef gold is mostly somewhere between £2 and £3.
Fever and dysentery are very prevalent in the place.

Vic. Govt. Metallurgist Jenkins mentions in
his annual report that the increase of temperature with
depth in Bendigo mines has been hitherto greatly under-
stated. As the result of experiments made down to
about 3000 ft, he reports a rise of 1“ Fahr. for every
75ft.—40° for 3000 ft.
From a northern correspondent :
The Clarence bar is again becoming troublesome, and
threatens seriously to interfere with the trade of the
finest river on the N.S.W. coast. At the north side of
the entrance the occasional big floods sweep away the
sand and leave a clear entrance for a time ; but the sand
again gradually encroaches, leaving only a small channel
for navigation. The first attempt at improvement was
begun 25 years ago, and abandoned after an expenditure
of £200,000. Then Sir John Coode, an English engineer,
was consulted, and he submitted a plan providing for
north and south breakwaters, to be followed by inner
training walls ; but the Public Works Committee shelved
the essential outer breakwaters and recommended the
inner training-walls only. These are now approaching
completion, having cost between £lOO,OOO and £lno,ooo,
but during their progress the bar has become steadily
worse, and it looks as if all this money will have been
wasted. A couple of years ago a local resident submitted
plans for cutting a canal through the neck of the inner
South Head, thus giving a straight channel to deep sea
water with a natural headland on each side. The Public
Works Department treated this idea with scorn, but as
the completion of Coode’s entire scheme will probably
cost another £500,000 and take years to execute, the
local man’s plan should be worth inquiring into.
Mount Morgan up to date :
Mt. Morgan G.M. Co. still reigns supreme over the
township (says a Bulletin contributor). There are about
1500 men employed bv the co., and upon the proceeds of
the labor of these men the entire population (over 9000
persons) depends, directly or indirectly, for existence ,
for, beyond the employment given in and about the one
mine, owned by the one co., there is no work whatever,
excepting incidentally. Therefore, with very rare excep-
tions, any man who’s “ sacked ” by the co. must neces-
sarily leave the place for ever, sacrificing whatever in-
terests he may there possess. See, then, what a serious
thing it is for a married man with a family, who has his
all staked in the place, to fall under the displeasure of
the co. or its agents. This displeasure may be
incurred by an employee on its becoming known
that he is in the habit of carrying his “crib”
wrapped in a democratic newspaper, and of
reading the same during crib-time. Although the Mt.
Morgan Jingo prates a great deal about “ glorious
liberty,” he enjoys little or none himself, for he dwells
under autocratic government. During the first collection
of money for the first “Patriotic” fund in 1899 the bosses
went round amongst the men, with subscription lists in
their hands, and it was significantly bruited that all who
refused to give at least a full day’s pay would be instantly
“ sacked.” The majority acquiesced, but a few stubborn
souls refused, and the darkness of Hades thereupon
enveloped them. The same tyranny is in evidence at
every general election ; all recognised supporters of any
candidate other than the one whom the co. is supporting
(who has been one of the co. for the past nine years)
being instantly persuaded to seek employment elsewhere.
As a curiosity, I attach a poem written by a Mt. Morgan
Milton under the eye of the boss.—
TRAITORS 1

Loyalty despises!
Boycott all the brutes—
Boer sympathisers.

Traitorous advisers!
Only fit to shoot
Boer sympathisers.
White flag, red cross sneaks 1
Kopje lurking shicers !
Ourse ’em branch and root—
Boer sympathisers.
Here is another version, from “Canip ” :
Consider the complacency of Mount Morgan manage-
ment. A few months back, a labor-man with extreme
views wished to address the men, but, finding he would
have to wait until knock-off time, he appealed to the
management and obtained an hour’s leave for the hands
to come and listen to vituperation of capitalists ! By the
way, Mount Morgan, with an electoral roll containing
about 90 per cent, of manual laborers, has never returned
a labor member. The directorate has always treated men
as self-respecting humans—no slave-driving, no petty
oppressions.
