“So Little Else Hast Thou.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah, nowhere do these suns go westering
With the wild wander of those suns away :
Ah, nowhere do those stars sequestering
Behind far peaks in my far land make play
In this worn island with the steady eyes,
And learned old lips, grown up past all surprise.
Ah, nowhere do these fields go wandering
Into wild, vast, indefinite white nights :
1 roam these meadows, and my strange eyes,
pondering
On £ a y red poppies, see long southern lights
Break through vague worlds of dim, provoking
trees,
With haunting breaths, and unlived histories.
Ah, nowhere is there where the heart can fill
Grey hunger with great sweeps of mystery-land:
All, all, is branded, builded—dale and hill;
All, all, is written blackly by man’s hand.
Ah, poor worn island, with maternal brow,
Give peace, give peace—so little else hast thou !
ENG.
LOUISE MACK.
Impressions of Travel.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Travelling is delightful — only for the travellers.
You have to go away to find how many decent
people stay at home. My first discernible emotion
after I left Australia was surprise at the kind of
people who travelled. I could never have believed
it. All the bourgeoises, the blind-souled, silly-
lipped—hundreds and hundreds of them, ever
about you, day and night—it hurts to hear them
speak the names of Rome, Venice.
I have heard Santley voice his experience cor-
respondingly. The hundreds of appalling inani-
ties that you have to journey that long, long
journey with, steal its poetry as a malodorous wind
steals the scent of a rose.
* * * *
There is only one degree worse than the
Travelled. That is the Much-Travelled. This
person has been everywhere, and killed the charm
of every place he went to. No glamor of strange
lands clings to him. He went, that’s all. That
says everything. He went, and his money.
* * * *
I have never seen these things written before
about this particular voyage, but I guess that
other people have thought them, felt them, writhed
under them.
You see, the majority of passengers are not the
genuine Australian article. They are Scotch-
Australs, Cockney-Australs, provincial-English-
Australs. They have made money in Australia,
brewing, baking, mining, shopkeeping, public-
housekeeping, farming. And they are—well, I
should think they would be inestimable in their
own homes. But r at sea —at sea they’re all at sea.
Five hundred or so of these worthy souls break-
fast, lunch, dine, and sup with you 126 times
running. For over 30 days you see them inces-
santly, hear no else but them. They pay their
passage, and have their rights to look after, and
the justice in you makes you censure yourself for
resenting their presence in a sunset across the
desert as we come through the Canal —the dear
desert that bears the last likeness to Australia we
shall see for many a day
» * » *
And yet beer-makers and shirt-makers and
bootmakers and bread-bakers, will not fit in with
the poetry of an evening when the faraway Nile
gleams in a scarlet overflow to the left, and a dull
purple world stretches in dim mystery to the
right, and behind, up the Canal waters, comes a
slow procession of stately ships, following us up to
the Mediterranean beyond.
I never learnt so much about universal hate as
I did that night when I found never a corner I
could look from, alone, and had to mix in all their
company with all that loveliness, and feel the
loveliness hiding itself from me, disdainfully.
* * * *
As we came through the Canal Tennyson’s line
grew suddenly alive —
The stately ship glides on—
Five great, solemn, dignified vessels crept after
us, one behind another, stealing through the green
line of water that breaks the desert in two.
* * * *
A long sea-voyage shows you what we owe to
our labels. Several hundred people are suddenly
thrown together. Most are unknown to most.
You look round and study them furtively. You
have nothing to help you but your intuitions.
These are invariably wrong. Mine were, at any
rate. At first everyone looks commonplace; all
the women look plain, all the men look stupid.
Here and there you find a “ nice ” face, but that
is the limit of your discovery. You won’t find
out what they are, what is in them, what they
think and like, till you get some light from their
land-life on them, till you learn where they came
from or what they were at before. No; labels make
us. Take off our labels, deprive us of every shred
of our natural environment, remove our friends
from us, and mix us all together, and we’re all
lost, indistinguishable. It’s like learning to read
all over again, learning to know people with abso-
lutely nothing to assist you in your search.
» * * *
Instance. I took a great dislike to one little
man. He was fair, and had pale eyes. He used
to wear a little cap, and had a little fair mous-
tache. I summed him up as a common, pushing
little clerk, and mentally described him as
officious and conceited. One day I heard he was
an artist. I didn’t say anything aloud, but to
myself said: “Artist! I know the sort of artist
he is.” One night we had a concert. He recited.
I remember sitting there, thinking “I know the
sort of thing this will be.” He began it in a very
sickly voice, and I said to myself, “ I told you so.”
Then the thing changed, and turned out a smart
satire on its previous sickliness, and I was a bit
surprised. And then we had a paper, and it came
out, and there was a cartoon, and a page of carica-
tures, and- -well, I don’t like to paint my disgust
—with myself. They were magnificent! And
I got to know him afterwards, and found he was
the brightest, wittiest, and most modest of youths,
and that he had a career behind him. And now
he is fast making a fine one before him, in London.
Some day he will be at the top of the tree, and I’ll
remember my “ intuitions ” at sea.
» • * *
Colombo ! Colombo »—that first sight of the
East, how it thrills ! It seems impossible.
Afterwards, the voyage over, the passengers
scattered, the beauty of it begins to wake within
one—the long dream days come back, the sunsets
at sea return. Colombo, the Desert, the Canal,
Naples, Sicily, Corsica, Spain, they all return
in new beauty, and the environment of cabin and
saloon fades away. And suddenly it dawns on
you that there were many there you like, and
suddenly you find yourself the niche for many
friends, and at last there comes a day when you
actually confess to yourself that you would even
like to take that voyage again, on the same boat,
with the same people.
* * * •
I have seen Naples, Rome, Nice, Genoa, Lon-
don, and what do you think ? I can never convince
myself it was the Rome, the Naples, the Genoa, the
London. There is always another Rome, another
Naples ; there is, there is, there must be. This
has not grown out of any feeling of disappoint-
ment. It means that for so long I knew a Rome
and Naples of my own that I still see them, still
know them, still expect to go there some day.
« * * •
Besides, I went to Rome in a coat and skirt, in
a hat, in a railway-train, and to my the Rome I
went in a toga, in a chariot with knives sticking
out at the sides. And I carry about one horrible
recollection. It shames me. I’ll out with it, and
have done with it. I can never think of Rome
without remembering the delicious melting bun
and inimitable coffee at the railway-station
at dawn one morning in spring. Rome, Coffee,
Railway-Station, Bun ! Isn’t it awful!
LOUISE MACK.
“ Fifty years ago,” writes Baron Brassey,
“ we had 200,000 British seamen in oqr mercantile
marine ; now we have scarcely half that number.”
Britannia used to rule the waves personally. Now
she hires Hans Jorgensen and Ram Chunder to
rule them for her.
Bishop White, of Carpentaria, at whose
head-quarters (Thursday Island) there are about
1200 Japanese, tells a Vic. interviewer despon-
dently : “I fear that the Christian nations, by
their recent actions in China, have destroyed
what hope the Japanese had of Christianity.”
Lord Playfair, an eminent British Free-
trader, only 14 years ago :
We are a great exporting nation. . . The United
States cannot be so, for her cost of production is raised so
high by Protection that her exports are of small account
in the markets of the world.
Only 14 years ago ! And already the U.S. has
got ahead of Britain in exports, and almost
wholly supplies its own market as well, while
Britain depends more on imports than does any
other country. The Freetrade prophecy and the
penny balloon are the two most burstable things
on this earth.
“ Alone ” : The Lancet, in a recent article
about smoking, asserts that the cigar is most
harmful; the pipe next; and the cigarette least
injurious. It is further stated that nicotine is
harmless to the smoker, and that pyridine and its
derivatives work the mischief. The toxicity of the
smoke, it is considered, depends on the com-
pleteness of combustion, “and combustion is more
complete in the cigarette than in the pipe or
cigar.” Looks to me as if the Lancet were
reasoning from the apparent and relative toxicity
of the three smokes, without giving sufficient con-
sideration to the three different kinds of weed
used. Seems probable, that if the pipe-bowl,
cigar and cigarette were all one thickness, and
filled with the same tobacco, combustion would be
most complete in the one that kept the weed
burning longest; then the pipe might come out
best, and the cigarette worst; for several reasons.
The prize quotation was certainly made by
Thomas Gibson Bowles, M.P., in the House of
Commons apropos of Lord Rosebery’s lately-
expressed intention of “ploughing his own fur-
row.” Rosebery’s remark, he declared, reminded
him of the words of Alexander Selkirk:—
I am out of humanity’s reach,
I must finish my journey alone ;
Never hearing the sweet music of speech,
I start at the sound of my own.
And then turning to his own party—
The beasts (laughter) that roam over the plain
My form with indifference see ;
They are so unacquainted with men,
Their tameness is shocking to see.
Bowles is the dapper personage who edits, or used
to edit, Vanity Fair, and it was he who originally
said (pointing at Chamberlain): “In all history
no one named Joseph was ever an entirely satis-
factory person.”
IN MEMORIAM.
DENIS MURPHY.
The other day a boy wandering in the scrub
near Bondi, Sydney, found a skull and some other
fragments of human remains: Subsequently,
other fragments were discovered, said to be relics
of one Denis Murphy, a chemist, 50 years old,
from Ireland, who made a living by hawking
rabbits. Was last seen in the neighborhood about
four months before, and then appeared to be in
feeble health.
Not upon the Never Never,
In the desert, grim and bare,
Where the wings of Death for ever,
Make the only Shadow there.
Not amidst the lonely ranges,
Where the creeks are blind and dry ;
And the foot of white man strange is,
Murphy laid him down to die.
No ; where half-a-million people
Lived around, he made his bier ;
He could gaze on spire and steeple
Of a score of churches near.
Half-a-million people near him—
Christian people, on the whole—
And not even one to cheer him
With a send-off to his soul.
Better thus, perhaps—for Denis
Lay in nobler state and pride
Than the proudest Doge of Venice
By his Adriatic bride.
For the moon, in silver sandals,
Stood and mourned above his bed,
And he had the stars for candles,
At his feet and at his head.
Of the tribe of Might-have-Beeners
Denis was, beyond mistake,
But the wild winds were his “ keeners”
And he had a royal wake.
And, when Gabriel the Warder
Blows his Trumpet of Surprise,
In the lightest marching order,
Denis Murphy will arise.
He’ll be foremost at the lev4e,
Or his fortune will be hard,
For he’ll have no tombstone heavy
To take with him as a card.
